This report is based on the findings of an Appraisal Mission in June 1995. Members of the Appraisal team were
Brian Berman (Task Manager), Florian Grohs (Economist), Willem Zijp (Extension Specialist) and George Bestari
(Project Management Consultant). Pre-appraisal assistance was received from Bank staff: Louis Carbonnier
(Forestry Specialist), Peter Hopcraft (Agricultural Economist), Stephen Lintner (Environment Specialist), Walter
Ochs (Drainage Specialist), Hillar Lauri (Operations Officer), Jo Ann Paulson (Financial Sector Specialist), Omar
Razzaz (Land Reform Specialist), Kathleen McPhail and Miranda Munro (Participation and Social Assessment
Specialists), Karin Shepardson (YP Environment Specialist) and Ashok Subrammanian (Farmers Organization
Specialist). Detailed component design was carried out by Government teams in the Ministries of Agriculture and
Environment with assistance from the Bank's own resources, the Bank's cooperative program with FAO, EU
(Phare), bilateral assistance from Germany, and with consultants funded from the Trust Funds of Austria, Denmark,
Finland, the Netherlands, Norway, Sweden and the United Kingdom. Peer Reviewers were John Hansen, Severin
Kodderitzsch and Stephen Lintner. The Division Chief is Geoffrey Fox and the Country Director is Basil Kavalsky.

ESTONIA
AGRICULTURE PROJECT
I. BACKGROUND'
A. Country Context
1.1           The Republic of Estonia lies on the Eastern shores of the Baltic Sea and shares borders
with Latvia and Russia. Estonia has a territory of 45,000 square kilometers, almost the size of the
Netherlands. About one third of its 1.5 million population live in rural areas.
1.2           Since regaining independence in August 1991, Estonia has followed an open and liberal
reform path. It has successfully stabilized its currency, and has liberalized all agricultural commodity
prices, while creating competitive markets based on minimal barriers of entry, and free internal and
external trade. After declining in 1992 and 1993, real output in Estonia began to recover in 1994 and an
annual growth of about 5% is projected for 1995 and 1996. The unemployment rate (adjusted for recorded
jobseekers), has decreased from between 8% to 9% in 1993 to around 6% in the fourth quarter of 1995.
After a sharp decline in 1992, real wages have increased broadly in line with productivity. Annual
inflation has continued to decline reaching 48% in 1994 and falling further to 29% in 1995.
1.3           The recovery of output is underpinned by sound economic policies. The stability of the
currency is being supported by monetary and fiscal policies consistent with the operations of the country's
currency board arrangement. External trade and payments policies are liberal, with no tariff or non-tariff
barriers. Sectoral policies are aimed at creating and supporting a viable private sector and the service
sector in particular has developed rapidly. Privatization of former state-owned enterprises and non-land
properties has nearly been completed. Urban and rural land properties are being restituted to their former
owners, however, this process has been slower than expected.
1.4           Estonian agriculture contributes to economic growth and provides low-priced agricultural
products for the mainly urban population. Furthermore, exports of agricultural, forestry and food products
contribute significantly to total export earnings, while budget allocations to agriculture amount to less than
5% of the state budget, which is quite small in international comparison. Estonian agricultural policy-
makers are committed to continuing their liberal agricultural policy and pursuing the goal of closer
cooperation and eventual membership in the European Union (EU).
1.5           Agricultural Policy. In the context of the country's liberal economic and trade policy,
agricultural strategy has aimed at creating an open competitive agricultural sector based on private
ownership of land, a private agro-processing sector, liberal agricultural trade, fully market-determined
agricultural prices, and minimal budget support for the agricultural and rural sector. Although proceeding
slowly and with difficulty, Estonia has continued to restitute rural land. The privatization of agro-
processing enterprises is nearly complete, and these companies are in the process of adapting to the market
economy. Since 1992, agricultural prices are completely market-determined. There are no import or
export tariffs regulating agricultural trade, and Estonia has entered into trade agreements with the EU and
the EFTA countries, and is moving toward accession in the WTO.
I      Summarized from Estonia: Agriculture and Forestry Sector Review ( Report # 13316-EE, May 16, 1995).



2                                                                                      Chapter 1
1.6           Estonian agricultural policy to date can be described as exceptional within the former
Soviet Union (FSU) because price and market distortions have been almost completely avoided. The
single most important mechanism of Government support to the rural regions has been the establishment
of a fund with budget resources to provide loans for investments in agriculture and rural enterprises.
Commercial banks have used these resources to lend to agriculture and rural entrepreneurs at a capped
(and therefore subsidized) interest rate. Existing interest rate subsidies to the rural economy via this credit
fund2 are fully budget financed. Due to consistent and increasing pressure from the farmers' lobby to
support agriculture, the Government of Estonia (GOE) intends to continue to subsidize agriculture only
through the provision of credit funds from the budget with interest rate subsidies. In September 1995,
the Law on Regulating the Agricultural Market was passed by Parliament to replace the previous
Agricultural Market Law. The new law allows for price support payments to farmers and the imposition
of import tariffs on agricultural goods but does not actually determine tariffs. However, even though the
previous Agricultural Market Law allowed for price support payments to farmers, the GOE has not
regulated agricultural prices or trade in the past, and is committed to continue this open market policy in
the future.
1.7           Estonia is embarking on the next stage of improving rural institutions after having nearly
completed the transition to a market economy with minimal market distortions. Unlike other countries
of the former Soviet Union, the GOE has a well developed agricultural sector policy, and is now in a
position to effectively use public investments to rehabilitate rural infrastructure (including the deteriorating
farm drainage systems), promote the transfer to private ownership, improve agricultural technology
dissemination to the evolving private farms, promote rural entrepreneurship, and strengthen the sector's
public institutions.
B. Agriculture
1.8           Throughout its history, agriculture was the most significant activity of the Estonian
economy. Main export products were processed agricultural goods such as eggs, butter, cheese and flax,
and raw materials such as timber. The Soviet annexation in 1940 had a profound effect on the country's
economy through the elimination of private property, the collectivization of agriculture, and the
incorporation of Estonia into the Union-wide command system. Estonia was assigned the role of a
specialized livestock producer, and supplied dairy products, pork, and beef to neighboring republics, which
in turn, provided most of the grain and other needed inputs. Animal products accounted for more than two
thirds of the total value of agricultural production, and crop production was oriented mainly towards
roughage. The large agricultural trade surplus was based on heavily subsidized supplies of inputs and feed
from the other republics of the FSU.
1.9            Post-independence agriculture in Estonia is dominated by dairy and livestock production.
Even though livestock production has decreased considerably during the transition period, this sector
presents the highest competitive potential for Estonian agriculture. Crop production focuses on feed grains
and fodder crops for the livestock sector. In 1994 the sector accounted for about 9% of GDP, contributed
15% of exports, and employed about 6% of the Estonian labor force. It is estimated that agricultural
production decreased by about 40% from 1991 to 1993, resulting in a similar contraction of the
agricultural labor force. The food processing industry is characterized by over-capacities but, after
restructuring, is expected to contribute significantly to GDP.
2      The mission estimates the implicit annual rural credit subsidy to amount to US $1.0 million to date.



Estonia Agriculture Project                                                                       3
1.10           Exports. Although agricultural production has declined sharply, the export of agricultural
and food products has not declined to the same extent. Even though Estonia is a net exporter of meat and
dairy products, the trade surplus for these products has been declining, and future agricultural export
opportunities are not open-ended. Russia, the traditional customer, has imposed import tariffs on Estonian
agricultural products, yet agricultural exports to Russia continue to be of major importance, accounting
for 54% of total agricultural exports at the beginning of 1995. Other potential customers, such as the EU,
limit imports on the one hand through tariff barriers, and on the other hand, by their demand for high
quality and health standards for imported agricultural products.
.11          Food Demand. Consumption of meat and dairy products in Estonia has dropped since
independence from previously high levels. The cause is the decrease in real incomes of Estonian
consumers who have substituted relatively cheaper cereals and potatoes for the more expensive livestock
products. The combination of declines in local consumption of livestock products and increases in low
cost or better quality imports of agricultural and food products from neighboring countries, continues to
contribute to the contraction of the agricultural sector.
1.12           Farm Structure3. Before independence agricultural production was organized in 350 large
cooperatives, each managing some 3,500 ha with roughly 300 employees each. Nearly all of the assets
of state and collective farms have been privatized since independence. The farming structure has been
developing towards smaller sized family farms with an average size of 24 ha. Presently, the farm structure
is characterized by three different types of farms:
(a)    about 3,500 "legal enterprises" (joint stock companies, partnerships, and co-operatives)
which emerged from the former sovkhozes and kolkhozes (collective farms) now on about
70% of total agricultural land. The joint stock companies do not own land, the land either
belongs to the members, or is rented from the state. Often these enterprises have only a
transitional character and are dividing into smaller entities as the privatization process
continues and shareholders prefer to take their land and set up their own, private, farms.
Further contraction of these large-scale enterprises is expected over the next 18 to 24
months.
(b)    about 10.000 - 15,000 family farms with an average area of about 24 ha (17% of the total
agricultural lands). It is expected that the number of family farms will increase in the
near future (of these, only about 25% have registered their land titles).
(c)    more than 130,000 small, and non-registered, subsidiary plots, with an average area of 1.7
ha, often used by the shareholders of the large farming structures, to supplement their
wage income.
1.13           It can be expected that joint-stock farms will continue to down-size because members
wishing to start their own farms will withdraw their restituted land from the joint stock companies. The
number of private family farms will increase, and a larger share of rural income will be generated through
off-farm and non-farm activities as growth and employment opportunities in other sectors of the economy
increase. Some private farms will increase in size, whereas for others, land resource endowments will be
3      Information provided by the MOA. Due to the rapid transition of the farming sector, estimates of farm numbers and
land shares are changing rapidly, and do not necessarily correspond to other Government statistics.



4                                                                                Chapter I
inadequate for full-time farming and will need to be supplemented by off-farm income. Farm model
calculations (Annex 3) show that Estonian farms on good soils could compete internationally, especially
in dairy and beef production, if production efficiency was marginally increased (new techniques and
rehabilitated drainage), access to adequate credit was improved, input supplies and qualified extension
advice were secured, and quality and marketing of processed agricultural products was improved.
1.14         Livestock and Crop Production. Dairy production has traditionally been, and remains, the
major activity of the agricultural sector. However, it has also declined considerably since independence.
Beef meat production has declined even faster than dairy production due to its low profitability. Because
of climatic conditions, cereal production has never been of great importance and has continued to
decrease overall in the recent years. While the area planted with wheat and rye has increased slightly,
barley production, which is mainly used as animal feed, has decreased considerably.
1.15         As FSU markets have become less accessible and the supply of subsidized grain for animal
feeding has been withdrawn, herd owners have started to reduce production costs by placing more reliance
on pastures as a feed source. However, pastures have not been improved and managed in order to achieve
high yields and quality, and the milk yield per cow has dropped from an average of around 4,000 kg/year
to around 3,600 kg/year since independence. Only 23% of pastures contain legumes, and part of the
grasslands used for livestock feeding are in effect abandoned croplands containing a mix of volunteer
grasses and weeds. They will eventually reach the stage where they will no longer support livestock
production. Nevertheless low-cost technologies to increase milk and beef production and rehabilitate
pastures are available in Estonia and these are now being adopted successfully by a small number of
farmers.
1.16         Drainage. Historically, drainage has been a very significant element in maintaining the
productivity of both arable lands and those under forest cover. Drainage systems regulate the depth
(below surface) of the groundwater table and moisture of the soils during critical periods, thereby
lengthening the growing season and increasing crop and pasture yields. On areas where natural drainage
is inadequate, artificial drainage systems can extend the growing season for about a month by ensuring
that the groundwater table can be maintained at least 40 cm below surface. Well drained lands can be
planted earlier and harvested later, as waterlogging can be avoided and fields would be dry enough for
farm machinery to operate. Rehabilitation of drainage systems is an important prerequisite to increasing
crop and pasture production.
1.17         Of the 1.12 million ha of total arable land, some 739,000 ha or 66%  are drained
artificially. Routine maintenance of the drainage systems since independence has dropped dramatically
in most systems, due to constraints on the GOE's budget, to the point where permanent damage to the
system (e.g. plugging of tile drains) will be unavoidable in the near future unless proper operation and
maintenance (O&M) measures are revived. Much of the drain systems, particularly the sub-surface
systems covering 643,000 ha of the arable area, are now not functioning at full capacity. Additionally,
the drainage undertaken in the Soviet period was done without proper environmental assessment. Any
future investments in drainage rehabilitation will therefore need to undergo careful environmental
assessment.
1.18         Drainage is primarily the responsibility of the Land Amelioration Unit (LAU) of the
Ministry of Agriculture (MOA). LAU's twelve county-level Amelioration Bureaux (ABs) are presently
responsible for O&M on all the drainage and polder schemes in their localities. A Land Amelioration Law
which regulates the organization of Land and Water Associations (LWAs) was adopted by the Estonian



Estonia Agriculture Project                                                                  5
Parliament in April 1994.4 This law focuses on the privatization of drainage systems to individual fanmers
and on the establishment of LWAs. Membership in LWAs is compulsory for all farmers benefitting from
drainage systems. Following a transitional period, the LWAs are expected to take over the operation and
maintenance of their location-schemes from the county-ABs.
1.19          Repair and reconstruction of the drainage system was funded by the GOE with EEK 5.8
million in 1994, and in 1995 for EEK 10.3 million. A full national drainage rehabilitation program would
require massive budgetary resources, but the operational feasibility and economic justification of such a
massive program is questionable. Over 50% of the total areas of drained farmland have been classified
as lands of poor fertility. These areas, whether drainage is well maintained or not, do not have the crop
yield potential that would meet the thresholds for profitable farming under present cropping patterns and
open market economy input/output prices. Therefore, the Project would selectively finance drainage
rehabilitation only on the most fertile soils and would not finance any new drainage of agricultural lands
or forests.
1.20          Asriculture Extension Services. About 700 full and part-time agricultural advisors are
employed in the private sector (e.g. veterinarians, advisors from the processing industries, private
consultants, input suppliers); the public sector (e.g. staff from universities or colleges, research institutes,
county-based staff of the MOA); the media (mostly specialized press); and by the farmers themselves (e.g.
Farmers' Union with about 150 advisors, the Producers' Union, and farmers' circles). The present level
of public funding for extension is low, representing about 0.05% of the agricultural GDP whereas many
countries invest about I to 2% of agricultural GDP in extension. The MOA has decided to provide a large
part of the advisory services to the potentially more profitable farmers on a "for pay" basis, through a
private advisory system, while maintaining a small public advisory service to be available to farmers who
do not have the means to pay for advice. Rural information centers, which have been created in many
rural areas, assist with advice on off-farm job opportunities and rural entrepreneurship.
1.21          The extension service in the former Soviet Union was highly specialized and aimed at
servicing state and collective farms and ensuring the adherence to delivery of centrally planned production
targets. The advisors did not provide any business or market-related advice. The extension service now
requires retraining and re-orientation to provide advice relevant to an open market economy. The
emerging farmers lack business experience, and need advice on production techniques, agricultural
economics and farm management, while the managers of the profitable larger farms need advice on staff
management, marketing, and financial management. Several donor-financed technical assistanceprograms
have already been established in Estonia and are training agricultural advisors. In addition, EU (Phare),
as complementary support to this Project, has recently started a technical assistance program to train
agricultural advisors and to develop private agricultural advisory services.
1.22          Research. Research was highly centralized in the pre-independence period, but is now
being conducted at three levels. Universities undertake academic research, while applied research is the
responsibility of the Academy of Science and of the independent State research institutions and
laboratories. Research at all levels is severely constrained by a shortage of funds, and requires
reorientation to market-responsive production systems based on economic priorities.
4       Other laws reguating the finctions of LWAs include: Land Amelioration Law (1994); Law on Associations (1992);
Business Law (1995); and the Wiaer Law (1994).

C. Agro-Enterpnses
1.23.        Aero-Processing. Most of the Estonian agro-processing (milk, meat, and grain enterprises)
have been privatized and sold, mainly to cooperatives, some of which have foreign investors. Small scale
activities have been successfully privatized, and several new enterprises have been established. Given the
high level of (nominal) overcapacity, technical deficiencies, and financial problems facing the large-scale
agro-processing enterprises, and the legacy of the export orientation to the FSU, significant
decommissioning of installed but out-of-date capacity, and upgrading of the remaining capacity, is required
to adequately transform these industries into efficient producers of the range and quality of products
acceptable in Estonia's future markets.
1.24         Food Ouality Control.  Present laboratories and their personnel are not equipped and
trained to meet international requirements in food quality control. The GOE plans to adjust quality control
measures and standards to meet EU food import standards, to enhance producers' export potential to the
EU and to other countries, and also to protect the health of domestic consumers. The trade agreement
between Estonia and the EU stipulates that Estonian agricultural exports must meet EU standards and that
the products be free of various contaminants and potentially harmful residues. Therefore, monitoring and
testing capacities need to be established to evaluate and ensure a high quality of food and livestock
products to exploit Estonia's export opportunities.
1.25         SuPDlY services have been improving since 1993 and agricultural input and machinery can
be purchased through dealers from the world market. Private farmers often depend on machinery services
from cooperatives, but their old Soviet machinery is outdated and will have to be replaced soon. New
arrangements, such as farmers' machine cooperatives, could provide the necessary services to small
farmers.
D. Land Reform
1.26         Land Reform. The total area of agricultural land and forest to be privatized is estimated
at 2.3 million hectares (including natural grass land, arable land, orchards, and forests) out of the total
Estonian land area of 4.5 million hectares. The reform of land ownership, (i.e., restoration of personal
property rights and the sale or lease of state lands) is a central element of Estonia's economic reform.
This also implies a reorganization of the collective farms. The Government recognizes that rural land
reform is the key element for rural economic transformation. The actual process of formally transferring
ownership to the newly established private farms has been slower than desirable. The approach chosen
for land reform - restitution based on pre-1940 boundaries - seems to be the most significant factor in
slowing down land privatization, and creating uncertainties as to the right of the cultivators. Many land
owners not involved in agriculture have chosen alternative lands or non-land compensation, in place of
accepting the restitution of their actual "ancestral lands." Consequently, a significant proportion of the
arable and forest land remains under State ownership at the end of the first stage of land restitution.
1.27         Despite the importance attached by the GOE to land reform and privatization, only 13%
of eligible rural lands (300,000 ha) have been devolved to private control (including restituted land, and
land transferred under the Farm Law Act of 1989). As of December 1995, about 220,000 ha (privately
and state-owned land) have been registered in the cadastre, and about 180,000 ha have been registered in
the Title Book. The slow progress of the land reform can be attributed to the difficulties in adjusting the
legal framework to handle high transaction volumes under restitution, insufficient institutional capacity
and lack of coordination between local cadastre centers (under the Land Board), title book offices (under



Estonia Agriculture Project                                                                7
the Ministry of Justice), municipalities, notaries, and the GOE's limited financial resources to complete
the land surveys and registration.
1.28         After completing land restitution, market forces will govern the disposal, utilization, and
price of land. The land reform process has, however, created complex questions about the disposal of
state owned arable and productive land, and the establishment and facilitation of private land markets.
An important task for the GOE is to work out a policy for the transfer of ownership of state-owned lands
in the rural areas into private hands. Maintenance and management of parks and protected areas
established after 1940, which include former private agricultural lands, create further problems.
1.29         The GOE has initiated several actions to accelerate land reform and ownership transfers,
including: the devolution of land reform authority to municipalities; plans to simplify land registration
procedures; replacement of the Land Board management; the limiting of the Land Board's functions to
quality control over the municipalities and private surveyors' cadastre activities; and strengthening the
capacity of the land and title book registration offices to handle the large number of land and title
registrations. In 1995, the GOE has transferred state cadastre functions from the state agency Est-Survey
to the Land Board and plans to completely privatize Est-Survey's commercial land survey activities by
mid-1996. County Land Cadastre Offices have been established in nearly all counties. The GOE has also
recently established a Land Reform Commission consisting of representatives of the Ministries of Justice,
Agriculture, and Environment, the Land Board, and members of Parliament, to coordinate land policy,
develop suggestions to speed up the land registration process, and to simplify procedures and regulations.
Legislation approved by the Estonian Parliament in 1995 assigns up to 100% of the land tax to local
governments and gives local governments greater authority over land titling and registration.
Municipalities are now authorized to sell state owned land (without pending restitution claims) and can
retain some of the sales proceeds to accelerate their work. The GOE has allocated about EEK 73 million
(US $6.6 million) in the budget for 1996 to carry out land reform.
E. Rural Finance
1.30         The Estonian commercial banking system is inadequate,5 in relation to the size of the
economy, despite the relatively large number of independent banks. The Estonian banking system has been
privatized6 and, in the process, has gone through major restructuring and consolidation. Yet, there are still
too many banks for the overall economy, and further bank consolidation and restructuring is expected as
the Bank of Estonia continues to tighten its requirements.
1.31         The GOE has established a large number of funds to channel budget resources through
commercial banks to various sectors, including agriculture, rural enterprises, and housing. In 1993, the
GOE established three funds -- the Agriculture and Rural Life Credit Fund (ARLCF), the Small and
Medium Enterprise Fund, and the Export Credit Fund that finance credit to agriculture. The finances of
these funds are derived solely from the GOE's budget and loans from these funds are exclusively
channeled through commercial banks. Up to December 1994, about EEK 200 million had been on-lent
from ARLCF to commercial banks and then to farners and rural enterprises. The Government allocated
5      A more complete discussion can be found in the Financial Institutions Development Project (Report #6376-EE).
6      Even though the State remains a significant shareholder in a number of banks.



8                                                                                 apter I
an additional EEK 91 million to ARLCF in 1995. Seventy-eight percent of the loans have been made for
agricultural activities, the majority directly for agricultural production. Up to 1995, the implicit annual
rural credit subsidy has been estimated by the appraisal mission to be about US $1.0 million.
1.32         The GOE plans to consolidate the large number of funds into one Regional Development
Fund and to allocate an additional EEK 100 million to this Fund in 1996. Although the GOE has not
fully developed the mechanism, it is anticipated that the funds would be on-lent to commercial banks and
then to farmers (about EEK 60 million). In addition, the Fund would finance interest rate subsidies (about
EEK 40 million) to cover the difference between commercial bank's market interest rates charged for
loans to agricultural producers and their interest rates charged to long-term borrowers in other sectors.
This would increase the rural credit subsidy planned for 1996 to US $4 million. The Government is fully
apprised of the impact of this subsidy, both on the budget and the banking system. However, it believes
such costs are acceptable relative to the negative economic impacts of the full protectionist support the
farming community would like to receive.
F. Environment
1.33         There is both a strong political commitment to, and a strong public awareness of,
environmental issues, including support for environment non-governmental organizations (NGOs) activity.
Estonia has inherited a number of serious environmental problems from the FSU, most of which relate
to pollution from heavy industry, ex-military sites, and urban waste disposal. Estonia has demonstrated
its commitment to the environment through its participation in multilateral efforts on the Baltic Sea, its
own and Bank-sponsored environment-related investments to support this amelioration effort,7 and ongoing
discussions with the Bank for additional environment-related investments. A draft overview of
environmental issues in Estonia is being prepared by the Bank. In addition, the Bank is assisting in the
preparation of a "Biodiversity Strategy" financed through the Global Environmental Facility (GEF). Under
the District Heating Rehabilitation Project, the GOE took a significant step in reducing air pollution,
through its examination and adoption of alternative fuels and clean combustion methods.
1.34         Environmental legislation in Estonia exists and is being revised and augmented. The
current transitional status is characterized by a combination of the administrative procedures, legislation,
and standards used when Estonia was part of the Soviet Union, and some new provisions stipulated by
the GOE. Estonia's Basic Law on Nature and Environment Protection was passed in 1990. This has been
augmented by the recent passage of important laws and regulations regarding hazardous waste, solid waste,
environmental impact assessment (EIA), and natural resource pollution taxes.  The Ministry of
Environment (MOE) is responsible for review of EIAs when proposed projects are of national significance,
while County Environment Departments (CEDs) are responsible for the EIA of projects of local
significance. The MOE, with outside assistance, is working to improve the quality of ETA oversight.
Training is also being provided to specialists from line ministries, commercial banks and consulting firms,
in the preparation and use of ELAs in project design and implementation.
1.35         Pre-independence agriculture, primarily because of the concentration of large livestock
numbers in large farm complexes, caused localized (point-source), and in some cases significant, pollution
of soil and water resources. Drainage constructed during this period for expansion of agriculture and
7     For example, the Haapsalu and Matsalu Bays Environment Project (Report #13907-EE, April 25, 1995).



Estonia Agriculture Project                                                                9
development of peat resources had a broad range of environmentally adverse impacts. Past over-use of
fertilizers and pesticides has also been reported. These problems can be avoided through the use of proper
environmental review procedures and adoption of mitigation actions. The decline in agricultural
production since independence, combined with the steep relative price increases of chemical inputs, has
reduced the previously high pollution loads. The decline in livestock populations, and their dispersal into
small units, has decreased the point-source pollution, but could result in more dispersed pollution if
livestock operations are not managed with adequate environmental safeguards (e.g. safe storage and limited
application of manure, etc.). Agricultural run-off, particularly to protected coastal areas and nature
reserves could cause environmental damages if mitigation measures are not enforced.
G. Bank Strategy and Experience in Estonia
1.36         Countrv Assistance Strategy. The key objective of the Bank's assistance strategy for
Estonia is to support development of a dynamic private sector, improve the existing public infrastructure,
and alleviate the pockets of poverty that have developed during the transition process, in order to raise
overall living standards. To achieve the objectives, the Bank's program is focused on two main areas:
(a) improving the environment for private sector investment and growth; (b) improving public
infrastructure services.
1.37         For agriculture, the Government has welcomed the Bank's support during the sector's
difficult transition to a market economy, which requires an agricultural sector based on private ownership
of rural land, enterprises, and farms, and competitive open markets, privatization of farm drainage
infrastructure, and effective public sector agencies to support private sector development in the rural areas.
The agricultural sector is of special importance to the Bank's strategy of supporting privatization in the
economy and specifically in the emerging rural private sector. This Project would support accelerating the
land reform, improving food quality control measures and standards to meet EU requirements, improving
rural human resource skills through public and private advisory services, upgrading and increasing
efficiency of farm drainage infrastructure and its privatization and transfer to the systems' beneficiary
groups.
1.38         Lessons Learned from Previous Bank Experience. Previous Bank involvement in project
implementation has included a 1992 Rehabilitation Loan (US $30.0 million, completely disbursed and now
closed), a Highway Maintenance Loan for US $12 million in 1994, a District Heating Rehabilitation Loan
for US $38.4 million in 1994, a Health Project in 1995 for US $18 million, a Financial Institutions
Development Project in 1995 for US $10 million (all effective and under implementation), and the
recently approved Haapsalu and Matsalu Bays Environment Project (April 25, 1995). Experience in
implementing these projects has shown that it is important in the initial stages of project implementation
to fully train the Project Implementation Unit (PIU) staff in project management, financial management,
and competitive procurement procedures. In general, implementation of projects in Estonia has been
satisfactory. Consequently, a joint PIU would be responsible to assist the implementation of the Bank's,
EU (Phare)'s and other donor programs in agriculture and the staff would be trained in management,
procurement, and monitoring & evaluation.



10                                                                               Chapter I
H. Rationale for Bank Involvement
1.39           The Bank has assisted the GOE in the development of its sector strategy through the
preparation of this Project and the preparation and discussions of the Agriculture and Forestry Sector
Review. This review highlights key strategy elements for the development of the sector: efficiency
improvements in primary agricultural production; development of agricultural advisory services;
agricultural marketing improvements; and rapid completion of land reform. The Sector Review notes that
low wages, low land costs, and the existing stock of skills, equipment, and productive infrastructure
provide Estonia with a competitive potential in dairy and beef production. This competitiveness has
already been demonstrated on a limited scale by some transformed farms and food processing industries.
The Sector Review discussions with the GOE stressed the need for an open agricultural economy, land
privatization, and the reorientation and strengthening of public and private agricultural services. In
general, the GOE's agricultural policy reflects these objectives, although progress has been difficult in
some areas during the transition period.
1.40         Rural and Agricultural Strategy. The GOE actively supports the development of the rural
areas in order to narrow the income gap between urban and rural populations. The main objective of the
GOE is to transform and improve living conditions in the rural sector, primarily through accelerating rural
land reform and supporting agricultural and rural entrepreneurship activities. A key issue of this strategy
is the association and anticipated future membership of Estonia in the European Union. Developing an
agricultural policy aligned with the EU would therefore be central to the activities of the MOA in the
future, and the GOE is in the process of developing and refining its agricultural policies to reflect this.
1.41         The MOA has proposed to the Cabinet a set of polices and strategies. These strategies
includes the following key elements:
*   acceleration of the land reform by simplifying procedures for land registration, and by initiating
the sale or lease of land that has not been claimed by former owners and is under state control;
*   improved marketing of agricultural products through improved market transparency and by
adjusting quality control measures and standards to meet EU requirements;
*   improved rural financial services to provide farmers and rural entrepreneurs with long-term credit
funds coupled with advisory services for borrowers and banks;
*   revision of the tax policy to create equal tax conditions for agriculture;
*   development of an efficient agricultural advisory service to promote structural and technological
changes in agricultural production and land use; and
*   development and rehabilitation of rural infrastructure in areas with a low population density.
1.42         Bank Support. The Bank supports the MOA's strategy to ease key bottlenecks in the
agricultural sector, primarily by accelerating the land reform, improving the maintenance of rural
infrastructure (drainage), developing the agricultural advisory services, and improving the food quality
control. The Project would therefore strengthen the private sector, improve the MOA's policy and
regulatory functions, and support a gradual transfer of non-essential Government functions to the private



Estonia Agriculture Project                                                               11
sector. The Project investments support key Government regulatory functions (food quality control,
environmental regulation in agriculture) and the gradual transfer of some government functions to the
private sector (land reform, drainage maintenance, advisory services). The Project assists the MOA in
rehabilitating and privatizing farm drainage infrastructure by handing over the responsibility for future
operation and maintenance to the beneficiaries. Furthermore, the Project supports the development of a
private farner-financed advisory service to complement a basic Government funded, modestly-sized,
agricultural extension service. These are investments of either a public good dimension, or necessary to
support the emerging private sector and reduce recurrent Govermment expenditures.
1.43         The Bank had intended to support the rural sector with investments in rural credit and
forestry. However the GOE, after consultations with the Bank, and for reasons discussed below, has
decided not to use loan funds from this Project for these priority areas.
1.44         The GOE plans to streamline support to agriculture and the rural areas through a Regional
Development Fund providing loan funds (for on-lending through commercial banks) and interest rate
subsidies for regional development, including agriculture. The GOE's policy is to continue to provide this
limited budget support to develop a viable private farming sector and to re-capitalize the emerging farms.
The Regional Development Fund (with interest rate subsidies) is seen by the GOE and the MOA as a
second best alternative, but it allows the Government to continue support for an open market policy in
agriculture. The Ministry of Finance judges that the distortion of the financial sector is small in
comparison to distortions that would be created by agricultural price support payments or an introduction
of significant agricultural import tariffs. Considering the high liquidity in the financial system and the
Government policy to subsidize interest rates, Bank lending for rural finance would not be appropriate at
this time.
1.45         For forestry sector investments, the GOE and the Bank have agreed during Project
preparation that the finalization of a Strategic Plan for Forestry (and related studies) should be a
prerequisite for determining future public expenditures in forestry. Assistance to the GOE for the
preparation of this plan, and the studies, is now being donor financed (see Annex 1).



II. THE PROJECT
A. Project Origin and Formulation
2.1            Estonia has followed a liberal reform  path: these reforms have successfully initiated
economic transformation. Whereas the liberal reform policy has been quite successful in the urban areas,
with wage incomes beginning to rise there, the reforms in rural areas have not shown similar levels of
success. This is mainly due to the slow progress of the land reform and the reorientation of agricultural
exports to western markets. The new Government is strongly committed to supporting development of
the rural areas and to narrowing the income gap between urban and rural populations.
2.2           The intent of the GOE and the Bank was to prepare a program of loan and grant supported
interventions (investments and technical assistance) to address agriculture and forestry productivity, rural
entrepreneurship and finance, rural infrastructure, rural land use management and related environment
issues, and land reform. During the course of project preparation by the GOE and the Bank, a number
of items were removed from the scope of the Project as they were either fully taken over by donor
supported technical assistance (forestry, rural entrepreneurship, rural finance -- Annex 1), or the GOE
decided not to borrow for investments (rural infrastructure other than drainage, and rural finance), but
instead to fund these from budget resources.
2.3           The Project supports the agricultural strategy through selected investments in farm
drainage rehabilitation, land use management, food quality control, and agricultural advisory services,
supported by the GOE's land reform budget. The components of this Project were designed by the MOA
and the MOE with bilateral', EU (Phare)2 and Bank3 assistance commencing October 1993. This report
reflects the outcome of the Bank's consultations with the GOE beginning with Project identification
(February 1994), followed by Project preparation missions (October 1994 and January 1995), appraisal
(June 1995) and included working groups to prepare the Project Implementation Plans (see Annexes -
Volume 2). Policy elements were extensively discussed with the GOE in a public review of the
Agriculture Sector Review in January 1995.
B. Project Objective
2.4           The Project objectives are to increase rural incomes and to stimulate the rural economy
through rural entrepreneurship. To achieve these objectives, the Project focuses on improving factor
productivity and competitiveness of agricultural products by providing assistance for the:
*    privatization of rural lands;
*    privatization and rehabilitation of select rural infrastructure;
*    improvement of human resources skills for entrepreneurship in rural areas;
Including Austria, Denmark, Finland, Germany, Netherlands, Norway, Sweden, and the UK under bilateral assistance
programs, and through Consultant Trust Funds.
2      Complementary donor-funded TA is concentrated in forestry, rural private enterprise development, and the
development of sector information systems (Annex 1).
3      Including the Bank-FAO cooperative program.



Estonia Agriculture Project                                                              13
*   introduction of new farming technologies consistent with transformed private agriculture; and
*   improvement in food quality.
2.5           The Project's objectives and strategies are consistent with the GOE's rural sector policies.
C. Detailed Project Description4
Land Reform (US $5.5 million)5
2.6           To accelerate the land reform, this component provides financing for the completion of
the National Geodetic Network; land survey works; and of a state rural land disposal study. Under
separate arrangements, donor-funded projects provide technical assistance to strengthen the institutional
and technical capacities of the Estonian National Land Board (ENLB), and land registration agencies.
2.7          National Geodetic Network. The Component would support the completion of a nation-
wide ground control network which coordinates all measured boundaries into one unified system. Over
a period of 3 years, 5,000 points would be measured by private surveying companies using GPS
technology.
2.8           Cadastral Surveying. This component would finance surveying and cadastre registration
of about 150,000 ha of rural lands over 5 years. This is equivalent to about 6,000 farmns of different sizes.
The surveys will be primarily carried out in the drainage rehabilitation areas financed under the Project
to complete the registration of agricultural land in the cadastre. The GOE would recover the costs of the
surveys from private land owners at a later stage. At negotiations, an agreement was reached that by June
30, 1996, the cadastre registration of Est-Survey (a state-owned surveying company) shall be transferred
to the National Land Board (para 5.1 (a)). As a condition of disbursement, the Borrower shall have:
published the tender for privatization of at least five of Est-Survey's regional land survey offices or
terminated Est-Survey's commercial land survey activities; and contracted all publicly-funded land and
property surveys to private surveyors under competitive bidding procedures (para 5.4 (a)).
2.9           State-Owned Rural Lands Disposal Policy. The Government would initiate studies to
analyze possibilities to lease and sell rural land under state ownership, to streamline land transfer
procedures, to clarify the rights and responsibilities of local governments, and to determine incentives for
title book registration. The study would also address the issue of future ownership of former private lands
which were designated and managed as "protected" areas during the Soviet period. By March 31, 1996,
the GOE would initiate studies, acceptable to the Bank; to formulate a national policy for the transfer of
State-owned rural andfarming lands to private ownership, and publish and present to the Bank a plan
for the development of such policy, including a timetable for its technical analysis and adoption, and, by
March 31, 1997, its implementation, taking into consideration the Bank's views (para 5.1 (b)).
Project Implementation Plans (PIPs) have been prepared for each major component, (see Annexes - Volume 2, PlPs).
Component costs, shown in parenthesis, are base costs.



14                                                                                         Chapter II
2.10           Institutional Strengthening. The following ongoing, or future, donor-funded technical
assistance and training6, complements the Project objectives and the land reform:
*    land registry management;
*    land assessment methodologies;
*    land and real estate legal aspects;
*    survey training; and
*    training of cadastre specialists.
Farm Drainage Rehabilitation (US $10.9 million)
2.11           The drainage rehabilitation component would consist of: (a) rehabilitation of drainage
systems; (b) development of Land and Water Associations (LWAs); and (c) development of an adaptive
drainage research program.
2.12           Rehabilitation of drainage systems. The Project plans to rehabilitate about 60,000 ha of
drained agricultural land in about 60 separate locations. This component would rehabilitate main channels
and collector drains, flush and repair subsurface pipe outlets entering collector drains, and selectively till
compacted soils as a complement to site-specific drainage improvement.
2.13           Drainage Site Selection Criteria. Areas for drainage rehabilitation works would be selected
according to site selection criteria7 that include: (a) environmental screening; (b) soil fertility; (c) land use
intensity; (d) condition of the drainage systems; (e) technical feasibility and designs; (f) investment cost
ceiling; (g) status of land tenure;8 (h) farmer's participation, including formation of LWAs; (i) commitment
of LWA members to contribute to rehabilitation costs and take over future operation and maintenance of
the system; and (j) location of markets. Most (95%) of the state-owned agricultural land would be sold
or leased (minimum 10 year lease contracts) prior to commencement of the drainage rehabilitation works9.
Special screening criteria would be used for any proposed activities in the catchment area of the Matsalu
Bay because of the ecological importance of the Matsalu State Nature Reserve. Project financed drainage
investments would be limited to rehabilitation works and investments would not be made for new
drainage schemes and would not include polders'0 or "wet forests." Assurances have been sought at
negotiations from the GOE that investments in rehabilitation would be made only on those sites that fully
meet the above mentioned selection criteria (para 5. 1 (c)). At negotiations, an agreement was reached that
before commencement of the drainage rehabilitation works at any specific drainage site: the Borrower
would (a) register in the cadastre all lands at that specific drainage site to benefit from drainage
6      Not included in Project costs, but provided for in the Project description as background.
7      Selection criteria are elaborated in the Project Implementation Plan of the Drainage Rehabilitation Component (see PIP
#1) and are included in a supplemental letter. Drainage sites covering about 40,000 ha are currently under review
Compare discussion in PIP, para 8g.
Details are specified in a supplemental letter.
10     Where submerged lands are now drained for agricultural purposes.



Estonia Agriculture Project                                                                           15
rehabilitation; and (b) make available all such state-owned agricultural lands for purchase or lease (para
5. 1 (d)).
2.14            Design and Implementation. Drainage rehabilitation works would be divided into a design
phase and a construction phase. The design phase would be completed in 4 years (3,500 ha in 1995 pilot
sites):" 16,500 ha in 1996; 25,000 ha in 1997; and the remaining 15,000 ha in 1998). The construction
phase would take an estimated two years for each site after completion of the design. The rehabilitation
work design, establishment of the LWA, and physical works would be completed in three years for each
site. Within this time framework, the aggregate plan for the construction phase would be spread over a
period of five years with construction completed by the end of 2000."2
2.15            Develonment of Land and Water Associations. 3 The formation of LWAs is a recent legal
requirement for all beneficiaries of Government drainage investments. LWAs are being formed in all
drainage basins where such investments are planned. The Project would provide training and advisory
assistance to the MOA's drainage staff and to the members of LWAs on the legal, organizational and
financial functions of LWAs. It is expected that about 60 LWAs would be established in the proposed
project area. The GOE has allocated budget funds in 1995 to: (a) train the staff of the ABs; and (b) to
recruit and train local consultants to serve as facilitators in the context of the LWA development and to
assist in drafting agreements between the Government and LWAs on the scope of rehabilitation works and
farmers' contributions.
2.16            The MOA's Land Amelioration Unit (LAU)14 plans to commission three studies, namely:
(a) a series of social assessments of target drainage areas; (b) clarification of the future role of the ABs;
and (c) a mid-term review of the LWA development program. The social assessment of target sites would
begin in early 1996 and continue over a period of two years. A pilot social assessment for the proposed
sites in Vigala and Rannu is currently being carried out and would include specific consultation with
representatives of the Matsalu State Nature Reserve, and representatives for Lake Peipsi. Training and
technical assistance for local staff in social assessments and participation would be funded through bilateral
and World Bank resources (Fund for Innovative Approaches in Human and Social Development-FIAHS)
in 1995 and in 1996.
2.17            Drainage Research. The existing drainage research program of the Agricultural University
at Tartu (Institute of Water Management) would be reoriented to address: (a) reduction in drainage
operations and maintenance costs; and (b) the envirommental factors in drainage. The GOE would provide
equipment and field instruments to the Institute of Water Management, and funding for the existing staff

in Tartu county were designed in 1995 using existing budget sources, followed by physical works in 1996 and 1997.

16                                                                                       Chapter 11
of the Institute to conduct this research. This Project includes related funding to expand the scope of the
studies and to provide training.'" A parallel technical assistance program is currently being sought by the
Institute for possible international collaboration and exchange in this field.
2.18           Cost Recovery. The GOE plans to privatize the ownership, operations and maintenance
of all farm drainage systems by handing over these systems to LWAs after completion of the drainage
rehabilitation works. Responsibilities of LWAs are specified in the Amelioration Law (May 1994) and
membership is compulsory for farmers in the drainage area. The GOE has determined that each LWA
must agree to contribute (in cash or in kind) 20% of the cost of the rehabilitation works (the remaining
80% would be covered by the GOE with budget and Project loan funds). In return, the GOE would, post-
rehabilitation, no longer accept any further financial responsibility for these on-farm drainage systems.
The cost sharing formula is a legal decision of the GOE and based on an estimated average payment
capacity by the beneficiaries. The GOE decided to cover the majority of the one time rehabilitation costs
because thereafter each LWA would take-over full ownership of its area-specific drainage system and bear
the full future O&M and rehabilitation costs.'6 However, the ABs would continue to be responsible for
operations and maintenance of rivers and main canals outside the drainage areas, as the main canals often
serve other users such as industries and municipalities. Cost recovery options for maintenance of rivers
and main canals are being considered, including the possibility of: (a) direct charges to landowners; and
(b) transferring the financial responsibility of this maintenance to the respective municipalities which
would then recover these costs through local taxes. At negotiations, an agreement was reached that the
members of the L WA benefiting from the Project's drainage rehabilitation shall contribute 20% of the
costs of the drainage rehabilitation works either in cash or by providing labor (para 5. 1 (e)).
2.19           Public Investment Program  for Related Rural Infrastructure. The GOE has allocated
significant sums in the Public Investment Program for 1995-1997 for rural infrastructure improvement.'7
The GOE would ensure that the necessary rural infrastructure upgrading, currently planned under the
Public Investment Program, would be provided at the locations of the participating LWAs as a
complement to their improved drainage systems.
Land Use Management (US $0.9 million)
2.20           Wetlands Management Strategy Study. The main objective of the study would be to
develop a national strategy for wetlands and to recommend a classification and identification system to
establish the value of wetlands. The study covers wetlands in forest and agriculture areas: drained lands
no longer cultivated, or to be abandoned to natural regrowth; peat-mined areas for restoration; and the
management of polders no longer under active operation. The study would be managed by the MOE and

coordinated with the "Wetstonia" initiative of the World Wide Fund for Nature/Estonian Fund for Nature

2.21          Environmental Monitoring of Drainage Rehabilitation. The MOE is responsible for
monitoring all drainage rehabilitation works and developing appropriate environmental screening criteria
and procedures to ensure environmental standards of the works. The project would support the MOE by
financing laboratory equipment to analyze and monitor water samples from the proposed drainage sites,
and local consultants to conduct environmental screening of the proposed sites. As a condition for
effectiveness, the Borrower would promulgate official regulations regarding the environmental screening
criteria and procedures applicable to all drainage rehabilitation works in the borrower's territory; and
have published these regulations and procedures in the popular media (para 5.3 (a)).
2.22          Environmental Studies for Future Drainage Investments. The Project would support a
sectoral environmental drainage study to analyze future options for drained lands, and the measures needed
to convert drained lands from present use to other uses such as forest land, parks, wildlife protection areas
or wetlands. This study would be managed by the Water Department in the MOE.
Agricultural Advisory Services (US $6.0 million)
2.23          The Agricultural Advisory Services component consists of: (a) the establishment of the
Private Advisory Services Development Fund (PASDF); (b) strengthening the public extension services;
and (c) support to rural information centers. The MOA has created a National Agriculture Extension Task
Force and would appoint staff to formulate the future extension strategy of the MOA, the operation of the
PASDF, and the extension program for the publicly funded extension service. This task force membership
would be expanded to include representation of all stakeholders"8, and would be supported through
ongoing technical assistance financed by EU (Phare). In addition, the MOA, with EU (Phare) assistance,
will train and certify public and private agricultural advisors,"9 especially in economics and farm
management, extension methods, and new technologies.
2.24          The Private Advisory Services Development Fund (PASDF) would be established under
the Project to develop advisory services targeted at the most viable farms (presently about 2,500 family
and 1,200 joint stock farms).20 The PASDF would, on a demand basis, provide the farmers with an
annual grant to partially fund their contract with the agricultural advisors of their choice. Funding is
planned to serve about 2,800 farmers in 1996 expanding to about 5,000 by the end of the Project period,
equivalent to about 100 and 170 full time advisors respectively. The private advisors would be recruited
from the existing the MOA county-level staff and the other advisors already working in the sector. They
would provide advice on farm production, business skills including loan applications, marketing, farm
organization, and other areas. A farmer could apply for PASDF support from the county extension office,
select an advisor and sign a contract which includes terms of reference and payment details. The selected
is     This includes representatives of various farmers' groups, product-specific groups, advisors, research institutes,
universities, commercial suppliers of agricultural inputs, Ministry of Environment, the media, and environmental NGOs.

20     Family farm numbers are expected to expand during the Project period as the joint-stock farms downsize.

2.25         The publicly funded extension service would be strengthened through donor funded
training and technical assistance to develop educational programs on improved agricultural practices
adapted to the transformed farms. This support is mainly directed at the 150 agricultural extension agents
employed by the Farmers' Union (salaries paid by the GOE) who target their services to the middle
category of farms (about 10,000 family farms and 1,000 joint stock farms). The Project also supports the
development of training materials for the extension programs (both public and private).
2.26         Rural Information Centers would be strengthened with equipment from the Project and
donor-funded technical assistanceto assistthe rural population in finding alternative off-farm employment.
About 5 rural information centers have already been established, financed by the GOE and managed by
the Ministry of Economy. The Rural Information Centers target those farmers (estimated 5,000 family
farms and 1,500 joint stock farms) that may need to supplement their income through non-farming
activities, or even leave farming altogether.
Food Quality Control and Veterinary Laboratory (US $1.7 million).
2.27         The Project would strengthen the National Veterinary Control Laboratory through: (a)
acquiring laboratory equipment for the National Veterinary Control Laboratory to improve monitoring and
testing of contaminants and harmful residues in agricultural food products of animal origin and to improve
the testing capacities for infectious animal diseases; and (b) training staff, technicians, and scientists of
the National Veterinary Control Laboratory in the operations and maintenance of the newly acquired
equipment. Both interventions are necessary for Estonia's agricultural products to meet EU food quality
certification requirements. This component would be complemented by donor-funded projects to provide
technical assistance and training for the development of food legislation, institutional strengthening,
establishment and operations of modem food quality control facilities, both within the agro-processing
industries, and as commercial services to them.
Project Management (US $0.9 million).
2.28         A Project Implementation Unit (PIU) would be established to serve all components of the
Project in planning and management, procurement, accounting, reporting, monitoring and evaluation,
general administration, and overall coordination among the line implementing agencies (Chapter 3).
Project funds would support the hiring of consultants and local contract staff. The PIU would assist both
the Agriculture Project and the EU (Phare) Agricultural Assistance Program in implementing these
projects.

contingencies). The total Project cost include US $4 million in price23 and US $1 million in physical
contingencies. Physical contingencies have been estimated by adding 5% to the base costs of most
investment expenditure items, with the exception of the PASDF. Physical contingencies are not applied
to this item, because it represents a projected expenditure allocation ceiling. A summary of Project costs
by component is presented in Table 2.2.

2.31             The GOE is eligible for single currency loans under the expanded SCL program  for 100%
of its anticipated FY96 lending program of US $15.3 million equivalent. The Borrower informed the
Bank during negotiations that it wishes to borrow in a single currency (DEM) at a variable LIBOR-based
interest rate for the proposed Project. The Estonian delegation justified the choice of a single currency
loan by stating that most of Estonian's loans are LIBOR-based to ensure that the country does not pay
higher than market rates at any point in time. Estonian's currency is pegged to the Deutsche Mark.

F. Procuremene5
2.34         At negotiations agreement was reached that all procurement would be carried out in
accordance with the Bank's Guidelines for Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits (January,
1995), and Guidelines for Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by the World Bank as
Executing Agency (August, 1981) (para 5.1 (h)) Table 2.4 summarizes the procurement amounts and
methods.
2.35         The GOE recognizes the importance of establishing public procurement regulations as part
of its transition to a competitive market economy. The Bank has been assisting with this since February
1994. Estonia's Law on Public Procurement was approved by the Council of Ministers in May 1995 and
was passed by the Parliament in the fall of 1995. The recently completed procurement strategy paper
recommended a central Procurement Advisory Service (PAS) in the Ministry of Economy, which has been
established with assistance from the Finnish Government. A "General Procurement Notice" containing
information about bidding opportunities has been prepared and it will be published in the "Development
Business" in accordance with the Bank's Guidelines for Procurement.
2.36         Procurement Management and Training. Procurement would be the responsibility of the
implementing Ministries, supported by the PIU. The PIU would hire a full-time procurement specialist
with qualifications approved by the Bank, who would be assisted by an international procurement
consultant. In addition, the Ministries would be assisted by the newly established PASDF. Relevant
personnel from the MOA, the MOE, and MOF have participated, and would participate in procurement
training courses organized by the Bank. The efficiency and capability of the Borrower to carry out the
Bank's procurement procedures would be strengthened through the services to be provided by PASDF,
training, and the use of Standard Bidding Documents. A project launch workshop (training in
procurement, disbursement, accounting and reporting requirements) is scheduled prior to project
effectiveness.
2.37          Civil Works (US $7.9 million). All civil works would be procured through National
Competitive Bidding (NCB). NCB items for civil works are for drainage rehabilitation and maintenance.
The number of contracts is expected to be about 60. NCB is appropriate given that: (a) the average
values of works per site are small (less than US $150,000 and would not exceed $300,000); (b) locations
are scattered geographically (and thus not permitting "packaging" of a number of site contracts) and cover
non-contiguous drainage systems of 100 to 150 hectares in size; (c) implementation requires continuous
participation and contributions from local farmers; (d) ownership of the drainage rehabilitation is linked
with intensive participatory preparation of establishing Land and Water Associations; and (e) small
contracts to local contractors reduce mobilization and organization costs. Domestic bidders would not
25    Major procurement packages and their schedules are presented in the respective Project Implementation Plans (see

receive preferences, and contracts must be awarded to the lowest evaluated responsive bidder. The

2.39         Technical Services (US$ 6.7 million). This would consist of surveying contracts for
individual packages at an estimated cost of US$ 250,000 each, through NCB procedures acceptable to the
Bank, and following a prequalification of prospective contractors. At negotiations, the Borrower agreed
on the principles for standardized bidding documents for technical services. As a Condition for Board
presentation, the bidding documents and the pre-qualification criteriafor the technical services (rural land
surveys) would be submitted to the Bankfor review (para 5.2 (a)).
2.40         Consultant Services are divided into several categories:
(a)  Studies (US $0.8 million). The Project would finance studies on land use management and for
agricultural advisory services.
(b)  Design (US $1.2 million). The Project would introduce standardized contracts for engineering
design and civil works supervision for drainage rehabilitation works. Each Amelioration Bureau
(AB) would select a consulting company, through a competitive process, to provide all engineering
design and civil works supervision for all drainage rehabilitation sites within the responsibility of
the AB. The contracts would be grouped into packages of approximately US $50,000 each and
administered by the 12 ABs.
(c) Agricultural Advisory Services (US $4.4 million). The Bank would partly finance the
employment of consultants (local and possibly some foreign experts) in connection with the
provision of private advisory services for priority programs on agricultural extension. The
beneficiaries of the agricultural advisory services would directly procure their own private advisors
using a standardized contract acceptable to the Bank. Given the large number of private advisors26
and the objective of the Project to develop a competitive private advisory services, a large number
of individual contracts would be submitted for reimbursement. The MOA would self-finance 20%
of payments made by the PASDF for agricultural advisory services. As a condition for
disbursement, the Borrower would present to the Bank a standardized contract format for
agricultural advisors' services, and the selection criteria for agricultural advisors to be eligible
under PASDF, both satisfactory to the Bank (para 5.4(b)).
(d) Training (US $0.4 million). Consultants would be contracted for training related to food quality
control; development of LWAs; and skill development training in extension.

exceeding US $50,000; all contracts with consulting firns exceeding US $100,000; and terms of reference
for all consultant services irrespective of the contract value. Other procurement would be subject to the
