H. Environmental Aspects
2.48          The proposed project has been reviewed consistent with the requirements of Estonian
Governmental Regulation No. 314 of November, 1992, concerning "Environmental Impact Assessment"
and the provisions of the Bank's Operational Directive 4.01, "Environmental Assessment. " A review of
environmental aspects of the proposed project was prepared with the participation of the MOE, which
confirms that all components correspond to Estonian environmental regulations, and this is provided as
Annex 4. The farm drainage rehabilitation component has been placed in screening category "B" and
activities to be supported under this component would be subject to environmental screening and review
consistent with procedures jointly developed by the MOE and the Bank. Annex 4 describes the potential
impacts associated with these components, provides environmental screening procedures for sub-activities,
recommends mitigation measures to minimize these impacts, and reviews proposed monitoring actions.
All other project components have been placed in screening category "C."
2.49          The Wetlands Management Strategy Study has been classified as being of State importance
by the MOE because it addresses key environmental issues in Estonia. The MOE plans to have the
preparation of the Wetlands Management Strategy Study include the preparation of an environmental
impact assessment consistent with Estonian procedures to allow for upstream consideration of
environmental issues in this strategy and to provide an effective means for public participation.



Estonia Agriculture Project                                                          27
2.50         Environmental studies for future domestically financed options in drainage investments
would be conducted to ensure that investment project designs are environmentally sensitive and to improve
the sustainability of drainage infrastructure and promote environmental protection. A framework for
environmentally sustainable management of wetlands would be established through the Wetlands
Management Strategy Study. Overall, the Project has been designed to integrate environmental controls
and seek public input on environmental concerns. A number of NGOs (Estonia Fund for Nature,
Stockholm Environment Institute-Tallinn) have been consulted during the project preparation process,
especially regarding the drainage rehabilitation component. Representatives of the Matsalu State Nature
Reserve have been consulted on the design of environmental screening criteria for drainage rehabilitation
in the catchment area of the reserve, given the ecological importance of this area.
I. Poverty Impact
2.51         Since independence, Estonia has moved rapidly towards a market economy. During the
transition period, the living standard for segments of the population have declined as the costs of living
have increased faster than average wages. The transition period has been especially severe on the rural
population and the rural population is among those with the highest probability of becoming poor. Since
the situation in Estonia is changing rapidly, only limited and piecemeal information is available on rural
poverty. Project beneficiaries (the rural population), have a significantly larger proportion of poor people
than the population as a whole. However, the current restructuring of agriculture has had a negative
impact for some segments of the rural population.
2.52         The following differential impacts may be seen:
(a) Rural Unemployment. Employment in the agriculture sector has declined from around 100,000
to about 30,000 from 1991 to 1994 but this decline seems to have lessened. Some of the
redundant farm laborers might have found jobs in the expanding service sector, but many have
to depend on seasonal or part-time employment, and on the products of their household plots until
the rural job market improves. The Project actively pursues the creation of rural jobs and off-farm
employment through it's various components.
(b) Farmers in areas with good agricultural potential would benefit as the project components
specifically support the development of economically viable farms. However, it is expected that
farmers in marginal areas would not benefit from the on-farm investments in drainage
rehabilitation but would have access to the rural advisory services to identify potential alternative
off-farm income sources.
(c) Urban and Rural Consumers would benefit from the project as food products continue to be
produced at or below world market prices, on efficient and unsubsidized farms. Lower food prices
would directly benefit the poorer segments of the population.
2.53         The Project would support policies that deliberately target the viable agricultural sector
and might therefore negatively affect a number of farm workers. However, the support to the rural
economy would increase off-farm employment and rural job opportunities. Furthermore, the benefits of
the reform policy accruing to the majority of the rural and the urban population clearly outweigh the short-
term costs. In the long run, the reform policies of the GOE supported by the Agriculture Project would
create a competitive and productive rural sector that provides jobs and improves incomes for the rural
population, and is not dependent on income transfers from taxpayers or consumers.



28                                                                              Chapter I
J. Gender and Family Issues
2.54         The need for a specific loan component to address gender issues was evaluated during
project preparation and not seen as necessary. Men and women in Estonia have equal rights to land
ownership. Women share responsibility in the rural sector in Estonia, including, for example, management
responsibilities in large farm organizations, and political leadership for rural municipalities. The breakup
of large collective farms is expected to create more family operated farms and businesses. The social
network of many of the former collective farming structures, such as child care and community activities
has been partly taken over by the municipalities. Downsizing of the agricultural sector may result in a lack
of employment opportunities for the younger and the older generation -in the rural areas. The Government
therefore plans to target support (outside of the Project) directly to these groups in the rural areas.



M. PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. Organization and Management
3.1          Proiect Steering Committee. Overall Project coordination would be handled by a Project
Steering Committee (PSC) consisting of: (a) key members from the Ministries of Agriculture, Environment
and Finance; and (b) associate members from at least the Ministries of Justice, the Farmers' Union, and
the Producers' Union, the Food Board, a representative of the municipalities concerned, and two Joint
Project Directors from the MOA and the MOE. The key members of the PSC would meet on a monthly
basis and, with the associate members, every three months. The PSC would be maintained until
completion of the Project. The GOE has designated two Joint Proiect Directors one each from the MOA
and the MOE at Chancellor or Vice-Chancellor level, to supervise Project implementation. Day-to-day
implementation responsibility would be assigned to the Project Manager and the Project Coordinator from
the MOE. Draft terms of reference for PSC, and its membership, are provided in Annex 5.
3.2          A centralized Proiect Implementation Unit (PIU) would be established within the MOA,
headed by a Project Manager and reporting directly to the Joint Project Directors. The major task of the
PIU is to assist the line agencies of the MOA and the MOE in implementing the Agriculture Project and
administering EU (Phare)'s assistance program for agriculture. The PIU would be responsible for: (a)
coordination of, and support to, line implementing agencies; (b) administration, accounting, and financial
management; (c) procurement coordination and supervision; (d) loan disbursement; (e) monitoring and
evaluation; and (f) reporting. The PIU would consist of a Project Manager, EU (Phare) program
coordinator, procurement specialist, advisory services and monitoring & extension specialist, accountant,
two secretaries and drivers. Draft terms of reference for the PIU, its local staff and consultants are
provided in Annex 5. The PIU would assist both the Agriculture Project and EU (Phare)'s agricultural
assistance program in procurement, disbursement, monitoring & evaluation, project administration and
supervision of Project implementation. This joint PIU, which effectively continues and widens the tasks
of the currently operating EU (Phare) PIU, would be established in early 1996 and financed by EU
(Phare). The MOA, EU (Phare) and the Bank are presently negotiating a Memorandum of Understanding
on the funding and supervision of the PIU, to support the Bank's, EU (Phare)'s and other donors'
programs. The joint PIU would operate prior to effectiveness.
3.3          The MOA and the MOE would implement the individual components through the normal
day-to-day functioning of their responsible line agencies under the overall supervision of the Project
Manager and the MOE Project Coordinator and with administrative assistance from the PIU. The
implementing agencies would be responsible for procurement and would receive assistance from the PIU
and the Procurement Advisory Service established in the Ministry of Economy to provide general
assistance in procurement. During Project preparation, the existing EU (Phare) PIU has supported the
preparation activities and will continue to assist the implementing line agencies until the joint Bank - EU
(Phare) PIU is in place. Currently, the implementing line agencies are receiving assistance from the EU
(Phare) PIU, consultants, and from the Bank's resident office in Tallinn, for Project preparation.
3.4          Coordination of all donor-funded technical assistance is under each of these Ministries'
Foreign Relations Department. The Bank would exercise general oversight of the donor-funded technical
assistance complementary to the Project, with prior agreement from the donors and the GOE, and in the
context of the Bank's normal supervision.

accounting, Project management, and monitoring & evaluation with qualifications
and terms of reference satisfactory to the Bank. (para. 5.3 (b)).

operating costs for the proper functioning of the Project administration in the subsequent budget

agreement.

Ministry of Agriculture
3.7           The MOA, through the PIU, would provide Project coordination and administration
functions for its Project components.
3.8           The Land Amelioration Unit (LAU) and its regional offices -- Amelioration Bureaux
(ABs) -- would be responsible for the implementation of the drainage rehabilitation component. The
LAU, located in the MOA, is responsible for the supervision of the ABs. The LAU would also oversee
site selection and design of drainage rehabilitation, coordinate environmental site screening with the MOE,
and provide technical support for regional efforts to establish LWAs. The LAU would coordinate: (a)
consulting services from local institutions, and from donor-sponsored technical assistance,2 for training for
LAU staff in social assessment and participatory techniques for LWA formation; and (b) the drainage
research sub-component with The Institute of Water Management of the Agriculture University of Tartu.
The LAU would also be responsible for coordination with the MOE on issues of environmental impact
assessment of each drainage site.
3.9           The National Agricultural Extension Task Force (NAETF),3 with wide stakeholder
participation, would formulate and periodically monitor and modify the operations of the PASDF and the
future extension strategy for the MOA. The MOA and its county-level extension offices are responsible
for administering the PASDF. As a condition of effectiveness, the MOA would have, to the satisfaction
of the Bank; expanded the participation in the NAETF to fully reflect the wide diversity of stakeholder
interests; the stakeholders 'representatives would have been appointed by their constituent groups; and
the MOA would have provided the NAETF with a secretariat consisting of a manager, staff an operating
budget, and terms of reference (para 5.3(d)).

Ministry of Environment
3.11            The MOE would implement the Project through its relevant line departments. The MOE's
Department of Environment and Water (DEW) would be responsible for operational coordination at the
working level (among the two implementing departments within the MOE,4 and between the MOE and
the MOA) and for ensuring that the environmental requirements of the Project's components are fulfilled.
The Land Board would be responsible for implementation of the land reform component.
3.12            The Department of Environment and Water5 in addition to its coordinating role with the
MOE and the MOA, would be responsible for the environmental screening of the drainage rehabilitation
component. The County Environmental Departments (CEDs) are responsible for environmental screening
of sites proposed for drainage rehabilitation, and would revise agricultural extension materials to include
environmental issues. Environmental issues of national interest, that may arise from the county level
screening of the drainage sites, would be referred by the CEDs to the DEW for appropriate actions. DEW
would manage the Wetlands Management Strategy Study and the Environmental Studies for Future
Drainage Investments.6
3.13            Estonia National Land Board. The ENLB will be responsible for: (a) development of
cadastral registration and cadastral mapping in Estonia; (b) overseeing the decentralization of cadastral
work to the county level; (c) streamlining the surveying and registration process; and (d) ensuring
coordination with the Title Book Offices. The ENLB will be responsible for contracting out all the survey
contracts through competitive bidding procedures, and for supervising the contracts. The ENLB would
appoint staff required for the coordination of the land reform component and would closely liaise with the
Land Reform Commission'. A Contract Administration Unit (CA 11 will be established in ENLB to
organize and supervise the contracting of the land survey works. The CAU will organize this competitive
bidding and perform relevant accounting functions. The project coordinator of the CAU would coordinate
with the local municipalities, the relevant ministries, and other involved authorities. Survey contracts will
be prepared together with the local municipalities. At negotiations an agreement was reached, that, by
June 30, 1996, the Borrower would establish a Contract Administration Unity with a manager and four
staff to manage and supervise the land survey contracts (technical services) (para 5. 1 (k)).

C. Participation
3.14          The preparation of the Project has involved representatives of the Govermment,
environmental NGO's, local authorities, and outside experts in preparing detailed project implementation
plans for each component. In addition, through technical assistance and the Bank's own staff efforts,' the
participatory approach has been introduced for the design of the drainage rehabilitation works, assessment
of the potential environmental impacts of drainage rehabilitation, and for the formation of the LWAs. The
Project will ensure that the farmers, organized in LWAs, will be involved throughout the process, assisting
in the design, and contributing to the implementation of the rehabilitation works. The Project would
utilize this participatory approach in the development of the national Agricultural Extension Plan. This
involvement of stakeholders (farmers and advisors) in the design of both the drainage and the agricultural
advisory systems could result in new grass roots farmers' organizations (LWAs) developing a range of
additional services to members such as input supply, machinery pools,9 marketing and possibly credit.
D. Implementation Schedule
3.15          The time-based implementation schedules for each of the Project components, and the
major sub-components are included in the individual Project Implementation Plans (PIPs) of Project
components, as well as in the "Implementation Plan Summary" (Annexes -Volume 2).
E. Accounts and Audit
3.16          The Borrower's records and accounts of the Project, including the Special Account, for
each fiscal year would be audited by independent auditors acceptable to the Bank and furnished to the
Bank within four months after the end of each year. (para 5.1J()). The audit reports would contain
separate opinions on: (a) compliance by implementing agencies with relevant covenants of the Loan
Agreement with the Bank; (b) the Statements of Expenditures (SOE); and (c) the Special Account. The
Bank required audits would not cover donor funded activities, even if supervised by the Bank.
F. Reporting and Evaluation
3.17          The implementing line agencies responsible for various aspects of the Project would
prepare quarterly reports covering progress in the physical implementation of the Project, the use of
Project funds and, where appropriate, the Project's impact. The format of these reports would be agreed
with the Bank. The reports would be consolidated by the PSC into semi-annual progress reports to be
submitted through MOF to the Bank, and relevant co-financiers within two months of the end of each six-
month reporting period. These semi-annual progress reports would also include an implementation plan
and work program for the six months following the reporting period. The results of environmental
screening shall be included in the semi-annual progress reports for the drainage investments.
Including use of the Bank's Fund for Innovative Approaches in Human and Social Development (FLAHS).

G. Bank Supervision
3.20          Given that there is, as yet, limited experience with implementing Bank Projects in Estonia,
as well as the fact that the Project covers several aspects of both public and private sector rural
development, there would be a significant role for Bank supervision. The supervision missions would use
the Project Objectives and Development Indicators (Annex 2) for the M&E of the Project. It is
recommended that Bank supervision include":
(a)  a Project-launch mission to establish with the implementing Ministries, the operational, accounting
and reporting procedures for the Special Account;
(b)  semi-annual supervision missions, to review the status of Project implementation and assist with
any issues that might arise. The first supervision mission would take place within six months of
effectiveness;
(c)  an implementation review to be carried out within 12 months after loan effectiveness to determine
any revisions that might be needed in the implementation plan; this implementation review would
be conducted in conjunction with a supervision mission;
(d)  a mid-term  review, not later than end-June 1998, to be carried out in conjunction with a
supervision mission, based on the review of M&E performance indicators as of end-December
1997;

specific drainage investment Projects for consistency with World Bank Operational Directive 4.01,

(f)  recognition of the importance of effective implementation of "environmental screening procedures"
to the success of the drainage rehabilitation component, field based supervision would be
conducted by Bank environmental staff for proposed pilot activities at Vigala and Rannu to assure
their effectiveness and transferability to future activities; and

IV. BENEFITS AND RISKS
A. Benefits and Justification'
4.1           The major benefits are:
*      accelerated development of a rural land market;
*      increased agricultural production and farm income in the drainage rehabilitation areas;
*      improved farmers' information on new farming technologies and management practices;
*      development of grassroots rural institutions;
*      improved food and livestock quality standards to protect consumers and increase export
potential of food and livestock products to the EU;
*      protection of the environment through environmental controls in agriculture, drainage, and
wetlands management; and
*      revitalization and development of farms, services and enterprises that would create
additional employment opportunities in rural areas.
4.2           Country and Agricultural Strategv. The Project supports the GOE's strategy to transform
the rural sector, through support of private agricultural and rural initiatives, into an economically viable
and competitive market-based system. The Project selectively provides investments to improve existing
key infrastructure in target areas (farm drainage rehabilitation), strengthens the public agricultural
extension service and supports the development of private agricultural advisors, and enhances food quality
control functions. The Project focuses on investments that initiate a gradual transfer of Government
functions to the private sector (advisory services, drainage maintenance), or improve core Government
functions (food quality control, public advisory services, environmental controls in agriculture).
4.3           Scope of the Analysis and Summary Results. The financial and economic analysis is
divided into two sections: (a) The financial and economic farm model analysis of the profitability of crop
and livestock production, which shows an estimated ERR of 16% (the farm model analysis shows that the
Project investments in drainage rehabilitation and private extension, if complemented by privately financed
on-farm investments in farm assets, would increase farm incomes by 40% after two years compared to
a situation without Project); (b) the major economic benefits and the expected impact of the Project
investments on Government expenditures of each component are analyzed, partially using cost-
effectiveness analysis.
I     The Economic and Financial Analysis of the Project is described in detail in Annex 3.



36                                                                                    Chapter IV
B. Financial and Economic Farm Model Analysis
4.4           Dairy farming is the dominant farming system in Estonia, and has the highest potential
to develop into a profitable enterprise. The farm model analysis assesses the impact of the Project
investments in farrn drainage rehabilitation and agricultural advisory services on farm income from dairy
farms. In order to increase farm production and returns to capital and labor on a dairy farm, investments
in drainage rehabilitation and agricultural advisory services have to be accompanied by other investments,
typically in buildings (farm buildings, milking parlor), machinery (milking system), or livestock (milking
cows). The farm model analysis therefore provides a financial and economic rate of return of the Project
investments in drainage and advisory services in conjunction with assumed complementary privately
financed investments in farm machinery, livestock and buildings. The investments would be self-financed
or financed through commercial bank loans.
4.5           The impact of a combined investment in drainage rehabilitation, advisory services for
promotion of improved pasture and livestock management supported through the Project, and the privately
financed on-farm investments in farm buildings, livestock and machinery are evaluated using a model of
a 25-ha private dairy farm. In the model calculation, the bulk of the investment costs (estimated at about
EEK 90,000) can be attributed to buildings, machinery, and livestock (88% of the combined investment
costs),2 whereas the corresponding costs to the farmer for drainage rehabilitation and advisory services are
considerably lower (9% and 3% respectively of the combined investment costs).3
4.6           The Financial Rate of Return (FRR) of drainage rehabilitation, agricultural advisory
services and the complementary privately financed investments in farm assets is estimated at 22% for the
model farm. This FRR is based on the farmers' contribution to the investment requirements, the
incremental benefits of these investments, and the projected incremental costs of agricultural production.
4.7            Farm Income and Farmers' Debt Servicing Capacitv. Assuming a fanner would take a
loan (EEK 90,000; 10 year maturity; 2 year grace period; 4% real interest rate) to improve a 25 ha dairy
farrn, a farmer could service the loan and after two years achieve an annual income that would be 40%
higher than without the investment. Nine years after the initial investment, the model farm would provide
a monthly income of EEK 1,130 per person4, which is comparable to the national average wage and nearly
twice as high as the average farm wage. Model calculations for larger farms (50 ha and 100 ha) show
that the profitability further increases with economies of scale.
4.8           Farm Model Economic Analysis. The economic analysis of the farm models uses import
parity prices for cereals and fertilizers.4 All other input and output prices have been converted using a
standard conversion factor of 1.0, equating financial and economic prices.5 The economic costs for
2      Farm credits are available from commercial banks refinanced through ARLCF. The GOE has allocated EEK 100
million in 1996 to ARLCF for on-lending to farmers,
3      Farmers would contribute 20% of the rehabilitation investments and around 10% of the costs of the advisory services,
with the GOE covering the balances on a grant basis.
4      Using constant end 1994 prices.
5      Because of Estonia's open economy and the absence of tariff barriers.



Estonia Agriculture Project                                                               37
investments in drainage rehabilitation and advisory services reflect the full costs of these investments to
the economy, i.e. they include both the farmer's and the GOE's portion of these investments.
4.9           The Economic Rate of Return (ERR) of the farm model is estimated at 16% and the Net
Present Value (NPV) is EEK 31,000 for a 25 ha farm model. The ERR is lower than the FRR in the
financial analysis reflecting the full investment costs for drainage rehabilitation and agricultural advisory
services to the economy.
Sensitivity Analysis for the Farm Model
4.10          Overall, the ERR of the farm models is not sensitive to significant changes in costs and
benefits: benefits would need to fall by 24% or costs increase by 31% for the ERR to fall to 10% (i.e.
with a net present value of zero). If the benefits are delayed by two years, the ERR would drop to 11%.
In addition, a risk analysis6 for key assumptions, including: (a) inadequate rehabilitation and maintenance
of the drainage systems beyond the project period; and (b) change of key agricultural output prices, has
been prepared. The risk analysis shows that the ERR is sensitive to shortfalls in projected crop yield that
might occur if drainage systems are not maintained. To ensure the projected yields are achieved in the
Project area, the Project therefore carefully targets the investments to the high potential agricultural areas
where farmers arguably have the highest incentives to maintain the infrastructure. Farmers' participation
during rehabilitation and subsequent privatization of the drainage infrastructure would also promote a sense
of ownership and responsibility for maintaining the drainage system. The crop and milk yield assumptions
of the "with Project scenario" have been conservatively estimated and are not more than 10% higher than
current average actual yields in Estonia. The risk of cost overruns is reduced by limiting rehabilitation
expenditures to EEK 1,500 per hectare. It is also unlikely that crop or livestock prices would fall by more
than 20%, due to the currently depressed output prices caused by the inefficient processing sector.
C. Analysis of Benefits by Component
4.11          This section provides an analysis of the economic benefits of the expected development
impact of each component. However, as illustrated in the farm model analysis, many of the benefits of
the components are interrelated and can not be analyzed, or achieved, in isolation.
Land Reform
4.12          Benefits.  The Project would support the development of a rural land market by
accelerating the registration of rural land in the cadastre and title book. These measures would (a)
facilitate the access of farmers to long-term credit because commercial banks would be more inclined to
accept land with a secure, transferable and mortgageable title as collateral, and (b) increase the movement
of agricultural land through sale or lease to efficient agricultural producers, thereby increasing agricultural
production and incomes.
4.13          Cost Effectiveness Analysis.  Land with a registered, secure, transfereable and
mortgageable title could be used as collateral to obtain a long-term mortgage loan from a commercial
bank. The benefit of land title registration could be assessed as the difference between the interest rate
6      See Annex 3.



38                                                                                    Chapter IV
for a long-term mortgage loan which is typically lower than the interest rate of a loan of the same maturity
using a moveable collateral (currently about 20 - 25%). A simple cost-effectiveness analysis shows that
a reduction of the interest rate for a loan of EEK 100,000 (10 years maturity, no grace period, constant
principal repayment) by 2% would be sufficient to cover the estimated costs of EEK 9,625 for land
surveys and cadastre registration of an average 25 ha farm. The component would have substantial
financial and economic benefits for the economy by supporting the development of mortgage banking.
4.14          Cost Recovery. The GOE plans to recover the costs for the land surveys from the
property owners. For this purpose, the GOE is developing incentives to assist the owners in financing
land surveys and registration in the cadastre and title book. The land surveys would be conducted by
around 200 private surveying companies operating in Estonia.
Farm Drainage Rehabilitation
4.15          The Project would finance rehabilitation of drainage systems on about 60,000 ha of the
most productive drained farmland, covering less than 10% of the total drained farmland in the country.
Well maintained and functioning drainage systems are a pre-requisite to increasing the efficiency of
Estonian livestock farms. After fully rehabilitating the drainage system in the Project area, crop
production would increase by about 11,000 tons of feed grain, 35,000 tons of hay, and 12,000 tons of
potato annually. The incremental production of feed grain and hay, even though these crops could be
valued with market prices, is only used as an intermediate good for dairy and livestock production and
should not be valued separately'. The farm model analysis therefore provides the best approximation of
the benefits of drainage rehabilitation. In addition, the organization of farmers in LWAs and the
involvement of the farmers in the design of the drainage system could also result in new community
services provided by these LWAs such as input supplies, machinery pools, marketing and possibly credit.
Tlhe drainage rehabilitation investments would directly benefit small local contractors carrying out the civil
works and also stimulate the development of other rural support services to agriculture in the rural areas.
4.16          Cost Recoverv. The GOE plans to privatize the ownership, operations, and maintenance
of all farm drainage systems by handing over these systems to LWAs after completion of the drainage
rehabilitation works. Each LWA must agree to contribute (in cash or in kind) 20% of the cost of the
rehabilitation works, the remaining 80% would be covered by the GOE with budget and Project loan
funds. In return, the GOE would, post-rehabilitation, no longer accept any further financial responsibility
for these on-farm drainage systems. Each LWA would have full ownership of its area-specific drainage
system and bear its full O&M costs. This will reduce the GOE's future investment costs in rehabilitating
drainage systems and its recurrent expenditures for operation and maintenance of these systems.
Agricultural Advisory Services
4.17          Benefits.  Extension plays a key role in the dissemination of information on new
technologies, and improved organizational and management practices. Research results confirm the
significant and positive impact of extension in many countries. Extension would likely have a substantial
7      A separate economic rate of return has been calculated for the farm drainage rehabilitation component and is estimated
at 23% and the NPV at EEK 26 million. Estimated benefits include the incremental production of feed grains, hay and potato
valued at local market prices. Costs include all rehabilitation investments,O&M costs and costs for establishment of LWAs over
15 years.



Estonia Agriculture Project                                                             39
impact on the transformation of the Estonian farming sector to a market oriented production system.
However, the benefits of extension during the transition period are extremely difficult to quantify due to
the paucity of data and the lack of experience in similar situations. Evidence from an extension project
in northern Estonia shows that minor improvements such as adequate maintenance of the milking
equipment results in significant quality improvements. Quality improvements would substantially increase
farm income because of the large difference in payment for low quality and high quality milk.
4.18         Public versus Private Advisory Services. The Project assists the establishment of a new
advisory system in Estonia consisting of a public and a private extension service. The Private Advisory
Services Development Fund requires only an initial investment, because the farmers are expected (in the
medium-term) to fully pay for advice that directly benefits their farm operation. Over an eight year period
the GOE's contribution to the private advisory service would be gradually phased out. In addition, the
GOE, with Project support, would continue to provide advice to farmers who can not afford the private
advisory services, but only with public domain technical information. The GOE would therefore reduce
its active involvement in extension to a more strategic and supervisory role, thereby containing recurrent
Government expenditures for extension.
Food Quality Control and Veterinary Services
4.19         Benefits. The Project would improve state food quality and veterinary laboratories in
order to protect domestic and foreign consumers, and increase the export potential of agricultural products.
The Project investments are aimed at enhancing and monitoring the quality of food and livestock products
in accordance with EU regulations.
4.20         Cost Effectiveness Analysis. Estonia does not fully use the quota for livestock exports
to the EU at the favorable import tariff, which is 60% less than actual tariffs. If Estonian livestock
products meet EU standards, the fulfillment of the quota would result in a tariff reduction of US $5 - 6
million over the next three years. The component investments (US $1.2 million) could therefore initiate
production quality improvements at the farm level and in the food processing industries. However, private
sector investments in production facilities at the farm level and at the agro-processing level would have
to complement the Project investments to increase the quality of food and livestock products.
4.21         Public versus Private Investment and Cost Recoverv. The Project supports the initial
phase of a broader program to improve Estonia's food quality at the production, processing and marketing
level. Part of the future control functions would most likely be undertaken by private laboratories, but
some of these functions would remain in Government hands. It is expected that the Veterinary Laboratory
would charge for the tests to partially recover costs.
Land Use Management
4.22         Benefits. This component is aimed at strengthening environmental controls in agriculture
to minimize negative external effects of agricultural production on the environment. The MOE would
actively participate in developing environmentally sustainable agriculture in Estonia. In addition, the MOE
would receive Project support to develop and enforce environmental regulations and monitor the impact
of the drainage rehabilitation investments on the environment.



40                                                                                Chapter IV
D. Risks
4.23          The main Project Management risks and the actions taken to mitigate them are:
Drainage Rehabilitation:
Risk:         The commitment and ability of the farmers to pay their share for rehabilitation and to
undertake the fullfunding offuture operations and the maintenance of their rehabilitated
drainage systems.
Mitigation:    LWA formnation is the key mitigating factor; both the MOA staff and group facilitators
have been trained in the participatory approach to group formation;8 LWA formation will
have at its core the development of the group's mutual interest in the continuing operation
of the drainage system; experience from other countries shows that mutual-interest
LWA's do work, and that group social pressure does contribute to coherence and the
fulfillment of obligations; no investment in rehabilitation at any site would be undertaken
if the LWA has not been formed and is not committed to contributing its share of the
rehabilitation costs and to undertaking all future maintenance.
Risk:         Iffarmers are unwilling to pay their share of rehabilitation costs, field investments would
be delayed, and possibly a portion of the loan commitment would not be utilized.
Mitigation:    Formation of LWAs to receive the loan-funded rehabilitation has been ongoing for about
a year prior to project effectiveness; target farmners (i.e. only those with good soils) are
aware that without rehabilitation they would not achieve the required productivity to be
profitable; the costs of contribution by the farmers is affordable from the post-investment
incremental benefits, as investment cost is limited to EEK 1,500/ha, of which the farmer
would pay 20%, much of which would be in kind, with the balance from cash or a
credit.
Agricultural Advisory Services:
Risk:         Farmers are not willing to pay for private advisory services.
Mitigation:    Farmers would be initiated into the "for-pay" system through the GOE grant partial
payment for agricultural advisory services, resulting in both familiarization with this
system and permitting changes in design depending on the farmer responses; farmers and
advisors would be represented on the National Agricultural Extension Task Force and the
design of the private service would be responsive to client needs; private agricultural
advisory services would be targeted only at those farmers with higher income potentials;
those farmers not willing to pay, or unable to pay, for private extension advice would
continue to be served by the public extension service, but at a lower frequency of
contacts, and only with public domain technical information (i.e. no business advice).
8      Including use of the Fund for Innovative Approaches in Human and Social Development (FIAHS).



Estonia Agriculture Project                                                               41
Rural Land Reform:
Risk:         Implementation of land reform will continue to be slow.
Mitigation:    The GOE has, in addition to the funds provided under this loan, allocated a substantial
amount of funds for land reform and land survey works for 1996; the policy study on
disposal of State-owned rural lands should set the GOE's future action plans; strong
political willingness of the present government to move land reforms as fast as possible;
strong desire by all sectors of the population to gain land titles; donor assistance with
strengthening institutional capacities and developing associated legal reforms.
Proiect ImRlementation Delays:
Risk:         A longer implementation period than planned would be required, due to limited local
implementation capacity and extensive farmers 'participation.
Mitigation:    Pre-launch training in participation techniques;9 donor assisted technical assistance for the
advisory service already in the field; the longer term benefits of assuring stakeholder
participation and ownership justifies the risk of a slower implementation schedule: the
project implementation schedule has taken into account a realistic time period for the
effective development of stakeholder participation and ownership.
Com2lementarv Private Sector Investments:
Risk:         The private sector would not complement the Project investments with privately financed
investments.
Mitigation:   Training and advisory services promoting investment opportunities and explaining
financial possibilities supported through technical assistance under the Project would assist
the private sector with investments: registration of secure, transfereable and mortgageable
land titles would facilitate the use of land as collateral to obtain long-term loans from the
private sector. The economic analysis shows satisfactory benefits of the proposed
investments even in the (unlikely) absence of complementary investments.
9      See foonote 7.

selection criteria. (Para. 2.13);

procedures (Pars. 2.8).
(b)  has presented to the Bank a standardized contract format for the agricultural advisory services, and
the selection criteria for agricultural advisors to be eligible under PASDF, both satisfactory to the
Bank. (Para. 2.40(c))
D. Recommendation
5.5          With the above agreements and conditions, the proposed Project would be suitable for a
Bank Loan of DEM 22 million (US $15.3 million) payable in seventeen years, with five years grace, at
the Bank's standard LIBOR-based interest rate for a Deutsche Mark single currency loan. The Borrower
would be the Republic of Estonia.

ANNEX 1
ESTONIA                              ANgE I1

Complementary Donor-funded Technical Assistance
1.    This annex presents the Technical Assistance Program identified jointly by GOE and the
Bank, within the framework of the strategy of agriculture, forestry and the rural economy, and
considered to be complementary to the investments funded under the project.
2.    For some of the items, donor-funds have been committed (time is noted where
appropriate). For the remainder GOE, is seeking interested donors.
Land Reform
3.    Land Registry Management Advice (1996-98) provides assistance to the local land registry
and title book offices. This program will be carried out by SwedSurvey and financed by BITS
as part of a larger program which has been agreed in principle to start in 1996 and continue up
to 1998.
4.    Land Assessment Methodology Development (95-96) A land assessment methodology has
been developed in close cooperation with Finnish experts as part of an ongoing project.
5.    Land and Real Estate Legal Advice (96-98) provides advice to private real estate agents
in all legal aspects of land markets. This program has been intensively reviewed and two
possible donors - Sweden (BITS financing) and Austria have been identified.
6.    Training of Surveyors (95-97) provides training for trainers in surveying, and will later
offer courses on the use of equipment and software purchased under the first phase of the EU
Phare-funded LIMPE-project. This aspect is included in the ongoing LIMPE-project and in
fiurther TA programs that are currently being developed.
7.    Training of Cadastral Specialists (96-97) provides training for the local cadastral
specialists in using a modem cadastral system. This program would be primarily included in a
EU-PHARE-program, and some part of the program would be covered by BITS and SwedSurvey.
Forest Institutions Strengthening
8.    Forest Strategy Study'. This study will review and recommend structural changes to the
forest sector as well as operational strategies and investment plans, with the overall objective to
achieve a proper balance between environmental, social and economic aspects of the sector. It
would include an assessment of appropriate user fees for public and private harvesting of wood
Finnish Governnent funded



ANNEX-1
Page 2 of 5
resources to ensure that reflect the current value of the resources and that fees for public owned
resources are adequate to cover their management and site rehabilitation costs.
9.          National Forest Inventorv. In principle all forest land in Estonia is covered by
forest management plans which are renewed on a ten-year rotation. The information from these
plans will be used as a base for simulations analyzing future available volumes from the Estonian
forests. The National Forest Inventory will consist of a systematic pattern of sample plots
covering the whole country, regardless of land use, which will be carefully measured in order to
form a statistically controllable database to be used for estimating total standing stock, age and
species distributions, areas of forest types, vegetation cover, etc. These results will be used to
calibrate the forecasts based on the forest management plans and to verify regularly that the
national forest resource and related biodiversity is managed on a sustainable level.
10.         Assistance to the Forestry Department. Technical assistance in the transformation
to market economy, and in the implementation of market economic mechanisms which have been
tested and developed as part of the Suure-Jaani project2 and defined as concrete strategies by the
Forest Strategy Study.
11.         Suure-Jaani Proiect. This Danish funded forest management project which has
now been operational for a little more than a year has been successful in introducing new
management principles and planning and control systems for a state forest region. It is important
to maintain the momentum of these achievements and continue the process so that it can be used
as a demonstration site of best practices for the Forest Strategy Study's consideration, and also
a test and demonstration area for proposals that emerge from the Forest Strategy Study.
12.         Implementation of Forest Stratey is the implementation phase of the Forest
Strategy Study, focusing on the management of the State Forests. This assistance would include
expatriate advisors to guide the forest administration in implementing recommended management
strategies. This assistance should also include support for the new forest regulatory institution,
which will be defined by the Forest Strategy Study.
13.         Training. The experiences gained in Suure-Jaani should now be used for a large
scale training program for the Forest Department. All levels of staff should be trained in
practical applications for economic and financial decisions, software systems, planning, budgeting
and control of forest operations, accounting, business administration, marketing and environmental
management. The training should be organized in a form that expatriate specialists train local
instructors, who gradually take over the whole program and turn it into a continuous education
and training unit. This training unit could also assist forest owners associations to set up special
courses for private owners on selected subjects.
2 Danish Govemnment funded.



ANNEX 1
Page 3 of 5
14.         Forest Research. Technical assistance to support a forest research program and
which would lead to the Forest Strategy Study.
15.         EU-Phare Support to Forestry. A certain amount of the EU-Phare program has
been earmarked for forestry support. At this stage this component has not been further defined,
but in principle these funds will be used for improving the information systems within the Forest
Department and as a complement to the other funding.
Rural Private Enterprise Development
16.         With the prirmary objective of fostering rural entrepreneurship, the project
preparation effort has identified a wide range of activities (for donor-financed technical
assistance) that would directly promote the establishment of rural enterprises.
17.         Farmer's co-operatives twinning. To achieve economies of scale in production and
marketing, small producers, which face processing enterprises as markets for their products, need
to work together in an organized manner, often as members of cooperatives. This will help
consolidate their bargaining power with cooperative management and with processors. Twinning
with similar functioning cooperatives in Western Europe and Scandinavia, for training of farmer
organization and cooperative members, (i.e. not cooperative managers and employees) would be
an effective means of both increasing empowerment, and of making the cooperatives more
responsive, competitive and efficient.
18.         Trade Promotion and Market Information Services. This component aims to
promote agricultural and food products on domestic and international markets. It will also
improve the transparency and accessibility of those markets through the establishment of market
information services. Trade promotion will focus on enhancing the preferences of domestic and
foreign consumers for Estonian agricultural products as a whole rather than for specific brands.
Support will be provided through technical assistance and training to an appropriate institution
(possibly the recently founded Association of Estonian Food Industry) to establish a market
development unit to offer a variety of services like market and price information for commercial
producers and intermediaries, and also more broadly-based, policy-oriented information
monitoring and analysis services.
19.         Business skills training for rural farm and non-farm enterprises. Training would
be in commercial, management and business skills necessary for the transition to the market
economy. The program would target the following five beneficiaries: (a) top- and middle-
managers of newly privatized agro-processing enterprises and joint-stock large farms (ex-
kolkhozes and sovkhozes) ;md managers of rural drainage construction companies; (b)
commercial, marketing, line-mnanagement and accounting staffs of these enterprises; (c) owners
of (new) private farms; (d) existing small- and medium-scale rural entrepreneurs; and (e)
farmers' organizations such as the Land and Water Associations, rural residents in transition from



ANNEX 1
Page 4 of 5
farming into rural service and rural enterprises. This training component would utilize the staff
at Estonian educational and training institutions operating in the rural areas: universities, technical
institutions, vocational training schools, farmers' support organizations.
20.          Farm machinery services-private sector development. Tailored to the services
provided by Germany in its transformation of agriculture in the new "Laender", this training
component targets Estonian farm co-operative recipients of donated Danish farm machinery. The
training emphasizes how to utilize this machinery to provide services on a fee basis to other
farmers, and how to avoid building overcapacity for individuals.
21.          Agro-processing engineering consultancy joint ventures. Both the major agro-
processors and the smaller scale farm-level processors require professional technical engineering
advisory services for the design and upgrading of existing plant, and the introduction of new
product lines. This is particularly important for two objectives:
(a) effective cost reductions in processing through efficiency improvement in installed
capacity; and
(b) upgrading to meet EU import food standards regulations.
22.          Estonia design engineers who wish to enter this new private sector field, that is,
a for-a-fee engineering consulting business environment, could be teamed with experienced
engineering consulting firms to form local joint-ventures. Such ventures would initially provide
services based largely on technical expertise and experience of foreign companies. Over time the
foreign expertise would be replaced with local expertise, and the role of the joint venture
partnership would be to provide technical back-stopping as a conduit for new technologies and
design innovations based on other countries' experiences.
23.          Because the Estonian industry cannot afford the fees of external design consultants,
the purpose in seeking donor assistance is therefore two-fold:
(a) to ease the cost of entry for their national consulting firms to establish these knowledge-
based joint ventures in Estonia; and
(b) to effectively subsidize the fee structure for these advisory services until both the internal
cost structure of the consulting firms decreases (due to increasing involvement of local
staff expertise) and the ability of local agro-processing enterprises to pay increases.
24.          Agro-enterprise and farm  management advisory/consultancy joint-ventures is
proposed as a service to the emerging agro-industry private sector. This TA component is ideally
targeted for those Bank loan recipients of major agro-processing investments which require
management advice during the transition period. Similar assistance is needed to developing farm
management and business advisory and training services.



ANNEX-1
Page 5 of 5
25.          Aaro-industry Studies Review. Past bilateral technical assistance provided strategic
studies for the privatization, restructuring and future market prospects for the major agro-
processing complexes (meat, dairy, grains). There is now a much-needed domestic and export
market re-analyses in order to assess optimum capacity levels. These studies will enable
individual enterprises in each sub-sector to make strategic decisions, and will assist banks in their
assessment of the viability of enterprise business and investment proposals.
26.          Joint-Venture Incentives for Forestry Nurseries. This would be a process of
utilizing export credits, bilateral grants for feasibility studies and World Bank credit lines in order
to establish two state of the art nurseries to supply the Estonian market with high quality
containerized planting stock.
27.          Support for the formation of private forest service companies. Such companies
should be able to offer services like forest planning, silviculture operations and harvesting on a
contractual basis. They should also be able to sign full management contracts for absent forest
owners. The intention is to set up a multitude of such small companies, some of which would
be initiated by entrepreneurial former employees of the Forest Department local districts who
might be superfluous once the restitution process takes off.
28.          Institutional strengthening for environmental impact assessment.  Technical
assi.tance would be used to provide the Ministry of the Environment's Environmental Impact
Assessment Department with additional capacity to address environmental issues associated with
the review and approval of agriculture and forestry projects. Counterparts in donor countries with
experience addressing environmental problems in the agriculture and forestry sector would be
identified to participate. Donors with particular expertise in projects involving agro-processing,
forestry wood processing, environmental health and hygiene, and agricultural runoff would be
sought.
29.          Strategic advice on wood industry lending. Many of the commercial banks which
will operate the FIDP credit line have limited knowledge and experience of financing wood
processing and forestry investments. This support would consist of organizing reviews of existing
credit applications, workshops on specific aspects of forest investment and on the job training.
If in this process the quality of credit applications from the forestry sector appear to be
incomplete and/or badly presented, the assistance should be extended to the companies applying
for credit. This activity would support preparation of environmental review procedures to be used
by commercial banks consistent with the procedures now being developed by MoE (with the
Bank under FIDP) for intermediate credit activities in Estonia (donor financed).

ANNEX 3
ESTONIA
AGRICULTURE PROJECT
FINANCIAL AND ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
A. INTRODUCTION
1.    In the financial and economic analysis, the Project benefits of the drainage rehabilitation
component and the agricultural advisory component are going to be quantified in terms of the output of
dairy and beef products. This analysis will be based on a dairy farm model of a typical 25 ha dairy farm
in Estonia. Dairy farms of that size are now the dominating farm type producing the bulk of the
agricultural output and have the largest potential to develop into profitable businesses. Drainage
rehabilitation will increase the agricultural production of mainly forage crops and to a lesser extent cereals
and potatoes. Rehabilitation and good maintenance of drainage systems are a key pre-requisite to improve
the efficiency of livestock and crop production in Estonia. A well drained field has a growing season
that is about a month longer than under wet conditions which then in turn allows higher crop yields or
pasture improvements with nitrogen-fixing legumes. Increasing fodder production will in turn increase
dairy and livestock production and lower production costs. Through the agricultural advisory services
component, farmers will have access to advise on improved technologies or farm management methods
through the support to the agricultural advisory service.
2.    Scope of the Analysis. This annex consists of: (a) a financial and economic farm model analysis
of the profitability of dairy and beef production; and (b) an analysis of economic benefits by Project
component.
C. FARM MODEL ANALYSIS
3.    The farm model analysis assesses the impact of the Project investments in farm drainage
rehabilitation and agricultural advisory services on farm income from dairy farms (the dominant farm type
in Estonia). However, dairy farmers would in many cases have to invest in, for instance, expanding the
herd size, or in new machinery for milking and fodder production to fully utilize the better drained fields
and use the advice offered by the extension services. The farm model analysis therefore provides a
financial and economic rate of return of the Project investments in drainage and advisory services in
combination with assumed complementary privately financed investments in farm machinery, livestock
and buildings. These Project benefits can only be evaluated accurately in conjunction with additional
privately financed on-farm investments through the farm model and not separately by component.
4.    Financial Prices and technical coefficients. Commodity prices and unit costs of production inputs
are based on actual end 1994 farmgate prices'. Farmgate prices for crop and livestock products closely
correspond to the world market prices. Production inputs (including seeds, fertilizers and agro-chemicals)
have been estimated based on information obtained from the Agricultural Research Institute in Saku and
verified through observations in the field. In the absence of reliable farm budget accounts, the costs of
I At appraisal in June 1995, harvest prices for 1995 were not yet available but the price/cost relation has not
changed from end 1994 to mid 1995.



ANNEX 3
Page 2 of 37
using farm machinery have been estimated based on international standards and modified to reflect
Estonian conditions. Quoted rental prices obtained from individual farm machine operators are
comparable to these estimated costs. The crop budgets and farm models have been discussed with staff
of the Dept. of Agricultural Economics, University of Tartu. The farm models are indicative and reflect
changes which are likely to occur on farms that have access to agricultural advice, medium-term farm
credit resources and a well functioning drainage system.
5.    Profitability of Crop Production. The profitability of the main value adding sub-sector, dairy
production, depends on a certain yield level below which the production of fodder crops and cereals would
not be profitable. The necessary crop yield level can generally only be obtained with adequate drainage.
Most of the analyzed crops, except potatoes, are intermediate goods and used as livestock feed. Therefore
the analysis of the FRR and the ERR focus on the impact of investments and increases of dairy and
livestock production on model dairy farms in Estonia.
Dairy Farm Models
6.    Farm models have been developed to evaluate the profitability of dairy farms of varying sizes and
different technology levels. The financial dairy farm models are developed to assess: (a) the current
profitability of dairy farms; (b) the financial rate of return of on-farm  investments in drainage
rehabilitation, agricultural advisory services and complementary privately financed farm assets; (c)
potential demand for loans, loan conditions, and debt service capacity of a model dairy farm, and (d) the
economic rate of return of on-farm investments in drainage rehabilitation, agricultural advisory services
and privately financed complementary farm assets. The complementary on-farm investments would be
either self-financed or financed through loans obtained from commercial banks and are not provided under
the Project.
7.    Four different dairy farm types are analyzed:
(a)    A private farm of 25 ha representing present situation and practices, with 11 ha arable
land, 3 ha grassland, 8 ha forest, and 3 ha of uncultivated land. The farm is oriented
towards milk production with a herd of 13 cattle including 5 cows. Cropping pattern:
50% barley and other cereals, 44% pasture for hay and grazing, 1% beet and 5% potato.
(Without Proiect Scenario).
(b)    A private farm of 25 ha as in (a) above, assuming changes in land use with crop rotation
extended to nine years including three years of cultivation and six years of pasture,
improved pasture management, improved feed conservation and an increase in herd size
to 21 cattle including 10 cows. It is conservatively assumed that the investments result in
a yield increase of 0.6 t/ha of barley, 4 tlha of potatoes, 2 tlha of pasture and an increase
in production of 200 kg of milk per cow. Cropping pattern: 25% barley, 65% pasture
hay, 5% legumes and 5% potato. (With Proiect Scenario).
(c)    A hypothetical 50 ha farm with the same management, land use, and cropping pattern as
proposed for (b) above, with a herd of 45 cattle including 20 cows.
(d)    A hyRothetical 100 ha farm with the same management, land use, and cropping pattern
as proposed for (b) above, with a herd of 88 cattle including 40 cows.



ANNEX 3
Page 3 of 37
8.     The transformation of a 25 ha dairy farm, from the present condition to a financially profitable
farm, requires: (a) periodic investments in drainage rehabilitation and maintenance including drainage
rehabilitation in the first year (EEK 8,200) and again after 9 years and annual maintenance costs over 15
years2; and (b) investments in advisory services for the promotion of improved pasture and livestock
management in the first years3 (EEK 2,500); (c) stable expansion/improvement and the acquisition of a
simple 2 cow bucket milking system (EEK 80,000).  Total investment requirements amount to an
estimated EEK 91,000 over a period of 4 years.
9.     The outlined changes in cropping pattern and the yield, increase feed supplies by over 90% from
35 to 68 tons of dry matter (Farm A to Farm B) and would nearly double the livestock carrying capacity
of a 25 ha farm. Milk production can be more than doubled from 16,000 liters to 34,000 liters per year
by reducing the culling rate of cows from 30% to 20%, increasing production by 200 kg per cow, and
discouraging prolonged retention of males in the herd. The increase in herd size from 5 to 10 cows also
contributes to a 55% increase in meat production, approximately from 1.5 to 2.3 ton liveweight per year.
10.    Production costs of milk per liter (excluding labor) decrease from 1.3 EEK/liter to 0.8 EEK/liter,
given the proportionally larger increase in livestock output. At the current farmgate price of 1.4 EEK/liter
in constant 1994 terms, the expected return on labor and capital increases six-fold, from 0.1 EEK/liter to
0.6 EEK/liter of milk produced and sold. Similarly, production costs decrease from 6.5 EEK/kg to 4
EEK/kg of beef production. At an average price of 7 EEK/kg (liveweight), the expected returns on labor
and capital would increase fivefold from 0.6 EEK/kg to 3 EEK/kg.
