ANNEX 3
Page 4 of 37
11.   Under the present situation (Farm A), the farm has a positive cash flow, but returns on family
-labor are negative, assuming an average farm wage of EEK 40 per day. The model therefore accurately
describes the situation of a large number of farms. After expanding herd size and investing in machinery,
buildings and improved pasture (Farm B), production costs of milk would decrease and returns on family
farm labor would increase to more than EEK 40 per work day. As farm and herd size increases (Farms
C and D), economies of scale further decrease the production costs per unit and consequently labor income
from the dairy farm would increase. The potential labor income from the improved farm models (Farms
B, C, D) is more than double the average rural wages in Estonia.
12.   Total farm income would increase from EEK 15,000 (without project) to EEK 41,000 (with
project) including forestry income of about 7,600 EEK/year by changing land use and introducing
improved pasture and livestock management. Even without reaching full employment of 2 family
members, total returns on labor and capital can be expected to reach more than EEK 1,700 per person per
month. Assuming a 10% return on invested capital, net returns on farm labor (including forestry) could
amount to 1,130 EEK/monthWperson, which is about the average per capita income in Estonia.
13.   Environmental impact. The increase in herd size will lead to an increase in the amount of manure
produced on the farrn which could increase non-point source pollution from agriculture if appropriate
mitigation measures are not in place. It will be necessary for the expanding farms to invest in appropriate
manure storage facilities and technology. In the model calculations, investment costs for appropriate
manure storage have been included in estimating the costs of expanding the stables. It is further assumed
that the farms will continue to use the existing technology for spreading manure. All new private
investment involving the construction of cattle sheds for more than 10 cows have been classified by
Estonian law as projects of local importance that have to undergo an environmental impact assessment
before the investment is cleared by the County Environment Department. The Government thereby tries
to assure that appropriate environmental mitigation measures are included. However, even after increasing
herd size, the assumed animal density in the model farms will be lower than in many European countries.
Farm Model Financial Analysis
14.   The financial rate of return (FRR) for these investments on a 25 ha dairy farm is estimated at 22%
and the net present value amounts to EEK 46,644. The FRR calculation is based on the following
assumptions: (a) all costs are in constant end 1994 prices; (b) benefits and costs are calculated for 15
years; and (c) quantifiable net returns are from incremental dairy and meat production. Recent data
published by MOA confirm the analysis and indicate that half of the private dairy farms in Estonia show
a profit in 1994. Dairy farm profitability improved noticeably since 1993, partly due to comparable on-
farm investments.
Farmers' Debt Service Capacity
15.   Farm income of a 25 ha dairy farm, excluding returns on family farm labor and debt service, are
estimated to be EEK 15,000 under present conditions. During the four year investment period, farm
income before financing would drop to EEK 12,000 in the first year of the investment, and then increase
to EEK 21,000 in the second year, EEK 35,000 in the third and stabilize at EEK 40,000 from the fourth
year onwards. The decline in income in the first year is due to an internal build-up of the herd which
prevents the sale of livestock during the first year.



ANNEX 3
Page 5 of 37
16.   The following calculation illustrates loan conditions and debt service obligations that guarantee
adequate cash incomes for the farmers during the investment period. A loan of EEK 92,000 with a ten
year maturity including a 2-year grace period during which interest is fully capitalized, a real interest rate
of 4% per annum (higher than the prevailing market rates), and debt service limited to no more than 40%
of farm income in any given year would ensure adequate farm income during the entire investment period.
Under these loan terms, farm income after financing of the 25 ha farm would increase from EEK 12,000
in the first year to about EEK 21,000 in the second and third year, EEK 24,000 from the fourth to the
ninth year, and EEK 40,000 after the loan has been fully repaid. Therefore, the estimated minimum
annual farm income of EEK 21,000 during the loan repayment period would in real terms exceed the
average rural income.
17.   The financial analysis shows that the proposed investments will have a large return and could
therefore be financed by the farmers. The analysis conservatively assumes a high level of necessary
complementary investments in private assets, even though many of the farms have already partly
purchased these assets. Therefore, on average, the expected investment in farm assets will be lower than
anticipated in the analysis. Even if farmers would not achieve the estimated expansion in herd size, the
agricultural advice would be used for efficiency improvements that do not require complementary
investments, and, part of the incremental crop production achieved after rehabilitating the drainage systems
that might not be used on the farms, could be sold to local markets.
Farm Model Economic Analysis
18.   Commodity and lnvut Prices. Economic farmgate prices of cereals are calculated using averages
of quoted CIF Rotterdam prices in 1995 of barley, rye and oats. For the purpose of economic analysis,
an average import parity farmgate price of EEK 1,101 per ton for cereals is derived by averaging farmgate
prices of barley, rye and oats. Considering that these cereals are used primarily as livestock feed grains,
this average farmgate price for cereals is projected by correlating its price movement with the Bank's
commodity price forecast of May 1995 for grain sorghum for years 1995 to 2005. The calculated
economic farmgate prices show only minor variations from the actual financial farmgate prices observed.
The average import parity farmgate prices of these feed grains are projected at EEK 1,055 per ton in 1995
up to the year 2000, and EEK 979 per ton in year 2005 and thereafter. Import parity prices have been
calculated for fertilizers and cereals. The economic import parity farmgate prices for N, P, and K
nutrients are derived from Urea, TSP and KCL prices respectively, based on the Bank's forecast of May
1995 for the years 1995 to 2005. Estonia is a net importer of these fertilizers. The farmgate import parity
prices of these nutrients in 1995, expressed in constant 1994 terms, are estimated to be EEK 5.5/kg for
N, EEK 4.5/kg for P, and EEK 3.0/kg for K. All other input and output prices have been converted using
a standard conversion factor (SCF) of 1.0, equating financial and economic prices because of the absence
of any trade restrictions.
19.   The financial farngate price for milk is also used in the economic analysis. The farmgate milk
price is not distorted because of Estonia's liberal and open trade and price policy. However, the costs of
processing milk are high in international comparison because of the inefficient Estonian dairy processors.
With the improvement of competition among milk processors, processing costs are expected to decrease
in the future. Cost savings in processing could translate into higher farmgate milk prices and consequently
increase the financial and economic rate of return of the described investments.
20.   Methodology. The economic analysis of the farm models uses the same prices and assumptions



ANNEX 3
Page 6 of 37
as in the financial farm model with one major change. The investment costs for drainage rehabilitation
and advisory services have been increased to reflect the full investment costs of these investments to the
economy and include both the farmers and the Government's proportion In the economic analysis costs
of fertilizer have been increased to reflect import parity prices at the farmgate.
21.    The with Project model shows a positive Net Present Value (NPV) of 30,874 and an economic
rate of return (ERR) of 16%. The ERR and NPV are lower than in the financial model reflecting the full
investment costs for drainage rehabilitation and agricultural advisory services to the economy.
Sensitivity Analysis
22.    Overall, the ERR is robust to significant changes in costs and benefits: benefits would need to fall
by 24% or costs increase by 31% for the ERR to fall to 10% (i.e. with a net present value of zero). If
the benefits are delayed by two years, the ERR would drop to 11%. If simultaneously incremental costs
increase by 20% and incremental benefits drop by 10%, the ERR would drop to 10%. In addition, a risk
analysis for key assumptions, including (i) inadequate rehabilitation and maintenance of the drainage
systems beyond the project period, (ii) shortfalls in projected yield or production increases, (iii) change
of key agricultural output prices is presented below.
23.   Inadequate rehabilitation and maintenance of the drainage system. Farmers would have to fully
finance the rehabilitation of the drainage system after about nine years to increase the life of the drainage
system to around 60 years. If farmers fail to rehabilitate the drainage system, and the incremental benefits
would drop to zero after year 10, then the ERR would drop to 10%. Therefore the Project has emphasized
that farmers are organized in Land and Water Associations (LWAs), and assume full responsibility for part
of the investment costs, and all maintenance as well as future rehabilitation costs. In addition, the benefits
of maintaining the drainage system are very high.
24.    Shortfalls in projected crop yield.  The model reacts sensitive to a shortfall in projected crop
yields. The viability of the livestock farms is jeopardized when crop and pasture yields cannot be
sustained at certain levels, a case when drainage systems are not rehabilitated and maintained. If crop
yields are 10% lower than assumed, the ERR of the economic farm model would fall to 10%. If yields
drop below the calculated level, the farm would then have to substitute the cheaper on-farm produced
forage crops with more expensive cereals purchased on the markets. The sensitivity analysis underlines
the importance of drainage rehabilitation being a pre-requisite to achieve and sustain the projected yields
for crops and pasture. If maintenance of the drainage system is neglected over several years, yields may
drop below the threshold yields resulting in cost of milk production that are higher than the current
farmgate milk price. However, the estimates of incremental crop yields and incremental milk production
are extremely conservative. For instance, barley yields have been projected to increase by only 0.6 t/ha
to around 2.2 t/ha (with Project). The yields used in the "with Project" scenario are comparable to average
Estonian barley yields that have fluctuated between 1.6 and 2.2 t/ha in the last two years whereas yields
on well drained soils are often higher than 3 t/ha.
25.   Change of key aericultural output prices. The model reacts less sensitive to changes of output
prices. If the milk price drops by 20%, the ERR would fall to 9%. However, a drop of the milk price
by 20% is unlikely, because the farmgate price is presently extremely low. Farmgate prices for milk are
likely to increase, because the processing costs of Estonian dairy processing plants are expected to decline
over the next years as the processors increase their productivity and reduce existing overcapacities.



ANNEX 3
Page 7 of 37
C. ANALYSIS OF PROJECT BENEFITS BY COMPONENT
26.   The economic analysis provides a discussion of the Project benefits for each component.
However, as illustrated in the farm model analysis, many of the benefits of the component are interrelated
and can not be analyzed, or achieved, in isolation.
27.    Country and Agricultural Strategv. The Project follows the GOE's and the Bank's strategy to
transform the rural sector, through supporting private agricultural and rural initiatives, into an
economically viable and competitive market-based system. The agricultural strategy in the past and the
near future aims at maintaining an open competitive agricultural sector based on private ownership of land,
liberal agricultural trade policies, market determined agricultural prices, and limited budget transfers to
the agricultural and rural sector. The liberal agricultural policy of Estonia to date can be described as
exceptional within the former Soviet Union because prices and market distortions have been almost
completely avoided. The recently completed Agricultural Sector Review for Estonia notes that low wages,
low land costs, and the existing stock of skills and infrastructure provide Estonia with a competitive
potential, primarily in dairy and related livestock production. The strategy is therefore aimed at increasing
the efficiency and profitability of agriculture in Estonia and not primarily at increasing agricultural
production or exports. Poverty and environmental issues related to the Project are discussed in separate
sections in the SAR.4
28.   Public versus Private Sector Investment. Following the outlined strategy, the Project selectively
provides investments to improve existing key infrastructure in target areas (farm drainage rehabilitation),
strengthen the public and support the development of a private advisory services, and enhance food quality
control functions. The Project focuses on investments that initiate a gradual transfer of Govemment
functions to the private sector (advisory services, drainage maintenance), or improve core Government
functions (food quality control, public advisory services, environmental controls in agriculture).
Land Reform
29.    Benefits. The Project would support the development of a rural land market by accelerating the
registration of rural land in the cadastre and title book. These measures would (i) facilitate the access
of farmers to long-term credit because commercial banks would be more inclined to accept land with a
secure, transferable and mortgageable title as collateral, and (ii) increase the movement of the production
factor land through sale or lease to efficient agricultural producers, thereby increasing agricultural
production and incomes.
30.    Land with a secure and registered title could be used as an immoveable and mortgageable
collateral to obtain a long-term loan from a commercial bank. Experience shows that long-term mortgage
interest rates are generally lower than long-term interest rates for other loans not using "immoveable" land
or buildings as collateral. At present, it is difficult to obtain long-term loans as the vast majority of all
loans are for a maturity of less than 3 years. In addition, commercial banks in Estonia consider any land
or building without a registration in the title book as moveable collateral and subsequently charge long-
term interest rates of about 20 - 25%. It can be assumed that a mortgage banking system would provide
farmers with access to long-term loan funds with lower than present interest rates. Mortgage banking is
only in the initial stages of development but it is likely that it would develop rapidly, if land and buildings
4 For poverty analysis and for the environmental analysis compare Staff Appraisal Report, Chapter 2.



ANNEX 3
Page 8 of 37
are registered in the cadastre and title book.
31.    Cost Effectiveness Analysis. The benefits of land surveys and title registration could be assessed
as the difference between the interest rate for a long-term mortgage loan which is typically lower than the
interest rate of a loan of the same maturity for a different purpose. A simple cost-effectiveness analysis
shows that a reduction of the interest rate for a loan of EEK 100,000 (10 years maturity, no grace period,
constant principal repayment) by about 2% would be sufficient to cover the estimated costs of EEK-9,600
for land surveys of an average farm of about 25 ha. Experience in developed economies demonstrates
that the differential in interest rates for long-term loans between mortgage loans and other loans is
substantially larger than 2%. The Project would therefore have substantial financial and economic benefits
for the economy by supporting the development of mortgage banking. This analysis is based on the
assumptions outlined in Table 2.
Table 2 Cost-Effectiveness Analysis for Land Reform

32.    Cost Recovery. The GOE plans to recover the costs for the land surveys from the property
owners. The GOE is developing incentives for owners to finance the land survey and to register their land
in the cadastre and title book. The land surveys would be conducted by around 200 private surveying
companies operating in Estonia.
Farm Drainage Rehabilitation
33.    Back2round.  Artificially drained farmland has been an important feature of the Estonian
agriculture, representing some 739,000 ha or 66% of the 1.12 million of amble land. Natural conditions
for agriculture are far from optimal, primarily due to the short growing season and the generally poor soil
fertility. The height of the growing season coincides with the period of highest rainfall. Although
precipitation is fairly uniform between 570 and 720 mm/year, 65% of this rainfall occurs during the 5-
5      At present, there is no land market so that the land value is assumed at 1/6 of the average price of
agricultural land in the US.



ANNEX 3
Page 9 of 37
month period from May to September with peaks in July and August. Plant growth and harvesting on
lands that have inadequate natural drainage are adversely affected by these conditions.  The growing
season on well drained fields is increased by about a month which results in earlier planting and
subsequent higher yields.
34.    The poor soil conditions and the short growing season are the major cause of the relatively low
crop yields in Estonia. Rehabilitation and good maintenance of drainage systems are key to the increase
the productivity of dairy farms. The farm improvements (reduction of livestock feed production costs and
increase in herd outputs) described in the farm model analysis hinge on changes in land use, cropping
patterns, improvement in pasture and livestock management, as well as introduction of improved grass and
legume species in pastures. Higher yields depend on a grassland technology utilizing nitrogen fixing
rhizobium activities of legumes. However, excessive soil moisture and poor soil aeration would inhibit
the growth of the high yielding legumes and consequently lead to nitrogen deficient pastures which would
naturally be overgrown with less productive grass and weeds.
35.    Routine maintenance of farm drainage systems has been reduced substantially since 1989 and has
eroded land productivity over time. Without regular maintenance and periodic rehabilitation, drainage
efficiency and consequently crop yields will continue to decline. If crop yields cannot be sustained above
the described yield threshold that guarantees positive returns, crop lands would be forced out of
production. Moreover, if maintenance is deferred too long, these drainage systems can no longer be
maintained or rehabilitated, and reconstruction would be prohibitive in costs and economically
questionable.
36.    Drainage Rehabilitation Investments. The Project therefore plans to rehabilitate about 60,000 ha
of the most fertile drained agricultural land in about 60 separate locations. Areas for drainage
rehabilitation works will be selected according to site selection criteria that include: (a) environmental
screening; (b) soil fertility;6 (c) land use intensity; (d) condition of the drainage systems; (e) technical
feasibility and designs; (f) investment cost ceiling; (g) status of land tenure; (h) farner's participation
including various pre-conditions for local LWAs; and (i) location of markets.
37.    Rehabilitation investments, including design and supervision, have been limited to expenditures
of not more than EEK 1,500 per ha (in 1995 financial terms). This limitation ensures that rehabilitation
investments are targeted to areas with low rehabilitation costs. In the future, routine maintenance and
punctual rehabilitation will be required to sustain the expected benefits from drainage rehabilitation
investments. While routine maintenance of the systems is required annually, punctual rehabilitation of
the main and collector drains, and repair of the subsurface drain outlets should be carried out every nine
to twelve years, in order to increase the life of the drainage system to 60 years.
38.    Large areas of substandard drained agricultural land will continue to be forced out of production
because it is no longer financially viable to maintain these systems. Current estimates show that without
any intervention about 144,000 ha' of drainage systems would permanently be forced out production, as
maintenance and rehabilitation programs fall behind schedule and reconstruction proves to be financially
6 Drainage systems should have soils of good quality (e.g. more than 40 soil points).
7  Working Paper 4, Drainage Sub-Sector Review and Project preparation, Estonia Agriculture and Forestry
Project, Food and Agriculture Organization (1994).



ANNEX 3
Page 10 of 37
inviable. Without the project, the area of adequately drained land is expected to decline from 591,000
-ha in 1994 to 447,000 ha in 1997. In addition, the contraction in livestock population, as caused by the
collapse of the Soviet markets and increasing trade barriers imposed by Russia, had accelerated the rate
at which substandard lands were forced out of production. Consequently, the expected loss in livestock
feed production would outweigh the incremental increases in crop production from the Project. The
incremental feed production from the Project, if translated into livestock production, corresponds to about
2% of Estonia's total milk production and less than 1% of the beef production in 1993.
39.   By rehabilitating only those parts of the drainage system that have the highest potential for
agricultural production, the drainage investments would intensify and increase the efficiency of livestock
fodder production and improve the competitiveness of livestock and dairy farms in the Project areas. It
can therefore be assumed that a small part of the reduction of agricultural lands can be attributed to the
intensification of the production in the Project area.
40.   Cost Recovery. GOE plans to privatize the ownership, operations, and maintenance of all farm
drainage systems by handing over these systems to LWAs after completion of the drainage rehabilitation
works. Responsibilities of LWAs are specified in the Amelioration Law (May 1994) and membership is
compulsory for farmers in the drainage area. GOE has determined that each LWA must agree to
contribute (in cash or in kind) 20% of the cost of the rehabilitation works, the remaining 80% would be
covered by GOE with budget and Project loan funds. In return, GOE would, post-rehabilitation, no longer
accept any further financial responsibility for these on-farm drainage systems. The cost sharing formula
is a legal decision by GOE based on a rough analysis of what farmers' are prepared to contribute in labor
towards the rehabilitation of the drainage system. Each LWA will have full ownership of its area-specific
drainage system and bear full O&M costs. The LWAs are expected to finance a second round
rehabilitation of the drainage systems after 9 - 12 years. Future GOE expenditures for drainage system
maintenance would be reduced by more than the actual contribution to the rehabilitation costs. However,
the ABs will continue to be responsible for operations and maintenance of rivers and main canals outside
the drainage areas, as the main canals often serve other users such as industries and municipalities. Cost
recovery options for maintenance of rivers and main canals are being considered, including the possibility
of: (a) direct charges to landowners; and (b) transferring the financial responsibility of this maintenance
to the respective municipalities which would then recover these costs through local taxes.
41.   Crop Response to Drainage. Rehabilitation and good maintenance of drainage systems in the
project area are expected to increase the growing season by one month, thereby contributing to incremental
yields of 0.6 ton/ha for cereals (primarily barley, oats and rye), 0.9 ton/ha in hay equivalent for grassland,
and 4 ton/ha for potato. These yield increments are considered conservative, and based on the actual
difference between crop yields on lands with good drainage and yields on poorly drained lands. The
cropping pattern in the project area is estimated to consist of 30% cereals, 65% pasture for silage and hay,
and 5% potato on average. Most of the cereal production in Estonia is used for livestock feed and hay
is a major input for dairy production.
42.   Benefits. At full development, incremental production of feed grains in the project area of 60,000
ha (Base Case can be expected to reach 10,800 tons of feed grains, 35,100 tons of hay and 12,000 tons
of potato per year. The incremental crop production, when translated into dry matter (about 31,600 tons)
and nutritional values, is equivalent to the amount of feed required for producing 15 million liters of milk
and 500 tons of beef (carcass weight). These equivalents in milk and meat production would represent
2% of the country's total milk production in 1993 of 750,000 tons and less than 1% of the beef production
in 1993 of 115,000 tons (carcass weight). In addition, the organization of farmers in LWAs and the



ANNEX 3
Page 11 of 37
involvement of the farmers in the design of the drainage system could also result in new community
services provided by these LWAs, such as input supplies, machinery pools, marketing and possibly credit.
The drainage rehabilitation investments would directly benefit small local contractors carrying out the civil
works and would also stimulate the development of other rural support services to agriculture in the rural
areas.
43.    An Economic Rate of Return (ERR) for the farm drainage component has been calculated
separately. The ERR for the farn drainage component is estimated at 23% and the net present value
(NPV) of the projected net benefits amounts to EEK 26 million. The analysis is based on the economic
costs for the rehabilitation and maintenance of the drainage system over 15 years and includes the system
design, works supervision, and LWA establishment costs as well as a second rehabilitation after 9 - 12
years. Drainage system efficiency has been conservatively estimated at 90%. The incremental benefits
are valued in terms of incremental production of hay, feed grains and potatoes. The benefits of
rehabilitating the existing system are high due to substantial yield increases triggered by relatively minor
costs for repairs and flushing of the system.
44.   However, even though the incremental production of feed grain, hay and potatoes could be valued
at market prices, the majority of the incremental production are only intermediate goods used for livestock
production. As shown in the farm model analysis, the farm drainage rehabilitation investments are a
prerequisite for complementary farm improvement investments aimed at increasing herd size, farm
production, and farm income. The combined ERR of investments in drainage rehabilitation, agricultural
advisory services and privately financed complementary investments is consequently lower than only
looking at farm drainage rehabilitation separately. The farm model analysis therefore provides a realistic
approximation of Project benefits for the drainage rehabilitation and agricultural advisory services
components.
Agricultural Advisory Services
45.   Benefits. Extension plays a key role in the dissemination of information on new technologies, or
improved organizational and management practices. Research results have confirmed that extension's
impact on agriculture showed "a significant and positive external effect" in 33 analyzed cases. A
calculation of net economic rates of return in eight studies shows highly positive results in eight cases,
ranging from 14% to nearly 500% (Birkhaeuser, Evenson, and Feder, 1988).
46.   Extension will likely have a substantial impact on the transformation of the Estonian farming
sector to a market oriented production system. However, the impact of extension during the transition
period is extremely difficult to quantify in isolation, due to the paucity of data, the lack of experience with
similar situations, and in many cases the necessity to complement the advice with actual on-farm
investments. Three sets of benefits can be expected: (a) benefits related to agricultural production; (b)
environmental benefits; and (c) societal benefits.
47.   Benefits related to (a) agricultural production in Estonia can be expected especially through
increased production (higher yields), improved product quality (higher milk quality), and improved on-
farm processing (improved storage of livestock feed). In addition, extension can help to reduce production
costs through new technologies but also through promoting cooperation between farmers to jointly use
farm machinery. (b) Environmental benefits can be expected through the promotion of improved and safe
storage and application of manure, enhancement of soil fertility and reduction of agricultural run-off
through drainage. Especially in the drainage rehabilitation area, extension service will play an important



ANNEX 3
Page 12 of 37
role in providing information of reducing potential negative impacts of drainage rehabilitation.  (c)
Societal benefits can be expected through provision of advice on alternative employment opportunities in
the rural areas, easing the transition out of agriculture, but also reducing the amount of out-migration from
rural areas. Evidence from a donor-financed extension project suggests that already minor improvements
of the maintenance of the milking equipment result in drastic quality improvements. These quality
improvements result in substantial benefits for the farmers because of the large difference in payment for
low quality and high quality milk.
48.   Public versus private advisory services. The Project assists the establishment of a new advisory
system in Estonia consisting of a public and a private extension service. This requires a redefinition of
the Government's role in extension. The development of a public and private advisory service is based
on a distinction between advice that has public good characteristics and advice that is a private good. The
rationale is that the public sector will continue to pay for, but not necessarily implement, extension in
some areas, but that the farmers will be expected to pay for advice that benefits them directly.  In
addition, some farmer's will have to leave agriculture, and information on alternative income provided
by the public advisory service can speed up that process, thereby enhancing the profitability of the
remaining farmers.
49.   Cost recovery. The proposed support for Estonian advisory services is important, but limited, both
in time and in scope. The Private Advisory Services Development Fund requires only an initial
investment, because the farmers are expected to take over those programs over time. The fund will give
non-transferable grants to farmers to pay for part of the advisory services of private advisors. Over an
eight year period the Government contribution to the private advisory service will be gradually phased out.
In addition, the Government will continue to fund a small public extension service, administered by the
Farmer's Union, and equally supported under the Project. The public extension service will provide more
technical advice of a general nature. The development of the private agricultural advisory services will
reduce the number of county-level field staff (from 6-7 per county to about 3-4) employed by the
Government. The Government will therefore reduce its active involvement in extension to a more strategic
and supervisory role. This will over time substantially reduce GOE's recurrent expenditures

Food Quality Control and Veterinary Services
50.    Benefits. The Project would improve one laboratory of the State Veterinary Department to
improve health and quality standards in order to protect domestic and foreign consumers, and increase
export potential of agricultural products. The Project investments are aimed at enhancing and monitoring
the quality of food and livestock products in accordance with EU regulations. If Estonian livestock
products meet EU standards, the tariffs for selected livestock exports to the EU would be reduced by 60%
(US $5 - 6 million over the next three years). These investments, in the order of US $1.2 million, would
initiate production quality improvements at the farm level and in the food processing industries. However,
private sector investments in production facilities at the farm level and at the agro-processing level would
have to complement the Project investments to increase the quality of food and livestock products.
51.    Public versus Private Investment and Cost Recovery. The Project supports the initial phase of a
broader program to improve Estonia's food quality at the production, processing and marketing level. Part
of the future control functions would be undertaken by private laboratories, but some of these functions
would remain in Government hands. It is expected that the Veterinary Laboratory would charge for the
tests to partially recover costs.

Land Use Management
52.    This component is aimed at strengthening environmental controls in agriculture. It consists of a
wetlands management strategy plan to develop a national strategy for wetlands development and
protection. In addition, the Ministry of Environment receives considerable support to develop and enforce
environmental regulations and monitor the impact of the drainage rehabilitation investments on the
environment.   To analyze the impact of future drainage investments, the Project supports a sectoral
environmental drainage study which analyzes the future options for drained lands.
53.    The agricultural strategy of the Government is aimed at increasing the profitability of the farming
sector through intensification of the production. Present and future yield and input levels of agricultural
production are and will likely be low compared to European agriculture because of the short summers
which do not allow extremely intensive production. The Ministry of Environment actively participates
in developing the framework for future agricultural production at an early stage to ensure adequate
environmental controls and the development of an environmentally sustainable agriculture in Estonia. This
is especially important for the drainage rehabilitation investments financed by the Project.

ANNEX 4
ESTONIA
AGRICULTURE PROJECT
ENVIRONMENTAL REVIEW
INTRODUCTION
1.           Introduction. The proposed loan to the agricultural sector in Estonia has been developed
to support economic transformation in rural areas. The loan package includes investments in land reform,
farm drainage rehabilitation, land management studies, agricultural extension and food quality control
laboratory. The Staff Appraisal Report (SAR) describes the proposed project framework, objectives, and
component in detail while this annex focuses specifically on the environmental review process, mitigation
and monitoring proposed for the project. A draft overview of environmental issues in Estonia is being
prepared by the World Bank. In addition, the World Bank is preparing a "Biodiversity Strategy" which
is financed through the Global Environmental Facility (GEF).
ENVIRONMENTAL FRAMEWORK
2.           Estonian Environmental Strategy. The cornerstone of Estonian environmental policy is
sustainable development which considers the basic needs of the present and the future. Estonian
environmental policy encourages a shift from previous production based actions to use of an ecologically
based systemic approach to balance human needs with ecologically sustainable practices (Keskkond, 1993).
The proposed Agriculture Project must be consistent with Estonian enviromnental goals by including
concerns for the sustainable use of natural resources into plans for development and restructuring of the
rural sector. This environmental review is a mechanism to assure that all relevant environmental
concerns are addressed in the project plans, recommended mitigation, and the project monitoring
program.
3.           Regulatory Framework. Estonia Governmental Regulation No. 314, adopted in November
1992, describes procedures for Environmental Impact Assessment. Under the Estonian system, a project
may be categorized as a project of State Importance, Regional Importance, or one which does not require
an environmental assessment. Projects of state importance are typically larger in scale with potential
impacts crossing county borders. Environmental assessments and reviews for state importance projects
are conducted by the Ministry of the Environment. Projects of regional importance are typically smaller
in size, located within the boundaries of a particular county, and environmental reviews and assessments
are conducted at the County Environmental Departments.
4.           Environmental review is required for all World Bank loans and credits as described in
the World Bank Operational Directive 4.01, "Environmental Assessment."  The purpose of the
environmental review process is:
"to ensure that development options under consideration are environmentally sound and
sustainable and that any environmental consequences are recognized early and taken into
consideration in project design."



Estonia Agriculture Project: Environmental Review                             ANNEX 4
Page 2 of 8
5.           The World Bank's environmental review procedures require the classification of projects
into one of three categories (A,B,C) that consider the nature, magnitude, and sensitivity of environmental
issues. Classification of a project is achieved through a screening process beginning at initial project
identification and continuing through project appraisal as more information becomes available. A detailed
Environmental Assessment (EA) document is normally required for category A projects that have
potentially diverse and significant environmental impacts. Category B projects undergo a more limited
environmental analysis since the potential environmental impacts are less severe. A project would be
classified into category C during environmental screening if it had minimal to no environmental impacts.
6.           Environmental Screening. Table 1 (to this annex) presents results of environmental
screening by the Estonian Ministry of the Environment and the World Bank for the Project. World Bank
guidelines recommend that the most stringent classification of any individual project component shall
determine the overall project classification. Both World Bank and Estonian environmental review
procedures were used to classify project components. The Farm Drainage Rehabilitation project
component has been classified as category B under the World Bank guidelines. This component will
require further screening for specific investments during the course of project implementation. Therefore,
the environmental review focuses on the proposed screening and impact analysis process.
7.           Environmental screening under the Estonian Environmental Impact guidelines classifies
the Wetlands Management Strategy Plan as of state importance. This study is considered strategically
important to Estonia and thus would benefit from public participation and full consideration of
environmental issues early in the planning process. All other project components were classified under
World Bank guidelines as category C, or under Estonian guidelines as not requiring an environmental
assessment or review. It is recognized, however that actions taken based on technical assistance, studies,
and strategies may have environmental impacts and quite often involve issues with a direct influence on
the environment.  The World Bank encourages and supports the Estonian decision to conduct
environmental assessments on technical assistance, studies, and strategies of regional or state importance.
Environmental reviews performed early in the process reduce the need for more detailed reviews further
downstream and provide a much better framework for future planning decisions.
8.           Consultation Process. The Project is being designed in collaboration with the Ministry
of Environment which is one of the implementing agencies besides the Ministry of Agriculture and the
Ministry of Finance. The Environmental Assessment Division of the Ministry of the Environment is
responsible for oversight of the preparation of the environmental review activities of the Project. The
Water Department of the Ministry of Environment is responsible for all environmental regulations and
screening procedures regarding the farm drainage rehabilitation component.  A number of non-
governmental organizations have been consulted during the Project preparation process, especially
regarding the farm drainage rehabilitation and the rural finance component. Mission members met with
the Stockholm Environment Institute - Tallinn which has been contracted to prepare supplementary
information and a training course on the environmental screening criteria for commercial banks using the
World Bank Refinancing Facility. Representatives of the Estonia Fund for Nature (Westonia Project)
have provided written comments during preparation which are included in the background working
papers. In addition, mission members met with the Estonian Farmers Union, staff of the University of
Tartu, and the Estonia Academy of Sciences, Institute of Technology. Representatives of the Matsalu
State Nature Reserve have been consulted concerning the design of the environmental screening criteria
for farm drainage rehabilitation in the catchment area of the Reserve, given the ecological importance of
this area.



Estonia Agriculture Project: Environmental Review                             ANNEX 4
Page 3 of 8
FARM DRAINAGE REHABILITATION
9.           Farm drainage rehabilitation investments. Investments are proposed to rehabilitate and
maintain selected drainage channels and supporting infrastructure in critical farming areas to prevent
permanent damage to existing farm drainage systems. Maintenance of fann drainage infrastructure has
been neglected during the previous years due to a lack of funding resulting in clogging of subsurface
pipes and reduction in channel conveyance capacity. Investments under this loan are very limited, and
will be selected using evaluation criteria to maximize improvements to the most productive agricultural
lands and to minimize environmental damage. Land amelioration (new drainage) will be limited to
rehabilitation of existing works and will not involve investments in new drainage schemes, nor include
polders where submerged lands are now drained for agricultural purposes, or support the drainage of
"wet forests ".
10.         Proposed drainage investments during the investment period include 200 kilometers (km)
of rehabilitation and 1250 km of maintenance of the main drainage channels; 1500 km of rehabilitation
and 3400 km of maintenance of collector (secondary) drainage channels; and pipe cleanout (flushing
sediment) at approximately 15,000 outlets in both main and collector drainage channels during
rehabilitation. Drainage investments are estimated to support approximately 60,000 hectares of land.
Environmental reviews will be conducted, if necessary, following Estonian and World Bank procedures.
However, drainage investment site selection with use agreed screening criteria to minimize potential
damage to the environment. Consultations have been undertaken in preparation of the proposed
Project with representatives of the Matsalu State Nature Reserve to review their views on potential
activities in this catchment area.
11.          Environmental screening and monitoring. The Ministry of Enviromnent has devised a
two step approach to ensure that the drainage rehabilitation investments will be undertaken in a way to
ensure minimal potential damage to the environment and public concerns are'adequately addressed. The
environmental screening and monitoring procedures are based on the Water Law (adopted May 1994) that
regulates water protection matters, and the Law on Environmental Protection of Shores and Banks
(adopted in 1995) that regulates environmental protective measures for natural streams and drains. The
two step environmental screening approach consists of: (a) environmental screening during site selection
and (b) environmental guidelines for the design of drainage rehabilitation schemes:
12.          Environmental screening during the site selection phase
Screening criteria will be used to exclude certain areas as potential project sites to
minimize environmental damage.  These have been drafted by MOE, Dept. of
Environmental Impact Assessment. This includes: (a) work will not be performed in
areas where fish and wildlife habitat is protected by Estonian environmental laws or
rules; (b) wetlands and estuaries with important wildlife or fishery values will be avoided;
(c) important native grasslands or forest lands of special significance will be avoided; (d)
national forest lands will be avoided until a forestry strategy is completed; and (e) polders
will be avoided until special site-specific environmental studies have been prepared.
Each site proposed by the Amelioration Units will be assessed by a local environmental
consultant (financed through project funds) who prepares an EIA report. This report will
be reviewed by the County Environment Department.



Estonia Agriculture Project: Environmental Review                                 ANNEX 4
Page 4 of 8
-      Water quality of the proposed drainage sites will be analyzed' to determine limits for
nutrient effluent in the drain water after rehabilitation.
-      The local Anelioration Bureau and the County Enviromnent Department would then
inform the public of the proposed project rehabilitation site and give the public (incl.
NGO's) a possibility to comment on the suitability of the proposed site for drainage
rehabilitation .
-      The County Environment Department then decides if the site is acceptable for drainage
rehabilitation and makes a recommendation to MOE and MOA.
-      In the case of highly sensitive areas (for example the catchment area of the Matsalu State
Nature Reserve) a final decision will be taken by MOE.
13.          Environmental screening during the design phase.
-      Environmental Guidelines for design of drainage rehabilitation works will be issued by
MOE. They cover: size of drainage units, regulations for dredging and straightening of
inlets, use of buffer areas or water protection belts, construction of sedimentation ponds,
and, in certain cases, long-term use of agricultural inputs.
-      'County Environment Departments will receive design plans for drainage rehabilitation
for approval and make recommendations on necessary mitigation measures. In critical
cases final r ecommendations will be issued by MOE.
14.          Different and stricter guidelines are proposed for all areas that are under enviromnental
protection, which includes ecologically sensitive areas like the catchment area of the Matsalu State Nature
Reserve. The screening criteria listed above are intentionally stringent to minimize investments in project
sites that require detailed environmental reviews during the early project stages.
15.          Maintenance of open drain channels will be carried out using techniques to minimize
environmental damages such as: (i) limiting excavation to design cross-sectional lines when channel
cleanouts are carried out; (ii) having equipment work from one side of the channel whenever possible;
(iii) consideration of sediment control by minimizing damage to vegetation on channel side slopes when
possible, and (iv) installing sediment traps when needed.
16.          Major Findings.  Potential environmental impacts from drainage rehabilitation and
maintenance include increased sedimentation, loss of critical wetland areas, groundwater table impacts,
loss of wildlife habitats, and downstream impacts to sensitive or protected environments. Mitigation
measures to minimize or prevent these impacts are available and shall be included in the project design.
Some areas of Estonia have large beaver populations that have historically caused damage to drainage
infrastructure. Mitigation against beaver damage may be required for proposed investments near large
beaver populations.
Laboratory equipment will be purchased by MOE using Project funds.



Estonia Agriculture Project: Environmental Review                             ANNEX 4
Page 5 of 8
17.          Proposed Mitigation Plan. The outlined environmental criteria for drainage rehabilitation
will ensure minimal potential damage to the environment and apply to all future drainage rehabilitation
works (above a certain minimum size). e.g., not only to the project financed rehabilitation works. MOE
plans to issue the environmental guidelines and the consultation process as a Ministerial Decree or a
Governmental Decree. Mitigation measures included in the overall project design are the use of initial
environmental screening criteria for investment site selection; and recommended environmentally sensitive
practices for implementation of drainage improvements. Site specific mitigation measures for investments
will be approved by the Ministry of the Environment county or state environmental impact assessment
staff on a case-by-case basis. Proposed technical assistance for environmental studies and drainage
research and curriculum revisions will provide a complementary infornation source for the development
and recommendation of mitigation measures. Research and studies would emphasize adaptive and applied
field methods to reduce maintenance costs, improve the sustainability of drainage systems, and promote
environmental protection.
18.          All proposed drainage investments shall be consistent with local land use management
plans. The Estonian Act on Land Cadastre passed in October, 1994 and effective January, 1995 describes
procedures for updating and establishing land use management plans. Revised land use management plans
should consider the importance of the environment, economic changes in the municipality, and desirable
social and economic projections
19.          Environmental Monitoring Plan. World Bank monitoring of the drainage investments
would be performed during regularly scheduled supervision missions. The Ministry of the Environment
would screen each proposed drainage investment plan receiving funding under this loan for environmental
impact assessment requirements. The Project Implementation Unit (PIU) would maintain records of each
letter from the Ministry of the Environment granting clearance of site specific investments. Proposed
investments that require an environmental assessment under the Estonian guidelines would be reviewed
by World Bank environmental staff for consistency and compliance with World Bank procedures.
ENVIRONMENTAL BENEFITS
20.          Environmental Benefits. Environmental benefits will be provided by several technical
assistance project components. A reorientation of drainage research and educational curriculum will
emphasize adaptive and field methods to reduce maintenance costs, improve sustainability of drainage
systems, and promote environmental protection. Environmental studies targeted at drainage investments
during the loan period and the future will assure that investments are made in an environmentally
sensitive manner. The agricultural training and field extension services provide an opportunity to
establish more sustainable farming practices than have been used in the past. Extension services would
also address environmental concerns related to farming practices through input provided by local county
environmental staff.
21.          The Wetlands Management Strategy Plan will provide a framework for future
management of these natural resources in a sustainable manner. The proposed Wetlands Management
Strategy Plan would complement the ongoing "Wetstonia Project" and develop a system for valuing
wetlands considering physical, hydrological, and biological criteria, as well as conservation and
development goals. The strategy will provide guidelines and a framework for rehabilitation of peat-mined
areas and wetland restoration projects.



Estonia Agriculture Project: Environmental Review                            ANNEX 4
Page 6 of 8
CONCLUSION
22.          Conclusion. No major significant adverse environmental impacts are anticipated from
this project. The component that was classified as category B and will require additional environmental
screening for specific investments as they are proposed during the investment period. Therefore, this
environrental review has focused on the procedures in place to ensure that investments are
environmentally sound and sustainable. The project will be regularly monitored for environmental issues
by the Ministry of Environment. The World Bank will be kept informed by MOE of any drainage
investments that require environmental assessments and will review environmental documentation by the
PIU on supervision missions.

ANNEX 5
ESTONIA

Terms of Reference for Project Management
Inter-Ministerial Project Steering Committee
A.   Objective.
The Inter-Ministerial Project Steering Committee (PSC) is established under the auspices
of the Project to meet regularly on a monthly basis to ensure that all project components
and sub-components are implemented in accordance with the overall objectives of the
project. The day-to-day tasks of the PSC wil be executed by two Joint Project Directors
(one each from the Ministries of Agriculture and Environment respectively).
B.   Tasks
The key tasks of the PSC would inclide the following:
(a)   Facilitate inter-agency national and country level coordination on project related
issues. As an example, the proposed sites for the drainage rehabilitation program
and LWA development will: (i) be included among the priorities of the ENLB's
country Land Board's efforts in speeding up cadastral land surveys and
registration.; (ii) receive timely attention from the MOE for environmental
screening at the county level, and final approval at the national level; (iii) become
specific targets for agricultural extension activities as demand arises out of the
establishment of the LWAs; and (iv) receive complementary investments in rural
infrastructure such as electricity, telephones, roads improvement and others as
appropriate in the context of Government's Public Investment Program.
(b)   Facilitate coordination, in the context of the project, with bilateral donors, EU
Phare, the World Bank and the Government, and assure that financial resources
required from these sources are made available to the implementing agencies in
a timely manner.
(c)   Specifically, assure that budgetary resources from the Government's Public
Investment Program provide adequate levels of funding for the various project
components.



ANNEX 5
Page 2 of 13
(d)   Regularly review project expenditures and costs to ensure that they are consistent
with the objectives of the project, and within justifiable limits.
(e)   Periodically review the overall status of project implementation, and provide
guidance to resolve major issues that impede the progress of the project.
(f)   Review the compliance of the project with the requirements of the Project
Financiers, including those of the World Bank that cover procurement, accounting
and audit, disbursement, and other covenants of the loan agreement.
(g)   Regularly review the work of the Project Implementation Unit, and as necessary,
recommend ways for improving its efficiency and effectiveness.
(h)   Serve as a forum for cross-fertilization of innovative ideas with the aim of
improving project delivery and targeting, and on certain issues for consulting with
a broader constituency within the Government.
(i)   Ensure that all implementing agencies and key stakeholders are informed of each
other's activities and concerns, and organize follow-up discussion to focus on
major issues affecting the project.
(j)   Ensure that the PIU and the associated departments and boards in the Ministries
adequately meet the requirements of reporting to the World Bank.
B.   Membership
The Inter-Ministerial Project Steering Committee will be chaired by the Chancellor of
the Ministry of Agriculture:
Chairperson:       *     Chancellor of the Ministry of Agriculture
Members:           *     Joint Directors from MOA and MOE
*     Vice Chancellor for Budget, Ministry of Finance

Finance

including representatives from
