2.    Project ImDlementation Unit
A.   Duties
Reporing to the Joint Project Directors of the Ministries of Agriculture and
Environment, and under the overall responsibility of the PIU Project Manager, the tasks
of the Project Implementation Unit (PIU) include:
(a)   On behalf of the Ministries of Agriculture (MOA) and Environment (MOE),
coordinate relations with the line implementing agencies, the World Bank, the EU
Phare Project Management Unit (which coordinates all bi-lateral and EU donors
for MOA), and the Ministry of Finance.
(b)   Coordinate, delegate, monitor and supervise procurement activities of the line
implementing agencies responsible for the respective project components, and
provide administrative and operations support throughout the process as required.
On procurement issues and processes, liaise with the Ministry of Finance,
facilitate so that all relevant parties are coordinated in a timely fashion, and
ensure that procedural requirements and documentation are fulfilled satisfactorily.
Ensure that all clearances and approvals for procurement, including those from
the World Bank, are sought and obtained to avoid unnecessary delays.
(c)   Monitor project implementation and expenditures, and prepare and submit loan
disbursement applications to the World Bank and the Ministry of Finance in
accordance with prevailing procedures.
(d)   Coordinate the preparation of quarterly reports covering progress in the physical
implementation of the project and the use of project's funds, and submit these
reports to the Inter-Ministerial Project Steering Committee (PSC) and the
Ministry of Finance (MOF). The MOF will then consolidate these quarterly
reports into semi-annual progress reports to be submitted to the World Bank and
co-financiers. Every six months, these quarterly progress reports will also



ANNEX 5
Page 4 of 13
include an implementation plan and work program for the next six months
following the reporting period.
(e)   Facilitate World Bank supervision missions, and follow up the implementation of
recommendations of the Bank supervision reports.
(f)   Follow up all implementation aspects of the project components, taking into
consideration various criteria and conditionalities already established in the
Project Implementation Plans, including covenants of the loan agreement. With
the line implementing agencies in MOA and MOE, prepare monitoring notes
concerning problems and delays, and provide suggestions to remedy these
problems through administrative, financial, technical, institutional or other
measures.
(g)   Prepare special reports, as necessary, for submission to the Project Coordination
Committee.
(h)   Perform an overall project accounting and record keeping tasks for the line
implementing agencies in MOA and MOE, and provide administrative, operations
and logistical support to these implementing agencies.
(i)   Perform or commission other tasks as stipulated in the Loan Agreement (eg.
annual audits, mid-term reviews, monitoring and evaluation, and Project
Completion Report), including the administration, accounting and other
operational requirements, by taking the following World Bank's guidelines into
consideration: Disbursement Handbook Guidelines: Procurement under IBRD
Loans and IDA Credits (January 1995)Guidelines: Use of Consultants by World
Bank Borrowers and by the World Bank as Executing Agency (August
1981).Guidelines: Financial Reporting and Auditing of Projects Financed by the
World Bank (March 1982).
(j)   Administer the Special Account, and ensure that reconciliations are performed
regularly on a monthly basis, and replenishment applications prepared for
submission to the World Bank on a timely basis.
B.   Staffing
The PIU, serving both the MOA and MOE, will comprise of 6 project hired persons
including: a Project Manager, a Procurement Specialist, an Accountant, a Monitoring
and Evaluation Specialist, an Administitive Assistant and a Secretary fluent in
Estonian and English. The six persons in the PIU will work on a full-time basis.
Depending on actual requirements, based on implementation experience, the staffing of
the PIU may be reviewed consequently.



ANNEX 5
Page 5 of 13
Professional services, involving national and international consultants, will be contracted
to assist the PIU. These services cover: (a) procurement; (b) accounting and auditing;
(c) project management training; (d) monitoring and evaluation. These professional
services are covered under separate TORs.
C.   Office Facilities and Requirements
The PIU will be physically located in the Ministry of Agriculture. The MOA will provide
adequate office space for the operational needs of the PIU. The PIU office will be
furnished with (a) adequate office furniture including desks, chairs, filing cabinets,
shelves, storage cabinets, and a set of meeting table and chairs; (b) office equipment
including 5 desktop computers and software, a network server and an internet linkage,
laser printers, a photocopier, 2 notebook computers and software, a fax machine and
adequate telephones including cellular telephones; (c) a minivan for official use; and (d)
miscellaneous office supplies and small equipment.
3.    Professional Services: Procurement
A.   Objectives
The PIU will oversee the overall procurement aspects of the project, and ensure
compliance of the project with all procurement requirements and conditionalities of all
project components. While the PIU has a national Procurement Specialist as part of its
staff, the project wil:l seek professional services on procurement with the objectives to:
(a) provide procurement advice and on-the-job assistance to the implementing agencies
of the various components of the project; and (b) train national staff, on procurement
aspects covering applicable procedures, planning, scheduling, execution, management and
monitoring.
B.   Tasks
Reporting to the PIU Project Manager, the Procurement Specialist of the PIU will
assume the day-to-day responsibility of all procurement reltea matters of the project.
During the first two ,years of the project, the PIU Procurement Specialist will be assisted
by calling on consultant(s) to perform professional services on procurement. The
Procurement Consultant will work with and train the Procurement Specialist of the PIU,
and jointly will cover the following scope of work and responsibilities:
(a)   Work with the Project Implementation Unit (PIU), and the  implementing
agencies under the Ministries of Agriculture and Enviromnent of the project, and
liaise with the Procurement Advisory Service of the Ministry of Finance.



ANNEX 5
Page 6 of 13
(b)   Familiarize national counterparts with procurement requirements of the project,
including specific procurement requirements of World Bank funded components,
as provided under the Bank's Guidelines for Procurement under IBRD Loans
and IDA Credits (January, 1995), and Guidelines for Use of Consultants by
World Bank Borrowers and by the World Bank as Executing Agency (August,
1981).
(c)   In accordance with the procurement requirements of each project components and
their methods of procurement, work directly with the relevant staff to review
detailed plans and realistic schedules, taking into consideration all of the
necessary steps.
(d)   In connection with the procurement packages of the project, provide advice and
assistance to the relevant national staff, in organizing and managing procurement
activities which apply procurement methods including: (i) International
Competitive Bidding, National Competitive Bidding, International Shopping,
National Shopping for the procurement of goods and works, and (ii) Procurement
of Consultants for technical assistance, specific studies and supervision services.
(e)   Together with the line implementing agencies, review the appropriateness of
procurement packages with respect to methods of procurement, to ensure that
procurement can be undertaken in the most efficient and cost effective manner in
accordance with the relevant guidelines applicable to the project.
(f)   Provide guidance and intensive assistance in identifying and organizing the
necessary technical support for the preparation of Bidding Documents, wh'.h may
generally include: (i) invitation to bid; (ii) instructions to bidders; (iii) form of
bid; (iv) form of contract; (v) conditions of contract including both special and
general; (vi) specifications and drawings; (vii) list of goods or bill of quantities;
(viii) delivery time or schedule of completion; and all of the necessary appendices
and formats. Bidding documents for International Competitive Bidding shall be
based on Standard Bidding Documents issued by the World Bank with minimum
changes, acceptable to the Bank, as necessary to address country and project
specific issues.
(g)   Provide advice and guidance as necessary in connection with the application of
procedures and requirements related to bid opening, evaluation and award of
contract. Particular attention may be paid to time allowance for preparation of
bids, bid opening procedures, circumstances related to clarifications or alterations
of bids, confidentiality, examination of bids, evaluation and comparison of bids,
and applicable domestic preferences.



ANNEX5
Page 7 of 13
(h)   Together with the national counterparts, establish a Procurement Monitoring
system, to allow collection and recording of procurement data by the PIU for:
(i) timely information flow, submissions and approval of TORs, shortlists, and
other review requirements; (ii) prompt reporting of contract award information
by the Borrower to the Bank; (iii) comprehensive quarterly reports to the Bank;
and (iv) revision of timing of procurement actions and critical paths, including
advertising, bidding, contract award, and completion time for individual
contracts.
(i)   Specifically, develop contracting capacity at:
the Land Board's Contract Administration Office for contracting out cadastral
survey work with a competitive bidding system, and recommend the staffing and
organizational requirements for such a task, taking into consideration the
requirements of the World Bank and the relationship between the Estonia National
Land Board and its county offices; the Ameloraton Unit and its Amelioraton
Bureaux at the county level for contracting out engineering design, civil works,
and supervision of civil works.
'.ij   In relation to contracts, develop administrative and technical procedures for
review of contract performance as an integral part of the approval process for
justifying contract payments.
(k)   Together with the national counterparts, develop a contract management system
to ensure that records and data are stored systematically, and cross-referenced
with the accounts of the project. The system must allow safe keeping of
procurement documentation for easy retrieval and referencing, with adequate
paper trails in accordance with generally accepted standards. The contract
management system must also be designed to support disbursement, reporting,
and accounting requirements of the project.
(1)   Assist the Ministries in drawing up individual job descriptions for administrative
staff assigned for handling procurement related matters, identify training
requirements for the staff, and perform the necessary training through workshops
and on-the-job applications. As appropriate, provide relevant recommendations
to the Ministries in order to ensure that project procurement activities and their
management are undertaken in an efficient, cost effective and timely manner.
C.   Timing
The PIU will recruit a Procurement Specialist (national expert) for a 5-year period as
part of the PIU staff. In addition, the PIU will require professional services from a
Procurement Consultant (intematonal) for a duration of about 15 person-months over



ANNEX 5
Page 8 of 13
a period of 3 years (1996-1998) of the project. Depending on the readiness of the PIU,
the Consultant's services may be mobilized in late 1995 or early of 1996, to speed up
procurement preparation. It is expected that all procurement activities would be fully
undertaken and managed by the PIU and national staff of the Ministries, without further
international consultant's assistance during the last three years of the project.
D.   Qualficatons:
The PIU Procurement Specialist (national expert) must have a university degree in
either administration, business, economics, engineering, management or related technical
fields, with at least 12 months of work experience in commerce, management and
administration. Preference would be given to persons possessing experience in
procurement of goods, works and services internationally and in Estonia.
The Procurement Consultant (international) must possess a university degree and have
at least 5 years of experience in the management and administration of procurement of
goods, works and services in the context of World Bank funded projects. The Consultant
must be familiar with procurement and commercial practices acceptable to the World
Bank, and conversant with the Bank's guidelines on procurement.
Professional Services: Accounting
A.   Objectives
While the PIU recruits a Project Accountant as part of its staff, the project will in
addition require professional services from international and local consultants to: (a) set
up the project accounts and their procedures in accordance with the requirements of the
project; and (b) work with the Project Accountant in the PIU to train relevant national
project staff of the implementing agencies in MOA and MOE, on the project accounts,
including procedures for internal auditing.
B.   Tasks
The scope of the professional services includes the following:
(a)   Based on the requirements of the project, including those outlined in the Bank's
Guidelines for Financial Reporting and Auditing of Projects Financed by the
World Bank (March. 1982), work with the Project Accountant (PIU) and establish
a system for project accounts to cover the accounting needs of all Bank-financed
project components. The project accounting system will take the following into
consideration:
(1)   Development of a chart of accounts, general ledgers and sub-ledgers, as



ANNEX 5
Page 9 of 13
appropriate and consistent with project budgetary and expenditure accounts
for use in the PIU and by the implementing line agencies.
(2)   Documentation  and  requirements  related  to  World  Bank  loan
disbursements, taking into account the necessary records of transactions
to be kept in support of disbursement applications made on the basis of
Statement of Expenditures (SOEs) and other disbursement procedures.
(3)   Cross-references  and  harmonization  with  contract management,
files/records under project administration.
(4)   Reconciliation of accounts and supporting documentation related to eligible
expenditures with respect to the Special Account of the project, and the
necessary requirements to support replenishment applications.
(5)   Progress and financial reporting requirements as agreed between the
Government of Estonia and the World Bank.
(6)   Relevant covenants of the loan agreement, particularly those relating to
procurement, disbursement, accounts and audit.
(b)   Based on the project accounting system, training sessions will be designed and
delivered to familiarize the accounting and administration staff of the project with
the system. On-the-job training and assistance continue until such time that the
accounting operations of the project run satisfactorily. Loan disbursement
procedures of the World Bank will be incorporated into the thuining sessions.
Mechanisms for internal auditing will be incorporated into the training program.
(c)   Integration of project accounts and budget accounting within each of the
implementing agencies responsible for project components.
Reporting to the PIU Project Manager, the primary responsibility of the Project
Accountant will include the following:
(1)   Reporting to the Project Manager of the PIU, perform  day-to-day
accounting tasks related to all aspects of the project accounts.
(2)   Ensure that all line implementing agencies under the Project conform with
the accounting and financial reporting requirements of the project.
(3)   Work with the Procurement Specialist of the PIU, including the
Procurement Consultant, to harmonize accounting requirements with both
procurement and loan disbursement requirements.



ANNEX 5
Page 10 of 13
(4)   Prepare (a) loan disbursement applications; (b) monthly reconciliation of
accounts of all project components and their expenditures; (c)
replenishment application for the Special Account; and (d) other financial
documentation as necessary. Assist and work with the line implementing
agencies in the preparation of project component budgets. Perform
financial monitoring of project activities, and present financial analysis as
required of the PIU.
C.   Timing
Part-dime professional services from international and local consultants will be required
for a period of about 6 person-months spread over the first two years of the project. It
is expected that the project staff in the Project Implementation Unit and line
implementing agencies will be able to handle the operations of the project accounts
without further assistance, commencing from the third year of the project. The Project
Accountant's position is for a duration of 5 years.
Professional Services: Monitoring and Evaluation Specialist
A.   Tasks
As part of the Project Implementation Unit and reporting to the PIU Project Manager,
the Monitoring and Evaluation (M&E) Specialist is required to:
(a)   Work with the Amelioration Unit and Amelioration Bureaux, and assist them in
site selection of drainage systems for rehabilitation, by reviewing the economic
selection criteria used for specific sites. Several factors to be considered include
the following:
the average cost of rehabilitation, including design, civil works and supervision.
Severely damaged systems and areas, requiring more than rehabilitation, must be
excluded as reconstruction costs are prohibitive and economically questionable.
The profitability of farm operations on the proposed sites, and the potential gains
that can be attributed to drainage improvement. This potential may include among
other things: (i) changes to cropping patterns and intensity; (ii) potential
introduction of new technology (such as grassland with legumes); (iii) the
possibility of early planting and delaying harvest time - lengthening growing
season by improving drainage conditions during critical periods; and (iv) crop
yield improvement and expected yields that can surpass profitable yield
thresholds.



ANNEX 5
Page 11 of 13
(b)   Work with the Land Board and the Amelioration Unit, and ensure that priorities
for cadastral land surveys and registration include the proposed drainage sites,
and that timing for these surveys is suitably planned to clarify land tenure issues
in the context of LWA development.
(c)   Work closely with the Amelioration Unit and the Task Force of the Agriculture
Advisory Services, and ensure that LWAs of the proposed drainage sites receive
adequate attention in the targeting of extension and advisory services. Demand
for advisory services among these LWAs will be assessed and responded to.
(d)   Together with the respective implementing agencies, based on the Project
Implementation Plans develop a monitoring and evaluation (M&E) system for
each project component, incorporating indicators for measuring project inputs,
outputs, impacts (micro and macro) and the means for verifying and measuring
these indicators.
(e)   Periodically, monitor and evaluate progress of implementation of all project
components, provide concise notes on possible measures for overcoming major
issues that constrain project implementation, for the attention of the PIU Manager
and the respective implementing agencies.
(f)   Identify bottlenecks and systemic problems in implementation, and review these
conditions jointly with the relevant agencies to come up with measures and
options for solving these problems. Alert the PIU Project Manager of major
problems that require the attention of the Inter-Ministerial Project Steering
Committee and/or the Joint Project Directors at MOA and MOE.
(g)   Assist all implementing agencies, on financial, cost and economic analyses related
to their project components; and when appropriate also involve the Procurement
Specialist and the Project Accountant of the PIU in the context of these analyses.
(h)   Develop rural income indicators, assess on and off-farm employment in project
target areas, and perform pre and post investment M&E on a sample basis.
(i)    Carry out field monitoring trips on a regular basis, in association with the
implementing agencies, and test out these M&E systems for further refinement
as necessary. Ensure that the M&E systems provide immediate feedback to allow
corrective measures to be introduced as early as possible.
(j)   Liaise with MOA and MOE policy units and develop environmental economic
impact assessment indicators for project target areas.



ANNEX 5
Page 12 of 13
(k)   Follow up on the development of land policy concerning land reform and disposal
of state lands, and monitor progress on land restitution in the project areas,
including cadastral registration and land titling.
(1)   Perform other tasks on monitoring and evaluation as assigned by the PIU Project
Manager.
B.   Timing and Qualifications
The position is for a duration of 5 years in the PIU. The selected person must possess
a university degree in either economics, agricultural economics, business management,
agriculture, engineering, or related fields, with at least 12 months experience in financial
and economic evaluation of investment programs. Experience in monitoring and
evaluation of projects is highly desirable.
Professional Services: Project Management
A.   Objectives
The objective of this consultancy is to train national project staff assigned by the
Ministries of Agriculture and Environment, on relevant aspects of project management
and administration, including monitoring and evaluation.
B.   Tasks
The tasks of the Project Management consultant include the following:
(a)   Conduct a rapid training needs assessment, based on the management and
administration requirements of the project and its components.
(b)   Design an appropriate training package, incorporating fundamental principles of
project management including topics to cover the following:
(i)   application of management principles to plan, organize, staff, control and
direct resources (including budgeting);
(ii)  techniques commonly used in operational planning, including the
application of critical path analysis and resource driven scheduling;
(iii) the use of computer software applications for project planning,
management, and administration, including spreadsheet applications;



Page 13 of 13
(iv)   planning of actions and tasks for achieving project objectives, and
organizing the necessary resources for carrying out the plan;
(v)   communications, including techniques for effective presentations, meetings
and negotiations;
(vi)  task management, including: division of tasks, organization of work
arrangements, roles and functions of work-groups, special task forces,
responsibility matrix, reporting channels, work delegation, and other
management functions and tools;
(vii)  monitoring and evaluation of physical progress of project implementation,
including use of indicators for measuring inputs, outputs, impacts,
progress milestones, and means for verifying these indicators;
(viii)  project administration  requirements, including: files and  records
management, contract management (procurement aspects covered under
a separate terms of reference), project accounts (a separate terms of
reference to set up and maintain these accounts), logistical and secretarial
tasks;
(ix)   other topics, as assessed to be necessary during training needs assessment,
to ensure the delivery of a comprehensive project management training.
(c)   Train staff of the Project Implementation Unit (PIU), and project staff of the
implementing agencies, through workshops and on-the-job applications.
C. Timing
Consultant's services will be required for a period of about 3 person-months, and the
training program is to be conducted in three stages: (a) training needs assessment; (b)
design of training sessions; and (c) delivery of training. A combination of international
and local consultants are required for the delivery of the training program. This training
program will be delivered as soon as possible after (a) all PIU staff have been hired; and
(b) accounts and procurement systems are in place.

Estonia Agriculture Project
Project Implementation Plan
Land Reform Component
I. The Project
A. Component Objectives
1.    The broad objective of the land reform component of the project is to assist Estonia in
the development of the land market, which would facilitate: (a) the land reform (restitution and
privatization of the land); (b) the use of land as collateral to obtain long tern credit, and
subsequent farm restructuring; and (c) exit and entry into farming. The establishment of such a
market requires developing the legal framework, as well as the technical and institutional capacity.
The immediate and main objective of the project component is to speed up land survey and land
registration in the land cadastre.
B. Component Scope
2.     Land Reform. The total area of agricultural and forest lands to be eventually privatized
is estimated at 2.3 million hectares (including natural grasslands, arable land, orchards and
forests). The reform of land ownership, farm organization and property rights is a central element
of Estonia's economic reform. The actual process of formal transfer of ownership to the newly
established private farms has been slower than desirable. Despite the importance attached by GOE
to land reform and privatization, only 13% of this area has been divested to private control to date
(including restituted land and land under the Farn Law Act of 1989). The slow progress of land
reform can be attributed variously to the difficulties in the development of the legal frmework,
insufficient institutional arrangements, and limited financial resources to complete the land
surveys. The approach chosen for the land reform - physical restitution based on pre-1940
boundaries - seems to be the most significant factor in slowing down land privatization, and
creating uncertainties as to the rights of the farmers. Even if restitution were proceed faster, it
is unclear that it would improve land use and expedite the development of an active land market.
Many land owners have chosen alternative lands or non-land compensation in place of accepting
their "ancestral lands." Consequently, a significant amount of the arable and forest lands remains
under state ownership after the end of the first stage of land reform.
3.     Up to December 1995, 23,529 units (approx. 223,000 ha) have been registered in the
cadastre and 11,000 parcels (approx. 180,000 ha) in the title book. Estimated registration in 1995
was 15,000 units (approx. 130,000 ha). The National Land Board (NLB) plans to register over
1,500 cadastral units (13,000 ha) per month in 1996.
4.     The implementation of land reform in Estonia requires well-defined prerequisites and
priorities, as well as better sequencing of reform elements, so that a well-functioning land market
can emerge. The most important part of the land reform is to continue, and finalize the restitution
of the properties. An important task for GOE is to work out a state land disposal policy for state-
owned lands in rural areas. The project also aims to accelerate the reform process on areas where
it has been slow.



PIP I
Page 2 of 9
5.     SimRlifving cadastral and title book registration. Titling is not an end in itself, but a
means to achieve security of tenure, and to provide information to facilitate land transactions
through an accurate and up-to-date registration system. Estonia adopted a land registration system
that is close to the German Real Property System and requires registration based on exact land
survey. The titling procedure was already simplified by the "Law of Amending the Legal Acts
Relating to the Implementation Law of Real Property Law", (August 12, 1994). However, the
Title Book Offices still need three months to register the title, which prolongs the procedure.
6.     The Government is working on simplifying regulations and devising appropriate measures
to limit the cost and time spent per unit restituted or privatized. The Governmental Decree "The
order for restitution of the properties on the base of existing maps" was passed in early 1995. This
order allows restitution of lands to the previous owners without completing an exact land survey.
The property borders would be registered based on existing maps prepared during the Soviet era.
However, only 20% of all properties could be registered this way. Another option being explored
would include the issuance of a "provisional" title with regard to the estimated boundaries.
Boundaries would only be demarcated in the field and simply indicated to owners. Boundaries
would also be registered on the cadastral maps. Further clarification of boundaries can be done
later, if deemed necessary by appropriate parties, prior to market transactions (buyers, sellers,
banks, etc.). Despite the above options, experience in East Gernany shows that transitional
measures cannot fully compensate for exact surveys, and that there is no alternative to carrying
out land surveys. Regardless of the legal framework, owners need to know what precisely they
own, and where their borders are, especially when it comes to land transactions and cultivation.
7.     Physical restitution versus voucher compensation. The vouchers will be replaced with
special privatization banking accounts. It is possible to use the privatization bank accounts to pay
for privatized lands and other properties. The compensation for lands and other properties is
deposited in a privatization bank account, and can be used for privatization of apartments, lands,
shares of companies, etc. In some rural areas, it seems likely that owners would more readily
accept compensation than restitution. In Tallinn, for instance, almost every property will be
restituted, but many owners opt for compensation for their agricultural lands in rural areas.
However the compensation value for rural lands is small. Owners are not willing to opt for
compensation immediately, and are postponing their decisions, although every owner was
supposed to have decided by April 1995 whether to opt for restitution or compensation. The new
Govermment has decided to compensate only the lands that could not be restituted.
8.     Reorganization of Est-survey. In a market economy, there is little need for parcel
surveying to be carried out by a public agency. To expedite parcel surveys, Est-survey, the state-
owned land survey company, which has already lost more than half of it's surveyors to the private
sector, needs to be privatized. Reorganization of the National Land Board (NLB) has started by
re-integrating the Department of the Cadastre of Est-survey into NLB. The cadastral Center of
NLB was established. The County Land Cadastre Offices have been established in ten of the
counties and the rest (five) will be established before the end of May, 1996. A special Decree
passed by the Government (Decree 9, January, 1995) regulates the re-organization of the National
Land Board. The Decree specifies that:
(a) The NLB would be an effective organization responsible for Land Cadastre,
Geodesy and Mapping;



PIP I
Page 3 of 9
(b) The activities of NLB would be coordinated with other involved institutions; and
(c) The NLB should not compete with the private sector on an equal basis for
specific land survey activities.
9. The Governmental Decree further stipulates that:
(a) The Department of the Cadastre of Est-survey, the Department of Informatics,
and the Archive shall be re-integrated into NLB.
(b) The Mapping Center shall be re-integrated into the NLB.
(c) The County Est-Survey Offices will be privatized or terninated.
10    Privatization of the county land survey offices was planned for the beginning of 1996.
NLB hopes that the tender for privatizing the first five County Offices of Est-survey can be
published by May, 1996 and that the bidding process can take place before June, 1996.
11.   Role of private sector At the present moment, there are 125 licensed private surveying
companies, and 48 individual surveyors in Estonia that are involved in land surveying, which is
a necessary part of the process of restitution, replacement, selling and purchase of land, as well
as of the contractual use of land. In addition, 138 licenses for geodetic and cartographic work
have been given out. A study has shown that approximately 95% of the companies are now
actively operating. There are still an additional 39 private companies and surveyors that have
applied for a license. In the near future, a new status of surveyor will be given out to some of
the license owners. In addition to the surveying work, the owner of the new license could also
perform juridical operations regarding cadastre works. According to estimations, the above-
mentioned companies would continue the work in the future, but their activities would be
supervised and they would be provided with up-to-date geodetic network information and existing
map information in order to guarantee a coordinated functioning of the whole cadastral system.
This task would be performed by the NLB.
12.    Prioritization and sequencing of survey works and cadastre registration. Even after all the
above measures are taken, the costs of titling, and the time it takes, are likely to be substantial.
Systematic titling should be sequenced and prioritized. The following criteria to prioritize the
survey work location, in order of importance are:
(a) Drainage rehabilitation sites: The project supports the rehabilitation of drainage
systems and these lands should be surveyed as a first priority. A large number of the
properties surveyed under this project can be selected on the basis of drainage rehabilitation
areas.
(b) Regional Policy Sites: These are areas where land reform has been slow in the
past and where demand for restitution is limited, but develop of local infrastructure and
farming is important.



PIP 1
Page 4 of 9
(c) Higher potential market value: These are areas with desirable land use/quality
attributes, or location attributes, such as better soil quality, better access to the urban
markets, etc.
(d) Lower potential cost for adjudication: These are areas where potential owners are
easily identifiable and whose claims are least likely to be contested by others in the process
of titling. Land covered by the present farming law, in which the private farmers have
perpetual use rights but no rights to sell or mortgage land, can fit into this criteria.
13. Adiustment in land confiauration and easements: The restructuring of large enterprise
farms offers the opportunity for adjusting the shapes and sizes of the subdivided parcels
within these farms. This is especially a problem where restituted owners have fragmented
pieces of land within a large farm, or where such pieces have no access to roads or
services.
ONGOING AND REQUESTED PROJECTS
14.   Under separate arrangements, donor-funded parallel projects provide technical assistance
and training for strengthening the institutional and technical capacities of the NLB and related
agencies.
(a) Land Registry Management Advice (96-98) will be carried out by SwedSurvey
and financed by Sweden as part of larger program which has been agreed in principle for
1996. Continuation of this sub-component is agreed with SwedSurvey for longer term.
(b) Land Assessment Methodology Development (95-96) has been agreed with the
Finnish Government and the project is running. The land assessment methodology so far
has been created in close cooperation with Finnish experts and the changes into the system
will be worked out together.
(c) Land and Real Estate Legal Advice (96-98) has been intensively investigated and
two possible donors have been identified: Sweden and Austria.
(d) Training of Surveyors (95-97) contains three different projects: Continuation of
LIMPE-project (EU-PHARE); the training for trainers in surveying; and later courses on
the equipment and software purchased in the LIMPE-project previous phase.
(e) Training of Cadastral Specialists (96-97). This Sub-Component is planned inside
the same PHARE-programn, and part of it will be covered by Sweden and Germany. It is
planned to introduce to the local cadastral specialists the goals of the modern cadastral
system and the different ways to achieve them.
(f) Support for development and implementation of the cadastral system in county
offices and central cadastral office. The main elements for the county (decentralized)
system are the interface to IPIS (alphanumeric cadastre) and data exchange between



PIP I
Page 5 of 9
counties, the central system and surveyors. The main elements for the central system are:
design of dataflow (basic map, cadastral map, ground control points); interface to other
registers; data exchange; and service for the public and private sector.
(g) Mapping and cartography. Project plans cover the period from 1995 to 1999. The
main activities are aerial photography, production of digital line maps, and orthophotos
corresponding to the national map-production plans. Total budget is approximately
8,000,000 SEK. Financed by Sweden.
(h) Photogrammetric and surveying equipment. This request includes 5 analytical
Photogrammetric instruments, digitizers, plotters and printers, workstations for map editing,
server, etc. to be delivered in 1996 and 1997. Total budget is approximately CHF
2.500.000. Financed by Switzerland.
D. Component Description
15.   The land reform component provides financing for land survey works, and for creating
and densifying the National Geodetic Network.
16.   The National Geodetic Network (NGN) will need reconstruction, and until the work on
re-measurement of the NGN is finalized, the kinematic GPS receivers can be used for
establishment of lower order networks, to coordinate the boundaries in one unified coordinate
system. This work will allow the definition of the boundaries to the cadastral map to be of higher
accuracy and will enable the surveying of the boundaries of large territories quickly and with high
accuracy. The program is planned for four years, because in 1999, the measurements on the NGN
will be completed or the network would be in the position that the majority of boundaries can be
tied to the NGN. Within four years, 3,000 points will be measured, with the points measured
using GPS technology available for future measurements. The estimated price for measurement
of one point is US$470, and the total price of the component is US$1,410,000 (60% with World
Bank financing for US$846,000). The most important idea of the component is not to re-
coordinate the boundaries, but to get exact information in one unified system. All the work will
be contracted out through local competitive bidding.
17.   Cadastral Survey. The lack of adequate budgetary allocations, as well as fixed-price
contracts to private surveyors (at one half of the market price), has contributed to a considerable
slow down of restitution irelated to cadastral registration. Est-Survey, the state agency which had
traditionally carried out parcel surveying, has been losing it's surveyors to the private sector.
Indeed, there is no justification for parcel surveying still to be carried out by the public sector.
A major component of the project - US$5,260,000 (60% World Bank financing for US$3,154,000)
- therefore, is budgetary support for procuring parcel surveying using Local Competitive Bidding
procedures. The contracts should be prepared in a way that the survey area is divided into
attractive sized packages at the district level, with a cost amounting US$250,000 per contract.
The choice and sequencing of areas should follow criteria identified above. According to the
proposals and estimations in the frame of this project, approximately 7,800 rural properties (total
area approx. 151,000 ha will be surveyed at cost of approximately US$35/ha.



PIP I
Page 6 of 9

II. Implementation Armngements
A. Responsibilities of Implementing Agencies
19.   National Land Board. The NLB will be responsible for: development of cadastral
registration and cadastral mapping in Estonia; overseeing the decentralization of cadastral work
to the county level; speeding up and simplifying the surveying and registration process; and
ensuring coordination with the Title Book Offices. NLB will be will be responsible for
contracting out all the survey contracts (World Bank and budgetary financed survey works)
through national competitive bidding procedures, and to supervise the contracts. NLB would
appoint staff required for the coordination of the land reform component.
20    The Government of Estonia. The Ministry of Environment, and the Land Reform
Commission will coordinate the activities in connection with this component in order to improve
the legal/policy environment of the land reform process. According to the Land Reform Law the
Government of Estonia and local municipalities are responsible for implementation of land reform.
The Land Reform Commission is mandated to coordinate land policy for GOE and legislators. The
Commission consists of representatives of the Ministries of Justice, Agriculture, and Environment,
The National Land Board, and members of Parliament and Local Authorities.
21.   The Contract Administration Unit (CAU) will be established in NLB to organize and
supervise of the land survey works. The CAU would consist of a project coordinator and 4-5 staff
to recruit surveyors through a competitive process. The CAU will organize this competitive
bidding and perform relevant accounting functions. The project coordinator of the CAU would
coordinate with the local municipalities, the relevant ministries, and other authorities to be
involved. Survey contracts will be prepared together with the local municipalities which would
decide where to carry out the surveys. The areas will be selected according to defined priorities.

ESTONIA
AGRICULTURE PROJECT
PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION PLAN
DRAINAGE REHABILITATION AND LAND MANAGEMENT COMPONENT
I. The Component
A. Objectives
1.    The objective of the component is to assist in the restructuring and revitalization of Estonian
agriculture. In line with this objective, the drainage rehabilitation and maintenance component focuses
on action required to address critical needs resulting from neglect of drainage infrastructure during the
transition period, and, after a one time rehabilitation, aims at handing over future operations and
maintenance of the drainage system to the beneficiaries. Not all of the lands drained in the past should
be rehabilitated due to both environmental and economic concerns. Hence, the project will focus on:
(a)   limited physical rehabilitation and maintenance of drainage systems on lands with high
agricultural values and production potentials;
(b)    promotion and establishment of Land and Water Associations (LWAs) to take over
ownership, management and financial responsibility for all on-farm systems (once
rehabilitated) above the large main drains; and
(c)   reorientation of drainage research and training, emphasizing practical field application for
cost effective maintenance.
B. Component Description
2.    The drainage rehabilitation component will consist of: (a) physical works on 60,000 ha over five
years (less than 10% of drained farmland in Estonia), covering main drain rehabilitation and
maintenance, rehabilitation and maintenance of collector drains, flushing and repair of subsurface pipe
outlets entering collector drains, and selective tillage of compacted soils in the context of drainage
improvement; (b) establishment of about 60 LWAs within the project area as formation of farmer groups
to assume ownership of on-farm drainage systems and bear full responsibility for future operations and
maintenance of the systems once rehabilitated; and (c) development of an adaptive research progmm to
test innovative techniques for reducing and controlling maintenance costs. This project component also
includes related training and studies. The drainage systems targeted for rehabilitation would be selected
on the basis of social, technical, economic and environmental criteria.
3.    The land management component consists of studies of, and equipment for, environmentally
sensitive areas.
Physical Rehabilitation of the Drainage Systems
4.    The drainage systems in Estonia are extensive and critical to the future productivity of agricultural
systems. Natural conditions for agriculture are far from optimal, primarily due to the short growing season



PIP 2
Page 2 of 18
and the generally poor soil conditions. Although precipitation is fairly uniform between 570 and 720
mm/year, 65% of this rainfall occurs during a 5-month period from May to September with distinctive
and critical peaks during short periods. On areas where natural drainage is inadequate during peak
rainfalls, improved drainage through artificial means can extend the growing season for about a month
by ensuring that groundwater table can be maintained at least 40 cm below surface. Drainage regulates
moisture content and the depth of the groundwater table in the soils. Well drained lands can be planted
earlier and harvested later, as waterlogging during rainfall peaks can be avoided and fields are dry enough
for farm machinery to operate. In the context of the project, drainage rehabilitation will not be carried
out on polders where submerged lands are drained for agricultural purposes. Drainage improvement
will be primarily aimed at regulating the depth (below surface) of the groundwater table and moisture of
the soils during critical periods, and thereby lengthening the growing season and increasing crop and
pasture yields.
5.     Transitional adjustments of the economy have resulted in a lack of funding for the maintenance
of the drainage systems since 1988. Subsurface pipe systems will require selective flushing of lines to
remove sediments and improve their proper performance, but rehabilitation work is important for restoring
the capacity and depth of many open main and field drains. The cleaning of open drainage canals allows
the free flow of subsurface pipes, thus maintaining their flow capacity. This work has to be done
selectively, applying a set of criteria for prioritizing rehabilitation work and limiting the physical works
to lands where environmental damage will be minimal and agricultural productivity can be profitable and
sustainable. When maintenance is deferred too long up to a point, these drainage systems can no longer
be maintained or rehabilitated, and reconstruction would be prohibitive in costs and economically
questionable. Rehabilitation costs on lands targeted by the project are estimated to be about EEK 1,500/ha
in 1995 financial terms, a small fraction (7.5%) of the full reconstruction costs of about EEK 20,000/ha.
Selection Criteria for Drainage Rehabilitation Sites'
6.     Selection of Areas. Drainage rehabilitation works will be undertaken on a total 60,000 ha, less
than 10% of the existing agricultural lands that have already been drained. Drainage investments will be
limited to rehabilitation works and will not involve investments in new drainage schemes. Sites where
drainage rehabilitation would not be allowed include:
*    areas where fish and wildlife habitat is protected by Estonian environmental laws or rules;
a    poldered areas;
*    wetlands and estuaries with important wildlife or fishery values;
*    important native grasslands, buffer zones, and forest lands of special significance;
*    forest lands, until the forest strategy study is completed;
7.     Potential sites would be screened individually and thoroughly, following a selection process to
ensure that technical, economic, social and environmental criteria are fulfilled. Each of the following
selection criteria shall be fulfilled before commencement of the drainage rehabilitation works:
(a)    Environmental Screening. Each drainage site shall be screened and cleared for
The selection criteria have been specified in a supplemental letter to the loan agreement.



PIP2
Page 3 of 18
rehabilitation by the Ministry of Environment or the respective District Environment
Protection Department according to official regulations on the environmental screening
criteria. The environmental screening process of the drainage rehabilitation program is
elaborated in Annex 4: Environmental Aspects;
(b)    Soil Fertility. Each drainage rehabilitation site shall have an average soil fertility of no
less than 40 points out of a scale of 100 based on the Estonian Soils Classification
System. Soils with less than 40 points can only be included up to no more than 10% of
each site area only when this substandard area must be included because of hydraulic
design requirements. This condition aims for the best 25% of the drained farmlands, and
practically excludes 75% of the existing drained farmlands;
(c)    Land use intensity. More than 70% of the agricultural land shall currently be utilized for
agricultural purposes;
(d)    Conditions of the Drainage Systems. Each drainage site shall be assessed and approved
for rehabilitation by the local amelioration bureau based on technical and engineering
feasibility;
(e)    Development Costs. Rehabilitation costs of the drainage system, including civil works,
designs, and supervision, shall not exceed EEK 1,500/ha in constant 1995 prices. This
limit will exclude areas that require excessive and complex rehabilitation works;
(h)    Farmers' Participation. Farmers would have fonned LWAs in accordance with the Land
Amelioration Law. The LWAs would:
(i)    make an application for the rehabilitation works;
(ii)   jointly approve the program of works together with the Amelioration
Bureaux in the county;
(iii)   undertake maintenance of the system after it is rehabilitated, and ensure
that on-farm drainage systems are effectively maintained;
(iv)   contribute 20 per cent of the physical rehabilitation costs through cash or
labor prior to commencement of designs and civil works; and
(v)    meet the administrative and operational costs (such as expenses for
meetings and book keeping) of the Association.
(f)    Land Tenure. Before commencement of the drainage rehabilitation works, no more than
5% of the agricultural land in any specific drainage site to benefit from drainage
rehabilitation would remain state -owned or not leased to the private sector, with the
exception of educational establishments. All lease contracts for state-owned agricultural
land should be for a period of not less than 10 years. The extent to which land has been
privatized and/or restituted will be considered2. This includes lands actually used by
farmers, lands leased to farmers, and lands for which the owners opted for compensation.
Further, all state-owned agricultural lands in a specific drainage site would be available
2      Cadastral surveys of drainage sites would be prioritized by the Land Board, and all lands benefiting from drainage would
be registered in the cadastre prior to commencement of the drainage works at any specific site.



PIP 2
Page 4 of 18
for sale or lease prior to the beginning of the drainage rehabilitation works on that site.
The number of unresolved land disputes in each proposed site will also be used as a
criteria for selection or non-selection, and will be evaluated through the proposed social
assessments; and
8.    In addition to the selection criteria, the rehabilitation and maintenance of open drains will be
carried out using techniques to minimize environmental damages. These implementation criteria will
include the following:
*     limiting excavation to design cross-sectional lines when channel cleanouts are carried out;
having equipment work from one side of the channel wherever possible;
*     in view of-the need for sediment control, minimize damage to vegetation on channel side
slopes; and
*     install instream sediment traps where needed.
9.    Phasing of Development. Applying the physical criteria for area selection, the 12 Amelioration
Bureaux (ABs) throughout the country (under the coordination of the Amelioration Unit in the Ministry
of Agriculture) have preliminarily selected 42,000 ha of drainage sites located in 13 counties. To date,
no areas have been preselected in Walga and Hiiumaa counties. These potential areas of 42,000 ha have
been preselected primarily on the basis of their physical characteristics and potential, based on surveys
conducted by ABs in 1994 and early 1995. Further preselection continues in order to identify more areas
for inclusion into a pool of potential areas which will then go through a more rigorous selection process.
It is planned that design work for the 60,000 ha development program will take place over 4 years
covering: 3,500 ha in 1995; 16,500 ha in 1996; 25,000 ha in 1997 and the remaining 15,000 ha in 1998.
Physical rehabilitation works for each site will be undertaken over a period of two years once design is
completed. It is anticipated that 40% of the physical works will be done in the first year after design is
completed, and the remaining 60% completed in the second year. Initially physical works will concentrate
on main canals and collector drains, and subsequently on repairs of subsurface field drains. Design
planning, LWA development and strengthening and physical works will take place over a period of 3
years for each site. With this time framework, the aggregate plan for physical works will be spread over
a period of 5 years: commencing in the summer of 1996 with 1,400 ha; 8,700 ha in 1997; 19,900 ha in
1998; 21,000 ha in 1999 and the rest of 9,000 ha in year 2000.
10.   Pilot sites. In the preselection process, the Amelioration Unit (AU) of MOA has identified two
pilot sites for detailed assessment and environmental screening in 1995. These sites, namely Vigala (2,300
ha drained area) of Rapla county and Rannu (1,660 ha drained area) of Tartu county (should they meet
the full requirements of the project, including the social preparation and LWA development aspects) would
be thoroughly designed in 1995 using existing budget sources, followed by physical works in 1996 and
1997 under the project. It is anticipated that the total areas of these 2 pilot sites for rehabilitation would
not exceed 3,500 ha.
11.   Cost Recovery. The GOE plans to privatize the ownership, operations and maintenance of all
farm drainage systems by handing over these systems to established LWAs. The Amelioration Law of May
1994 requires the formation of LWAs to take over the systems. Under the same law, ABs can levy cost
recovery fees from users. LWA Membership is compulsory for every beneficiary of the specific drainage
basin. GOE has determined that each LWA must agree to contribute (in cash or in kind) 20% of the cost
of the one-round of selective rehabilitation works, while GOE pays for the rest of the upfront costs for



PIP 2
Page 5 of 18
upgrading the systems to conditions that can normally be maintained. In return, the GOE would no longer
be financially responsible for these on-farm drainage systems, and each LWA will have full ownership
of its area-specific drainage system and bear full costs of the O&M responsibility. GOE is therefore
effectively privatizing the drainage systems. However, the ABs will continue to be responsible for
operations and maintenance of rivers and main canals outside the drainage areas, as the main canals often
serve other users such as industries and municipalities. Cost recovery options for the maintenance of
rivers and main canals are being considered by the AU, including: (a) the possibility of transferring the
financial responsibility of this maintenance to the respective municipalities which can then recover these
costs from beneficiaries through local municipal taxes, and; (b) direct levies to LWAs, and other users.
Development of Land and Water Associations
12.   The Government through the Amelioration Law of 1994 intends to privatize the ownership of
existing drainage systems and transfer the responsibility of operations and maintenance of the systems to
the farmers organized in Land and Water Associations (LWAs). The desirable direction for the
management of the drainage system is to promote increasing farmer participation in system management
and maintenance, with the Amelioration Bureaux (ABs) of the Government eventually playing a limited
but important regulatory and oversight role. However, the realities of the current conditions warrant a
transition arrangement until: (a) the ABs can effectively perform planning, regulatory, and emergency
management functions; and (b) the LWAs can take over most of the system management functions at the
local level above the main drainage channels. At present, there are four LWAs registered under the old
Law of Associations predating the present Amelioration Law. Five to ten LWAs are anticipated to be
registered in 1995. In the present context of Estonian agriculture, formation of such associations under
the new law (which specifies financial and management responsibilities of farmers) will be progressive
and will need a strong program of action for reasons given below.
13.   Given the uncertainties surrounding land ownership and use, unstable input and output prices, and
weak access to market intelligence, it does not appear easy for farmers to take over immediate
responsibility for the drainage system. The assumption of management and financial responsibilities for
the drainage system by the LWAs must be planned and developed in a sequential, participatory and
evolutionary manner. A blueprint approach is clearly not desirable. Under these circumstances, it is
planned that the Government will extend financial assistance to the LWAs for one round of selective
rehabilitation of system, especially collector drains and related drainage systems, to prevent their further
deterioration during the transition. Once rehabilitated to conditions where these systems would require
normal and routine maintenance, the LWAs would assume full responsibility for subsequent operations,
maintenance and periodic rehabilitation in the future.
14.   Size and Functions of LWAs. The size of the Association would depend on the hydraulic area
to be served, taking into account the size of each catchment area. It is expected that each Association
would eventually cover about 1,000 ha and the Associations in the proposed project area for Bank support
will likely number about 60. The primary responsibilities of the Association would be: (a) involvement
in the planning, acceptance and implementation of rehabilitation works; (b) system maintenance of on-
farm main drains, collector drains, and drain outlets; and (c) protection of the system against theft and
destruction. To this end, the associations would be legally empowered to enter into contracts with repair
and maintenance companies and borrow funds where necessary. In addition, the LWAs would provide
an organized forum for farmers and external agencies for discussions and action on business planning, land
improvement, and agricultural development issues including needs for advisory services or extension. The



PIP 2
Page 6 of 18
LWAs would also, over time, organize training programs for their members in technical and financial
management and investment planning. Relevant laws regulating the functions of the LWAs include: Land
Amelioration Law (1994); Law on Associations (1992); Business Law (1995); and the Water Law (1994).
15.   Internal Organization and Management of LWAs. While each LWA is responsible for establishing
each own management, the Land Amelioration Law stipulates that each association must comply with the
Law of Associations (1992). In general, a Chairperson is elected for a maximum period of 5 years, either
directly by members (a case when the size of the LWA is less than 10 members) or through the LWA
Board. Main responsibilities of the Chairperson include the overall functioning of the LWA, chairing the
LWA Board, and chairing the annual meetings of the LWA. The LWA Board, in cases needed for larger
LWAs, is elected by members. Annually, a third of the board members are to be replaced through an
election. Jointly, the Chairperson and the Board are responsible for: (a) day-to-day activities, including
supervision of farmers' contribution to the maintenance of the drainage system; (b) maintaining contacts
with the Amelioration Bureau, the Municipality, the county office of the Land Board, the Farmers' Union,
and other LWAs in the vicinity; (c) representing the LWA in meetings with third parties; and (d)
organizing annual meetings where decisions are made on budget, administrative and operational matters.
Voting is based on simple majority: one vote for each member (regardless of the size of the individual
member's farm), unless otherwise specified in the constitution document of the association. The LWA,
depending on size, will also have an Executive Secretariat consisting of technical staff who can deal with
specific matters related to (for example) operations and maintenance, machinery, technical calculations
and budgeting, preliminary designs and internal approval of works. The LWA may also employ
administrative and accounting personnel on a full or part-time basis. Should the LWAs be larger than
30 members, an internal auditing committee is formed.
16.   Procedure for LWA Formation. Once an initiative is taken to form an association by a group of
farmers, most commonly a Task Force is formed for the preparation of a constitutional meeting and its
subsequent series of discussions and consultations for the preparation of the Constitution Document of the
association. This Task Force may consist of a few farmers, a representative from the Amelioration
Bureau, and a representative from the Municipality. The LWA becomes a legal entity once registered
at the county level. The registration process may take one week to a few months in cases where legal
complication arises. Preparation for the Constitution Document requires active participation from farmers
so that intemal rules and regulations of the association can be formulated satisfactorily. The Constitution
Document must necessarily cover basic issues relating to: (a) level of membership entrance and annual
fees; (b) definition of shared responsibilities and costs; (c) procedures for calculating and allocating shared
costs; (d) collection procedures for shared costs; (e) description of the use of revenues and fees; (f)
regulations to deal with non-payment of shared costs; (g) methods to deal with inflation; and (h) other
administrative, financial, operational, management and organizational aspects of the association.
17.   Training and Government Assistance for LWA Development. The formation of effective LWAs
will require a major effort on the part of the Amelioration Bureaux. The experience of the present AB
staff is largely technical, and until recently has not involved extensive participatory interaction with
farmers. In some cases, the farmers too are new to the agricultural scene. It is important that the ABs
participate actively in the formation of Associations because they would have to enter into several rounds
of discussions and negotiations with members of the Association to come up with an agreement on and
joint approval of the scope of rehabilitation works and responsibilities, prior to the commencement of
physical works on each site. A systematic program of communication and training will be necessary to
induce ABs to participate actively in strengthening the LWAs in using enabling legislation. To this end,



PIP 2
Page 7 of 18
the staff of the ABs will need training and advisory assistance on the legal, organizational and financial
aspects of farmers' organization. The GOE is committed to provide funding through its budget sources
to: (a) train the staff of the ABs: and (b) recruit and train local consultants to serve as facilitators in the
context of the LWA development and to assist in the formulation of various agreements between the
Government and LWAs on the scope of rehabilitation works and shared responsibilities for each site.
Training of the AB staff and LWA development facilitators is planned to commence in 1996 and continue
intermittently until 1997.
18.   In direct connection with LWA development, the Government provides assistance in three forms:
(a) one-time assistance in the form of office equipment, software and supplies; (b) training and extension
related to skill development of the LWA leaders and members for organizing and managing their day-to-
day tasks (administration, accounting, technical operations and maintenance etc.); and (c) provision for
start-up costs associated with formation of the LWAs including consultations, communications, legal paper
work, right up to registration at the county level. The costs associated with these three forms of direct
assistance to the LWA are estimated on average at EEK 90,000 in year 1995 financial terms per LWA.
The GOE will provide the MOA with adequate budget allocations to cover these expenses.
19.    Social Assessment. Studies and Technical Assistance. During the course of the project, the
Amelioration Unit will commission three studies, namely: (a) a series of social assessment of target
drainage areas; (b) clarification of the future role of the Amelioration Bureaux; and (c) a mid-term review
of the LWA development program. These studies would be undertaken and phased appropriately to
support the overall development of the LWAs and the targeting of project's initiatives and activities.
Social assessment of target sites will be carried out early in 1996, and development of local skills to
undertake this social assessment has started in the last quarter of 1995 (post appraisal, prior to project start
date) with training provided through bilateral sources and World Bank's own resources (Fund for
Innovative Approaches in Human and Social Development-FIAHS). While the criteria for area selection
include the establishment of the LWAs and agreement by the associations to pay for part of the
rehabilitation costs, there are a number of factors that can influence the achievement of this requirement:
*      Farmers' willingness to invest in drainage rehabilitation within a specific site may not be
homogeneous; some farmers may be of pensionable age with a strong self-sufficient
attitude to survival, while others are young and learning-by-doing people who are
exchanging labor and machinery with their neighbors;
*      The level of experience among targeted farmers varies in light of new ownerships and
categories of farmers. This factor influences adaptability to an emerging market system
and ultimately farm restructuring;
*      The lengthy land restitution and privatization process underway has resulted in a mosaic
of interim and recognized rights to land ownership and use, and this condition to some
extent can inhibit long-term investments by farmers;
*      There is a need to tailor a package of assistance to farmers in order to support local
initiatives; and local capacity for designing and delivering this package is essential for the
sustainability of the investment strategy.
20.   Considering the above factors, the MOA will undertake social assessments of households, with
the objective of constructing social maps of the areas preliminarily selected for drainage rehabilitation and
using these maps as inputs for the final area selection in accordance with technical, social, economic and
environmental criteria. The preparation for this social assessment will focus on training local experts in



PIP 2
Page 8 of 18
the methodologv of rapid assessment techniques (both qualitative and quantitative). Once this training
is completed by the end of 1995 or early 1996, the Amelioration Unit will then engage these local experts
to conduct social assessments of the preselected areas in stages over two years.
21.    Technical Standards. Prior to project launch, and during the post-appraisal period, the MOA plans
to receive technical assistance from Finland3 for: (a) development of standards on designs, materials and
work quality for drainage rehabilitation, and environmental aspects arising thereof; and (b) training on
these standards for the technical staff of the Amelioration Unit, Amelioration Bureaux and drainage
construction companies in Estonia. It is anticipated that the output of this assistance will be used
subsequently as inputs for the preparation of standard bidding documents.
Drainage Research
22.    The existing drainage research program would be reoriented to address issues of reduction in
maintenance costs, sustainability of systems, and environmental protection. The research needs are
primarily of an adaptive type to improve subsurface drain life and reduce open drain maintenance costs,
and environmental aspects arising thereof. Results of this research are expected to be available in 1998,
and practical information packages will be prepared for dissemination to LWAs in 1999. The Institute
of Water Management of the Agriculture University (Tartu) will be responsible for conducting this
research. The GOE will provide adequate funding for this research program, estimated at about US
$200,000 (base costs) including the costs of field instruments and equipment (US $50,000) and US
$150,000 for operations and staff time over a period of 5 years.
Land Use Management Studies
23.    Related to the environmental aspects of drained lands and wetlands, the project through its Land
Use Management4 component covers the following:
(a)    Wetlands Management Stratezv Study. The main objective of the strategy would be to
develop a -national strategy for wetlands in Estonia. A key component would be to
recommend a classification and identification system to establish the value of wetlands
in Estonia. This is a complementary strategy exercise, covering wetlands in forest and
agriculture areas: drained lands no longer under cultivation, or to be abandoned to natural
regrowth; peat-mined areas for restoration; and the management of polders no longer
under active operation. This activity would be coordinated with the "Wetstonia" initiative
of the World Wildlife Fund for Nature/Estonian Fund for Nature and the actions to be
supported under the Bank financed Haapsalu and Matsalu Bays Environment Project. The
Wetlands Management Strategy Study would commence on June 1, 1996 and is scheduled
to be completed after one year.
3      Based on Estonian-Finnish cooperation agreement dated May 18, 1995, consisting of 6 person-months of Finnish experts,
travel expenses and logistical support at an estimated cost of FIM 200,000.
4      Detailed cost table presented in Table 5 of Annex 6.



PIP 2
Page 9 of 18
(b)    Environmental Monitoring of Drainage Rehabilitation. MOE is responsible to monitor
the drainage rehabilitation works and to develop appropriate environmental screening
criteria and procedures to ensure safe environmental standards of the works. The project
would support MOE by financing laboratory equipment to analyze and monitor water
samples from the proposed drainage sites, and local consultants to conduct environmental
screening of each of the proposed sites. The laboratory equipment would be installed in
the Estonian Environmental Research Laboratory.
(c)    Environmental Studies for Future Drainage Investments. The project also supports a
sectoral environmental drainage study which will analyze future options for drained lands,
and measures needed to convert drained land from its present use to other uses such as
forest land, parks, wildlife protection areas or wetlands. This study will be commissioned
by the Ministry of Environment, funded by the project and undertaken by consultants.
Three MOE's departments will be involved in the study: Departments of Water (main
responsibility), Nature Conservation, and Environmental Impact Analysis and Normative.
The study would commence on January 1, 1997 and is scheduled to be completed after
two years.
