II. Implementation Arrangements
A. Organizations Responsible for the Component7
25.    Under the supervision and coordination of the Amelioration Unit of the Ministry of Agriculture,
the Amelioration Bureaux will be responsible for the implementation of the physical rehabilitation of the
drainage systems in their own county. As there are 12 ABs throughout the country, three counties will be
covered by ABs located in nearby counties. The drainage rehabilitation works will be spread throughout
the country, and each AB is expected to be responsible for not more than 5 construction works at any
given time. This work will be done through contracts with private contractors and construction
companies. MOA will support a reoriented drainage research program to address concerns related to
system maintenance and sustainability. This research will be undertaken by the Institute of Water
Management of the Agricultural University in Tartu. LWAs will be fully responsible for future
maintenance and rehabilitation of on-farm systems.
26.    Environmental screening of drainage sites will be the responsibility of the County Environmental
Offices and under the overall supervision of the MOE. The overall coordination of MOE's involvement
in this screening process will be under the responsibility of the Water Department. The Amelioration Unit
of the MOA will liaise closely with the MOE so that timing and areas to be covered for environmental
screening fit with the overall implementation phases of the project.
7       The role and functions of an Inter-Ministerial Project Steering Committee (PSC), and its composition, are described in
Annex 5. On day-to-day tasks, this PSC is assisted by two Joint Project Directors, respectively appointed from the Ministry of
Agriculture and the Ministry of Environment.



PIP 2
Page 11 of 18
27.   The Project Implementation Unit (PIU) will assist the Amelioration Unit, the Amelioration
Bureaux, and the departments in MOE, in aspects related to procurement, loan disbursement, accounting,
monitoring and evaluation, as well as administrative support.
28.   GOE will provide a contingency fund to the PIU of about $400,000 equivalent, for the use by the
PIU in drainage site areas, to ensure that prior to commencement of rehabilitation works, all lands to
benefit from these works have been surveyed, registered in the cadastre, and that ownership has been
clarified and registered in the title book.
B. Agreements between Borrower and Implementing Agencies
29.   Agreements related to this component to be reached at negotiations between the Borrower and
Implementing agencies are as follows:
(a)    The MOA will work with the Ministry of Environment on assessments, evaluations, and
implementation required for the drainage rehabilitation and maintenance program to
ensure that only strategic drainage work will be undertaken in accordance with agreed
environmental considerations.
(b)    The Ministry of Environment and the Ministry of Agriculture will have reached agreement
on the environmental screening criteria and procedures for the drainage rehabilitation
component.
(c)    The MOA will undertake rehabilitation works only if all the criteria for area selection
are met, including that the obligations of the LWAs are fulfilled by the LWAs.
(d)    GOE will ensure that for the priority drainage rehabilitation sites the necessary rural
infrastructure upgrading,9 currently planned under the Public Investment Plan in general
terms, will be provided at the locations of the participating LWAs as a complement to
their improved drainage systems.
(e)    The GOE will provide sufficient funding allocations to MOA for the establishment and
promotion of LWAs, including the associated training and studies as determined under the
project. In addition, GOE will also provide funding to the Institute of Water Management
for the planned research program under the project, including provision of adequate
funding for operations and staff time required for the research.
(f)    MOE will commence the Wetlands Management Strategy Study and the Environmental
Study for Future Drainage Investments with the timing as shown in this PIP, or as
otherwise agreed during the implementation.
8     Terms of reference of the PIU are presented in Annex 5. This PIU reports to the Inter-Ministerial Project Steering
Committee.
9     Rural roads, water supplies, teleconmunications, and electricity.



PIP 2
Page 12 of 18
(g)    The Borrower shall use the standard bidding documents for NCB contracts issued by the
ECA Region.
(h)    The Borrower will establish, in the PIU, the contingency fund for land survey and
registration of the lands in each drainage rehabilitation site.
(i)    That the Borrower will prior to commencement of rehabilitation works, dispose of all
State-owned land in the chosen Project drainage sites either by sale or lease.
C. Role of the Bank During Supervision
30.   The drainage component will be supervised by Bank personnel (or designated consultants) in
accordance with the proposed supervision plan summarized in Chapter III (F) of the Staff Appraisal
Report.
m. Implementation Plan
A. Timebound Schedule
31.   The broad implementation plan for the drainage rehabilitation and maintenance component is
shown in Appendix I of this Annex. Timelines shown are for a cycle, from site selection and design to
rehabilitation works, including interactive and parallel development of the Land and Water Associations.
Appendix 1, as an example, shows the timing for Phase II (after a small pilot phase involving 3,500 ha)
16,500 ha. Critical and key steps for Phase III and Phase IV implementation schedules are similar, to
commence a year and two years later respectively from Phase II start-up date.
B. Procurement Arrangements and Schedule
32.     Table 2 lists the major procurement packages and their schedules of this project component.
Table 3 provides the procurement packages of the Land Use Management component. For all items funded
under the loan proceeds, GOE agrees that: (a) it would apply procurement procedures which are
consistent with the Bank's Guidelines for Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits (January,
1995), and Guidelines for Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by the World Bank as
Executing Agency (August, 1981) for procurement of Bank-financed goods and consultant's services, and
(b) that progress reports prepared by Government would include monitoring of the procurement process
of the various aggregate amounts and ceilings. Procurement methods for items funded under the loan
proceeds are as follows:
(a) Civil Works (US $7.9 million). All civil works items would be procured through National
Competitive Bidding (NCB). NCB items for civil works are for drainage rehabilitation and
maintenance. The number of contracts is expected to be about 60. NCB is justifiable because:
(a) Estonia's open competitive economy generates works at prices below the intemational market,



PIP 2
Page 13 of 18
and (b) Estonia's laws do not prevent international contractors from participating in NCB. NCB
is appropriate given that: (a) the average values of works per site are small (less than US
$150,000 and will not exceed $300,000); (b) locations are scattered geographically (and thus not
permitting "packaging" of a number of site contrcts) and catchment areas cover non-contiguous
drainage systems of 100 to 150 hectares in size; (c) implementation requires continuous
consultations with, and participation and contribution from local farmers; (d) ownership of the
drainage rehabilitation is linked with intensive participatory preparation of establishing Land and
Water Associations; and (e) small contracts to local contractors reduce mobilization and
organization costs. Domestic bidders will not receive preferences, and contracts must be awarded
to the lowest evaluated bid in sequence. At negotiations, agreements would be sought that the
Borrower shall use standard bidding documents for NCB contracts issued by the ECA Region

IV. Monitoring and Evaluation"
A. Key Development Indicators12
34.    Key development indicators include the following:
*      Increases in crop and pasture yields per ha to threshold yields specified in economic
analysis
*      Overall increases in agricultural production from drainage rehabilitation area
*      Number of farns with positive income (compare to farm  models in financial analysis)

*      Length of main canals/drains rehabilitated
*      Length of collector drains rehabilitated

ESTONIA
AGRICULTURE PROJECT
PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION PLAN
ADVISORY SERVICES COMPONENT
L. The Component
A. Objectives
1.    The uncertainties pertaining to production, transformation, marketing and land ownership have
created a large demand for agricultural extension advisory services. The objective of agricultural advisory
services in Estonia is to induce voluntary change among rural people by:
(a)   transferring technology to and from farmers for sustainable agricultural production,
processing, and marketing;
(b)    mobilizing and organizing farmers, rural groups and communities; and
(c)    educating farmers, building human resources and enhancing local capacity to generate,
transfer and use information relevant to entrepreneurial and employment opportunities,
technologies, and markets.
B. Component Description
Background
2.    Pre-independence, agricultural technology and advice moved rapidly between the technical staff
of research stations, state and collective farms, and the university. These highly specialized staff
exchanged information regularly, each of them dealing with a narrow field of activity. Because markets
and prices were predetermined and inputs provided at subsidized prices, questions on profitability,
sustainability and competition did not arise.
3.    As large farms have been dismantled, and small family farms as well as joint stock farms have
emerged, the new farmers need advice and skills that respond to the needs of a market economy. Several
extension systems used in European countries are being introduced and tested with bilateral assistance
(Denmark, Germany, Netherlands).
4.    There are about 700 people employed in Estonia who provide farmers with advice, nearly all on
a part-time basis. Agricultural advisors are employed in the following manner:
(a)   the private sector (e.g. veterinarians, advisors from the processing industries, private
consultants, input suppliers);
(b)   the public sector (e.g. staff from universities, research institutes, county staff of MOA);



PIP 3
Page 2 of 18
(c)   the media (mostly specialized print, and to a lesser extent radio and television); and
(d)   the farmers themselves (e.g. Farmers' Union with a staff of about 150, including 60 full-
time advisors in the field, serving the family farms; or the Producers' Union mainly
serving larger joint stock farms.)
Present levels of public support for extension are low, representing about 0.05% of the agricultural GDP
whereas many countries invest about 1 - 2% of the agricultural GDP in extension.
5.     Who are the Estonian farmers. and what do they need? Roughly speaking, there are three types
of farmers operating in Estonia: (i) viable farmers and farm managers; (ii) farms that need improvements
to become viable; and (iii) the farms of questionable viability, including many part-time farms.
Distinctions between the categories are blurred, but each category has different needs, that should be
addressed --and paid for-- differently. The Project supports all three types of farmers.
(a)    The most viable farmers, including about 2,500 family farms and 1,200 joint stock farm
managers need advice on production techniques, business skills including marketing and
organization, and tactical advice, for instance to expand a herd, or switch crops. Given
a proper introduction, many of them would be willing and able to pay for advice that
benefits their individual enterprise (business development plans, soil tests, or loan
application advice). The project foresees strengthening Private Advisory Services by
supporting a Private Advisory Services Development Fund (PSADF) to provide these
farmers with advice of their choosing. The fund has the dual purpose of providing
eligible farmers with temporary and partial funding to buy advice so they get to appreciate
the value of advisory services in increasing their income, while creating competition and
enhancing quality among private agricultural advisors.
(b)    The middle categorv of farms, including about 10,000 family farms and 1,000 joint stock
farms, mainly need technical and economic advice to make their current operation more
profitable. Very often, those needs can be efficiently met through educational programs
on milk quality improvement, general business skills, or pasture management, for instance.
Such advice covers more public domain information and will continue to be carried out
by the publicly funded extension services. The Project foresees strengthening publicly
funded extension through: (i) more efficient delivery of advice; (ii) improved county
inventories of current farming systems, problems and opportunities; and (iii) increased
farner participation in priority setting.
(c)    Marginal and inviable farms. There are also an estimated number of 5,000 family farms
and 1,500 joint stock farms that must leave farning or at least supplement their income
from non-farming activities. These farmers need information on altemative employment,
on legal matters pertaining to farm closure and land rights upon liquidation, and small and
medium enterprise skills. The govemment is supporting rural information centers that aim
at the most dynamic and risk taking segment of the rural population. The Project will
support expansion of selected, rural information centers to include information on
altemative rural employment, legal matters, and other information that specifically aims
to ease the transition out of farming.



PIP 3
Page 3 of 18
6.    Critical success factors. The success of advisory services depends on: (a) quality advice that
responds to the needs of the farmers; (b) early, strong and continued government support, including
sufficient implementation capacity in a national task force on agricultural extension; and (c) effective
public relations to inform farmers and the public at large of what to expect and what not to expect from
extension. Long term success of extension will also depend on improvements in the curriculum of higher
agricultural education in Estonia to ensure that the new private and public advisors have a stronger
economic grounding, more practical and stronger communication skills and a broader general training; and
on improvements in Estonian agricultural research to make it more responsive to farmers that operate in
a competitive market. Farmers contribute to their advisory services. Most importantly, they help set the
agenda, ensuring that the advice is relevant and the advisors held accountable. Second, the farmers are
expected to pay for an increasing share of the PASDF and the operation and maintenance of the rural
information centers.
7.    Implications on govemment spending and farmers' contribution. Proposed support for Estonian
advisory services is important, but limited, both in time and in scope. The Private Advisory Services
Development Fund requires only an initial investment, because the farmers are expected to take over those
programs over time. The Rural Information Centers will be partially self-financing over time, and only
minimal public spending will be needed. The public extension service will remain a government
investment in human resources, resulting in increased income and increased rural welfare. Chances to
sustainably fund the public extension services are high, because of the important reduction in county-level
field staff (from 6-7 per county to about 3-4).
The Private Advisory Services Development Fund (PASDF)
8.    This fund has the dual purpose of creating and developing demand for advisory services among
the most viable farmers who are willing and able to pay for services; and creating competition among
advisors to enhance their responsiveness to clients' needs. The program will be introduced in stages: a
simple, limited pilot phase to start with, and a mainstreamed, more elaborated phase later on. It is
estimated that about 3,000 farmers will need "private" advice, put it to effective use, and be willing and
able to pay for it. It is planned that each eligible farmer would receive a grant from the PASDF of 2,000
EEK in 1996, which would cover a comprehensive business plan and ten to twenty visits a year. The
Government would decrease the level of the annual grant and phase out the grant payment over 8 years.
Assuming that one private advisor would have about 30 clients, his or her gross income from this scheme
would be about 66,000 EEK, including the 200 EEK contribution of each farmer (in 1996). Such income
would allow for a monthly income of 2,500 EEK and an annual operational budget that would include
transport and communications of about 30,000 EEK. Over the 5-year Project period the mission estimates
that the services of about 100 to 170 full-time equivalent advisors would be funded by PASDF.
9.    In the pilot phase (1995-1996), funds from the budget, managed by the MOA, will be transferred
from the MOA to the counties, based on shares of arable land. A county level steering committee will:
(a)   set criteria for fanner eligibility. Many counties will apply one or two simple criteria
for eligibility, like 10 hectares of land, or 15 milking cows, for instance
(b)   inform farmers. Using direct mailing, public meetings and mass media carnpaigns, the
county extension service will make the farmers in the county aware of the program, and
of the exact procedures for them to follow in order to benefit from the program. In



PIP 3
Page 4 of 18
particular, the county office, overseen by the steering committee, will: (i) inform the
public at large of the program; (ii) invite eligible farmers to apply; (iii) register applicants;
(iv) provide the applicants with a draft contract between the farmer(s) and the advisor(s)
(c)     oversee payment of advisors upon satisfactory completion of advisory services, as agreed
in the contract between farmer(s) and advisor(s).
The county-level steering committee would have one representative from the Farmers' Union, one from
the Producers' Union, and one from the county extension service, and when formed, the heads of the
LWAs in that county.

10.    The farmers' responsibilities would be spelled out clearly during the public awareness carnpaign,
and include:
(a)     formulate demand for advice
(b)     apply for PASDF to the county
(c)     choose advisor(s)
(d)     sign contract with advisor, including a mutually agreed plan of action and
payment details
(e)     pay 10 % of advisor's fee in 1996, 15%  in 1997, 25%  in 1998, 25%  in 1999,
30% in 2000, 40% in 2001, and a higher share thereafter.
(d)     sign contract again, for delivery of advisory services
11.    The private advisors' responsibilities would be to:
(a)     advertize his or her availability, skills, particular interests



PIP 3
Page 5 of 18
(b)    sign contract with farner(s) that clearly spells out the type of advice to be given,
and the expected results of adopted advice
(c)    deliver advice as agreed
(d)    present contract, with farmers' signature for completion of advisory services to
county extension office
(e)   receive payment
12.   After the initial pilot phase in 1995-1996, a number of refinements will be made, depending on
experiences gained. They includes:
(a)    an increase in the amount to be distributed, to enable in a one-year period, a
business development plan to be developed, and a minimum of ten to twenty
farmn visits to be made;
(b)    a gradual decrease in public funding, to reach zero in 8 years' time, when farmers
are expected to pay for "private" information themselves (see Table 1);
(c)    a distribution of funds among counties, based on actual demand, rather than
simple hectarage;
(d)    a more elaborate set of eligibility criteria, based on a transparent system of
economic viability of the individual farm, using a simple points system that gives
weighted values for the major farm assets, under the prevailing market conditions;
(e)    a form of registration and quality control of the individual advisors, based on
farmer opinion and industry initiatives: associations of advisors of proven quality
would be provided with incentives that would be channeled through, and
controlled by, the farmers;
(f)   the training needs of the advisors will be assessed upon completion of the pilot
phase. A comprehensive training program among private advisors, possibly
combined with a government certification procedure, will be started on the basis
of skillgap analysis. Both the skillgap analysis and the advisor training will be
supported by the Project;
(g)    a more representative, elected steering committee.
13.   The National Agricultural Extension Task Force has a major role to play in operationalizing the
PASDF. The NAETF, through the staff of the MOA, will:
(a)   train the county extension staff;
(b)    oversee and encourage the development of extension tools, including simple
computer software to assist farmers' cost/benefit analyses;
(c)    assist in the election of the steering committee;
(d)   train the county steering committees, including PASDF procedures, registration
of private advisors, examples of successes of extension, Public Relations and
public awareness campaigning.
Public Extension Improvement
14.   Publicly funded extension is ongoing in Estonia, but needs to be more demand driven, held more
accountable by the farmers, make better use of the potential of existing resources and media, and work
towards clearly defined and monitorable goals. The project aims to support the public extension through:



PIP 3
Page 6 of 18
(a)    simple, practical country extension inventories, providing minimum information who the
farmers are: in terms of farming systems, trends in crop and animal yields, numbers and
types of farmers, gender, part- or full-time, alternative income, age distribution. This
information is presently fragmented or unavailable. The Project would provide training
for staff to draft a simple, 1 -2 page questionnaire, and incremental operational funds for
2 staff in each county (one from the county office, one from the Farmers' Union) to
carry out the inventory during 2 -3 weeks, and update it twice during the life of the
project, during 1 week each. Office supplies have also foreseen, to present the findings,
including simple maps of farming systems, in an attractive manner.
(b)   the use of rapid, participatory assessment (PRA) techniques to build consensus among
farmers, researchers and extension staff on priorities for extension programs. The Project
would provide training of staff in PRA techniques, incremental operation funds to carry
out one assessment in each of the 15 counties by 6 persons (representing county office,
Farmers' Union, Producers' Union, research, agri-business) during 3-4 weeks, to be
repeated once after two years. In addition, the Project foresees 2 staff months of Technical
Assistance to train selected staff in the use of PRA techniques, optimizing present
techniques to build consensus among all major stakeholders. The expected output of the
assessments would be a plan of action for the advisory services. Fanner needs would not
be limited to production techniques, but also include needs for business skills, tactical
advice and organizational aspects of farmer associations or commodity groups.
(c)    more coordinated production of extension materials, reducing present overlapping
initiatives, while improving the quality and uptake by farmers. The project would support
development and production of extension materials, training of staff and farmers, the
development of extension tools, including simple farm management software packages.
In addition, the MOA in the context of the Project would support incremental operational
funding.
(d)    more efficient diffusion, through the training of extension advisors, and the use of the
electronic and printed media. The Project foresees support for printing, diffusion on radio
and television of relevant programs, training of trainers, study tours, and incremental
operational funding.
(e)    more effective impact monitoring, through the use of agreed upon performance
indicators. The Project would support training and incremental operational funds to
collect, analyze, present and put to practical use, the findings, based on the performance
indicators. For a comprehensive list of indicators, see Section IV of this project
implementation plan.
The Rural Information Centers
15.   The government is supporting rural business centers that aim at the most dynamic and risk taking
segment of the rural population. The Government aims to expand the scope of these business centers to
include information on alternative employment and other legal and economic advice, that specifically aims
to ease the transition out of fanning. The concept for the centers was inspired by the Scandinavian



PIP 3
Page 7 of 18
telecottage model. The first telecottage opened in 1985 in Sweden. The following basic services are
provided:
(a)    information services: access to regional, national and international databases;
(b)    data-processing services: access to computers with a selection of programs;
(c)    IT consultancies: the telecottage management is undertaken by an "IT caretaker", who
also assists local businesses and organizations;
(d)    distance working: providing workstations for distance working;
(e)    education: open university type and online tutorials;
(f)    telecommunications: fax, e-mail, etc.;
(g)    village hall facilities: rooms and facilities for meetings, information.
The project would support modest funding to provide equipment and support the development of training
material. This initiative would require complementary technical assistance to assist in organizing these
centers as joint-stock companies, initially with the local municipality as the major shareholder and
progressively increasing the share of private ownership in subsequent years. The EU Phare program has
been planned to include this type of technical assistance for a duration of at least a year. Also, GOE
foresees expansion of the present advisory services with information on alternative rural employment, legal
matters, and other information that specifically aims to ease the transition out of farming.
Support to the National Agricultural Extension Task Force (NAETF)
16.    The NAETF plays a crucial role in the development of the Estonian advisory services. In
particular, the NAETF will:
(a)    coordinate and facilitate the development of the private advisory services through training
of staff, setting of criteria of farmer eligibility, preparing model and standardized
contracts, for instance;
(b)    contract selected extension products, like brochures, training programs, media productions,
through assistance in writing technical specifications, selection, negotiations, setting
indicators for performance;
(c)    inform policy makers on effects of policies at farm level, and of farmer appreciation of
public extension services;
(d)    facilitate linkages between extension and research; and
(e)    promote extension through improved public relations.
17.    Under the project, the NAETF's capacity to contract specific extension tasks, including writing
TORs, negotiating contracts and monitoring performance, will be strengthened through vehicles
redeployment, equipment, training, and technical assistance. The NAETF would benefit from substantial
assistance from an EU (Phare) supported program for at least one year. Currently, the NAETF receives
technical assistance (EU (Phare)) to develop the operation of the Private Advisory Services Development
Fund. This includes the preparation of pre-qualification criteria for agricultural advisors and the
preparation of standardized contracts for advisors and farmers. The County Extension officers will be



PIP 3
Page 8 of 18
trained in management and operation of the payments to advisors, and evaluation of advisory services.

19.    PASDF will reimburse farmers for each annual agricultural advisory contract up to a limit of EEK

Government will contribute the remaining 20%. Farners will pay at least 10% of the total contract value
in 1996 (estimated at EEK 2200) and over time, as the farmer's contribution to the advisory costs
increases, the proportion of grants paid per contract decreases.

II. Implementation Arrangements
A. Organizations Responsible for the Component
20.   MOA has decided not to do extension, but to get it done; and it should not favor any one advisory
service, but provide a level, competitive platform. The MOA has created a NAETF to manage the
development of advisory services and to redefine the Ministry's own role in extension. The NAETF is
responsible to an advisory council, with a maioritY representation of farmers, and in which research, the
University and colleges, the processors, input suppliers, commercial banks and the media are represented.
The advisory council would meet about twice a year and sets policy guidelines for the NAETF. This
NAETF will be expanded under the Project by relocating underutilized county staff, to become a group
of extension specialists, temporarily housed in the MOA, pending the privatization of the NAETF. Such
privatization of the NAETF would: (a) stimulate more farner participation, commitment and ownership
in design, implementation and monitoring of extension; (b) better integrate research, education and
extension; and (c) develop funding mechanisms for extension from alternative sources, including levies
on produce, farmer subscriptions and fees, and land taxes to cover part of the public extension
expenditures.
21.   MOA, through the NAETF, will establish and supervise the PASDF and appoint a manager. The
NAETF would prepare and finalize the operating policies of PASDF, prepare a standardized contract
format for contracts between advisors and farmers, and the selection criteria for the agricultural advisors.
For these purposes, MOA is receiving Technical Assistance from EU (Phare).
22.   The Project Implementation Unit (PIU)2 will assist the NAETF in implementation aspects related
to procurement, loan disbursement, accounting, monitoring and evaluation, as well as administrative
support.
B. Agreements between Borrower and Implementing Agencies
23.   Agreements relevant to this component to be reached at negotiations between the Borrower and
the implementing agencies under the MOA are as follows:
(a)    The GOE will provide sufficient funding to the MOA, including its NAETF, to cover
incremental operating costs associated with this project component. These costs cover
expenses for field trips, meetings and planning workshops, accommodation and per diems,
administration, logistics and other operational requirements to support the implementation
of the advisory services project component.
(b)    That by effectiveness, MOA will have appointed all the staff and members of the NAETF
to ensure adequate participation and consultation and establish county-level steering
committees, all with TORs, and to the satisfaction of the Bank.
(c)    As a condition of disbursement, the Borrower will present to the Bank a standardized
contract format for agricultural advisory services, and the selection criteria for
2     Terms of reference of the PIU are-presented in Annex 5. This PIU reports to the Inter-Ministerial Project Steering
Committee.



PIP 3
Page 10 of 18
agricultural advisors to be eligible for PASDF, both satisfactory to the Bank.
(d)    By March 30, 1996, MOA would establish the PASDF and appoint the manager of
PASDF under terms and conditions satisfactory to the Bank.
C. Role of the Bank During Supervision
24.   The advisory services component will be supervised by Bank personnel (or designated consultants)
in accordance with the proposed supervision plan summarized in Section III (F) of the Staff Appraisal
Report.
m. Implementation Plan
A. Timebound Schedules
25.   Key timing for major and key activities of this component are as indicated in the procurement
plan, as shown in Table 3. The NAETF, once fully in place, will prepare a detailed operational plan
annually, indicating detailed steps and processes involved, including the various consultations required for
coordinating and implementing the overall activities.
B. Procurement Arrangements and Schedule
26.   For all items funded through the loan proceeds, GOE agrees that: (a) it would apply procurement
procedures which are consistent with the Bank's Guidelines for Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA
Credits (January, 1995), and Guidelines for Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by the
World Bank as Executing Agency (August, 1981) for procurement of Bank-financed goods and consultant's
services. Procurement of technical assistance, equipment, and consultants for various studies under parallel
co-financing arrangements would be carried out through tied procurement in accordance with procurement
regulations of the respective donor countries and the European Union. Table 2 summarizes the major
procurement packages of this component and their schedules. Procurement for Bank-financed activities
would be undertaken in the manner as described below:
(a)    Equipment-including materials (US $0.17 million). Procedures for International
Shopping (IS) and National Shopping (?NS) would be used for procurement of contracts
valued below US $300,000 and US $50,000 respectively. For IS, at least three quotations
would have to be obtained from two countries. NS procedures would be used for
procurement of office equipment and furniture. Contracts awarded under NS procedures
would be based on comparing price quotations from at least three suppliers.
(b)    Training (US $0.04 million). Consultants would be contracted to deliver training in
Participatory Rural Appraisal techniques. Hiring of these consultants would be in
accordance with the Bank's Guidelines for Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers
and by the World Bank as Executing Agency (August, 1981).
(c)    Studies (US $0.32 million). The Project would finance studies for agricultural advisory
services. Contracts are estimated to be small in size, for both extension material
development and distribution, and studies related to skill gap analysis.



PIP 3
Page 11 of 18
(d)     Agricultural Advisory Services (US $3.3 million).3  The Bank would partly finance the
employment of consultants (local and possibly some foreign experts) in connection with
the provision of private advisory services for priority programs on agricultural extension.
The beneficiaries of the agricultural advisory services would directly procure their own
private advisors using a standardized contract acceptable to the Bank. It is estimated that
about 2,800 annual advisory contracts would be submitted for reimbursement.

IV. Monitoring and Evaluation
27.   A hierarchy of extension indicators will be used under the Project:
Input indicators
*     Inputs: Materials bought, equipment installed
Output indicators
Activities: Number of visits, meetings, demonstrations
Involvement: Farmers in contact with extension
Number of contracts financed through PASDF
Impact indicators
Opinion: Farmers giving opinion on extension's usefulness
IKnowledge Change: Changes in knowledge, attitudes and skills
Practice Change: Farmers adopting improved agricultural practices
Results: Changes in the standard of living of farmers
System Maintenance: Sustainability of advisory services
28.   The specific indicators will be developed by the NAETF with the assistance of the PIU's M&E
specialist. The NAETF would be responsible for the collection and analysis of M&E data.
Prerequisites
29.   To make indicators work, by making continuous and meaningful adjustments in content and
method of the advisory services, three prerequisites must be considered. First, the different categories
of farmers, with their farming systems, their constraints and their opportunities, must be known and
understood well. This requires the detailed assessment, described above. Second, on the basis of this
needs assessment, objectives need to be formulated in a participatory manner. Objectives should be
formulated in terms of changed behavior of different target groups, by the different target groups. Third,
extension managers must have the necessary technical and methodological knowledge and skills to make
it work.



PIP 3
Page 13 of 18
PROJECT INPUTS

ESTONIA
AGRICULTURE PROJECT
PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION PLAN
FOOD QUALITY CONTROL AND VETERINARY LABORATORY COMPONENT
1. The Component
A. Objectives
1.    The objective of the project component is to improve food quality and livestock quality standards
to protect consumers and increase exports of food and livestock products. The project would support: (a)
improvement of the National Veterinary Control Laboratory to control the quality livestock products and
foodstuffs of animal origin for domestic and export markets; and (b) training for the operations of the food
quality and veterinary control facility. The project would therefore provide Bank financing for the
purchase of modem laboratory equipment and instruments and the necessary training for the State
Veterinary Department in connection with the acquisition of this equipment, with specific objectives to:
(a)   set up a national program for the monitoring of contaminants and harmful residues in
animal products and foodstuffs of animal origin;
(b)   improve veterinary control of infectious diseases, and ultimately the health safety features
of foodstuffs of animal origin, in order to penetrate export markets and protect the health
of foreign and domestic consumers.
2.    This component are complemented by donor-funded parallel projects (partly ongoing) which
provide technical assistance and training programs for developing food legislation, and institutional
strengthening for the establishment and operations of modem food quality control facilities.
B. Component Description
Background
3.    Currently, GOE plans to ensure rapid establishment of internationally accepted food quality control
and standards in Estonia to meet both intemational and EU standards. At present, existing facilities are
inadequately equipped. The State Veterinary Department depends on foreign laboratories for certain tests
on residues and contamination. Since these tests must be taken at regular and frequent intervals, and
results made available quickly for regular monitoring, the present situation is a constraint to the
development of the local meat and food industry. Although Estonia has received export quotas and
preferential tariff reductions on a number of food and animal products, because of the lack of
internationally required food testing capability, export markets to EU countries remain inaccessible.
4.    Legal Aspects. The State Veterinary Department is an independent unit under the administration
of the Ministry of Agriculture. While the organization of the veterinary service and veterinary control is



PIP 4
Page 2 of 8
prescribed in the Law on Veterinary Service, the Food Law specifies that the control of the quality of
foodstuffs is conducted by several agencies including the National Grain Board, the State Veterinary
Department, the National Health Board, the National Board on Standards, and National Customs. In
accordance with the present rules prescribed under the Law on Veterinary Service, veterinary control
covers aspects related to the quality of meat, meat products, milk, milk products, eggs, fish, honey and
bee products, raw hides, wool and feather. Given the importance of the livestock sub-sector, this mandate
represents a critical support to the overall development of the sub-sector.
5.    Organization and Functions. The existing network of veterinary control consists of 15 County
Laboratories and the National Veterinary Control Laboratory. County Laboratories are mandated to carry
out basic biochemical, microbiological and serological tests to diagnose diseases, primarily of livestock,
fowl and fish. These laboratories also establish the quality of animal products and foodstuffs of animal
origin by means of chemical and microbiological analyses. Furthermore, the Veterinary Control
Laboratory is mandated with the following main functions:
(a)   full diagnostic examinations of animal, poultry, fish and bee diseases;
(b)    chemical, physical, microbiological, organoleptic and toxicological analyses of animal
products and foodstuffs of animal origin, feed and feed components, including evaluation
of their quality on the basis of these analyses;
(c)    methodological guidance on diagnostics and procurement of relevant diagnostics supplies
for County Laboratories;
(d)    examination and analysis of epizootological situation.
6.    Present Constraints.  To date, the present quality of animal products and foodstuffs of animal
origin is assessed to a large extent on the methods prescribed under the GOST system of the former Soviet
Union. Chemical and biological quality standards have been introduced in recent years in Estonia for
milk, milk products and meat products. However, in a transition economy and as Estonia pursues an open
market system, present standards need to be aligned with internationally accepted standards, including
methods for establishing and monitoring compliance of these standards. For example, in the context of
the trade agreement between Estonia and the European Union, EU standards require that for Estonian
exports of animal products and foodstuffs into the EU, the content of various contaminants and potentially
harmful residues must be ascertained. In addition, a program for monitoring and evaluation of these
contaminants must be established and institutionalized in Estonia. Present facilities and their personnel
are not equipped and trained to meet these international requirements. A number of guidelines will need
to be established, especially those concerning: (a) the use of veterinary medicines; (b) instructions for
testing animal products and foodstuffs of animal origin; (c) a system for sampling and transportation of
samples; (d) a list of contaminants, their descriptions and methods for establishing their occurrence; and
(e) instructions for dealing with cases where established limits for contaminants are exceeded.
7.    Inaccessible Markets. As a result of the present inadequacy of the State Veterinary Department
to meet the food quality testing requirements of the EU, potential export markets for certain products
covered under the trade agreement cannot be realized. Although, the food quality of certain products
would meet the EU standards, Estonia lacks the means of verifying this claim to serve as legal proof of



PIP 4
Page 3 of 8
evidence. Potential market opportunities include 60% tariff reductions on agricultural products shown in
Table 1.
Table 1
Trade Agreement between Estonia and The European Union
Food Items with 60% Reduction on Tariffs
(metric tons)
ITEMS                                  1995       1996       1997      TOTAL
Bacon                                    800       900       1,000     2,700
Chicken meat                             400       450        500       1,350
Skimmed milk powder                    1,000      1,250      1,500     3,750
Butter                                   700       750        300       1,750
Cheese                                   700       750        300       1,750
Sausages and similar products            400       450        500       1,350
Food Quality and Veterinary Laboratory Equipment
8.    The project provides funding for the acquisition of modern laboratory equipment for the
improvement and development of the National Veterinary Control Laboratory, to support the main
functions of this laboratory, particularly in the area of testing and control of contaminants and residues,
as well as the diagnosis of diseases. This acquisition would be packaged to include major items as shown
indicatively below:
(i)    Atomic absorption spectrometer
(ii)   Gas chromatograph
(iii)   Microwave for sample preparation
(iv)   Somatic sample counters
(v)    Colony counters
(vi)   Multi-purpose centrifuge
(vii)  Analytical balances
(viii)  Cryoscope
(ix)   Automatic Viscubator
(x)   Incubators
(xi)   Sterilization equipment
(xii)  Water filters



PIP 4
Page 4 of 8
(xiii) Microscopes
(xiv)  Computers and software
(xv)   UV detector
(xvi)  and many more small equipment.
9.    A complete list of equipment will be compiled by the end of 1995, and their specifications
prepared. Although an indicative list of equipment has been prepared, this list will require some updating
to fit with actual requirements, taking into consideration the inventory of availability and condition of
some of this equipment.
Training
10.   In the context of the planned acquisition of new and modem equipment, the project will provide
funding for training the staff, technicians and scientists of the National Veterinary Control Laboratory for
the operations and maintenance of the equipment. Training on modem methods for detecting and
measuring contaminants and residues are planned for about 6 persons for a duration of up to 3 months
each. It is anticipated that the overall training program will be spread out over a year, and scheduled
appropriately in line with the schedule of deliveries of the new equipment.
Technical Assistance
11.   Parallel projects already committed and funded separately by bilateral sources include: (a)
assistance from Denmark (Danish Ministry of Agriculture) for developing the legislative framework for
the quality of animal products; and (b) assistance from Finland for the development of monitoring and
evaluation procedures related to food quality control and testing.

II. Implementation Arrangements
A. Organizations Responsible for the Component
13.    The agency responsible for the overall implementation of this project component is the State
Veterinary Department. However, day-to-day operational and technical aspects are delegated to the
National Veterinary Control Laboratory which will receive all equipment financed under the project.
14.    The Project Implementation Unit (PIU)2 will assist in aspects related to procurement, loan
disbursement, accounting, monitoring and evaluation, as well as administrative support.
Detailed cost presented in Table 7 of Annex 6. Disbursement aspects are described in Section 11 (G) of the Staff
Appraisal Report.
2   Terms of reference of the PII are presented in Annex 5. This PIU reports to the Inter-Ministerial Project Steering
Committee.



PIP 4
Page 6 of 8
B. Agreements between Borrower and Implementing Agencies
15.   Agreements to be reached at negotiations between the Borrower and the implementing agencies
are as follows:
(a)    The GOE will provide sufficient funding to the State Veterinary Department and the
National Veterinary Laboratory to cover incremental operating costs associated with this
project component. These costs include expenses for administration, operations and
maintenance, and adequate staffing.
C. Role of the Bank During Supervision
16.   The food quality control and veterinary laboratory component will be supervised by Bank
personnel (or designated consultants) in accordance with the proposed supervision plan summarized in
Section III (F) of the Staff Appraisal Report.
III. Implementation Plan
A. Timebound Schedules
17.   This project component, consisting of equipment acquisition and related training, is intended to
be completed under the loan within a period of one year, and no later than 18 months from the project
start-up date. Schedules of events under parallel donor activities are determined separately by individual
donors. Basically, timelines to be monitored, are those relating to procurement packages described below,
with timing shown in Table 2.
B. Procurement Arrangements and Schedule
18.   For all items funded through the loan proceeds, GOE agrees that: (a) it would apply procurement
procedures which are consistent with the Bank's Guidelines for Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA
Credits (January, 1995), and Guidelines for Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by the
World Bank as ExecutingAgency (August, 1981) for procurement of Bank-financed goods and consultant's
services, and (b) that progress reports prepared by Government would include monitoring of the
procurement process of the various aggregate amounts and ceilings. Procurement of technical assistance,
equipment, and consultants for various studies under parallel co-financing arrangements would be carried
out through tied procurement in accordance with procurement regulations of the respective donor
countries. Table 2 summarizes the major procurement packages of this component and their schedules.
Procurement for Bank-financed activities would be undertaken in the manner as described below:
(a)   Equipment (US $1.1 million). Laboratory equipment would be procured in two or three
packages in accordance with International Competitive Bidding (ICB) procedures of the
World Bank. When appropriate, the entire equipment would be grouped into a package
to save administrative costs and gain benefits from economy of scale. The Borrower



PIP 4
Page 7 of 8
would use Bank's Standard Bidding Documents as applicable. The application of domestic
preferences would be in accordance with the requirements under Appendix 2 of the
Bank's Guidelines. for Procurement under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits (January 1995),
and expressed clearly in the bidding documents for goods.
(b)     Training (US $0.2 million). Consultants would be contracted to carry out the training
program as identified under the project. Hiring of consultants would be in accordance with
the Bank's Guidelines for Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by the World
Bank as Executing Agency (August, 1981).

IV. Monitoring and Evaluation
A. Key Development Indicators
19.   Key development indicators include the following:
Food quality and diagnostic tests conducted in accordance with international standards
Monitoring is in place, and reports on contaminants and residues regularly compiled and
sent to appropriate authorities including those of the European Union
B. Key Progress Indicators
20.   Physical:
*     Equipment procured according to planned schedules
Equipment delivered, and installed
Staff, technicians and scientists are trained timely
Equipment utilization once installed and personnel trained
21.   Institutional:
*     Operations and maintenance procedures in place
-     Monitoring program for livestock diseases in operation
-     Annual reports on food and livestock quality accepted by EU
22.   Economic Imgact:
Export trends under EU trade agreement for food items with 60% reduction in tariffs.
